“Rupert of Hentzau.”
Sequel to
“THE PRISONER OF ZENDA."
ANTHONY HOPE’S GREAT NOVEL.
THE BOOK OF THE YEAS,
Paper Covers, 35.; Cloth, 4s. Post Free.
“ The Prisoner of Zenda ” has been the success of many
seasons now ; and still it sells. Critics and public were
alike charmed by its free air of adventure, its dashing
fights and tender loves, its fine flavor of romance glow-
ingly, enchantingly pictured. “Rupert of Hentzau”
continues and perpetuates “The Prisoner of Zenda’s ”
charm. It has the same thrill, the same adventurous
atmosphere ; it is a hook to read breathlessly, without a
stop, if ever there was one. Ai dC. D. Gibson’s pictures
emphasise the flying incidents with his own unequalled
art.
W. W. PEARSON, Bookseller, 5 Macquarie Place, Sydney.

THE BED PAGE.
“What is Art?” asks serious Tolstoy, and
answers himself in a 237-page volume where Aus-
tralia isn’t mentioned once. Naturally, there-
fore, the volume falls fruitless on the threshold of
thesubject, and readers must find their main con-
tentment in its answer to the question “ What is
Tolstoy ? ” Tolstoy is a Russian writer of fiction
with a big brain which empties itself all at once
and all o’er, with a mighty uproar—in the way
that the water conies down at Lodore. Clearly,
Art isn’t Tolstoy. Art is a thing of calculated
effort and restraint, of calculated light and
shadow, of calculated basis and climax. Tolstoy
never calculates, never concentrates, never hesi-
tates. He labours and brings forth—a mouse? a
mountain ? an amorphous mass ? Tolstoy has not
the least idea. Whatever it bo, it. was in him, it
is out of him—and the congregation will join in
singing the praises of Cod, and Faith, and the
Remarkable Russian Peasant. Quaint fellow,
Tolstoy.
'*• * *
His book on Art pours itself out just as his
other books have poured themselves out. Good
sense, bad sense ; logic, no logic ; the sanest ideas
mixed with the queerest delusions; forth they
come tumbling in jolly comradeship, without the
least air of knowing what strange bedfellows they
are. But Tolstoy has his suspicions. “ I began
to write this book fifteen years ago, ” he says, but
“it proved that my views on the matter were so
far from dear that I could not arrange them in a
way that satisfied me.” And he “never ceised to
think on the subject ” ; and “ recommenced writ-
ing six or seven times” and si.-i or seven times
failed to satisfy himself ; until, one thinks, be
must have awoke to the fact that if he didn’t pub-
lish something he would presently die with all bis
art-music in him. So he lias publishe 1 the 2 57-
page volume . . . an 1 remains, alas ! so far from
clear, so far from clear,
* * * *
In the beginning Tolstoy discusses the time and
labor spent on “ what is called art,” and asks (1)
is that really art, and (2) if it is, is it worth the
bother of producing it ? That seems an excellent
position for discussion, and all Tolstoy’s positions
are similarly excellent for discussion. The nuis-
ance of him is that he is a thoroughly unscientific
discusser. He generalises from a single instance,
and as often as not he doesn’t state his instance
accurately or justly. For example,
The ballet, in which half-naked women make voluptu-
ous movements, twisting themselves into various sensual
wreathings, is simply a lewd performance.
The comprehensive objection to this is that
“the ballet” (1) is not necessarily performed by
women, (2) who are not necessarily half-naked, (3)
and who do not necessarily make voluptuous move-
ments, (4) or twist themselves, (5) or twist them-
selves into wreathings, (6) or twist themselves into
various sensual wreathings (7) and therefore “the
ballet” is not necessarily “a lewd performance.”
There are very good ballets in which some of the
performers are men ; ballets in which the women
are clothed ; ballets in which the effect is attained
without twisting and without sensual -wreathings.
What Tolstoy should have said is that,
A ballet in which half-naked women make voluptuous
movements, twisting themselves into various sensual
wreathings, is simply a lewd performance.
And even then his statement is debatable. Writes
Pierre Louys—
Aucun spectacle de la nature, ni les flannnes occiden-
tales, ni la temp Ste dans les palmiers, ni la foudre, ni le
mirage, ni les grands soulfevements des eaux ne semblent
dignes d’etonnement a ceux qui ont vu dans leurs bras la
transfiguration de la femme.
This “ transfiguration de la femme ”is simply
Tolstoy’s “half-naked women make their vol-
uptuous movements, twisting themselves into
various sensual wreathings.” But it is not there-
fore a lewd performance.
• » * *
This failure to reason logically vitiates the whole
of Tolstoy’s argument. He does not, like Owen,
re-build the mammoth from a bone; but from the
bone of a mammoth he builds a plesiosaurus or a
pterodactyl—an animal to which the bone never
dreamt of belonging. And some of his facts are
no more trustworthy than some of his opinions.
Referring to the widening of the meaning of “beau-
tiful” to include “good,” he says it is quite natural
in English to say “a beautiful deed” when we
mean ‘‘ a good deed. ” But it is not: the expression
“ a good deed ” has a distinct signification which
is not properly or adequately given by “a
beautiful deed.” And when Tolstoy goes further,
and says that the English language (with others)
“no longer has a suitable word wherewith to
express beauty of form, and has to use a combina-
tion of words such as ‘beautiful to look at’to
convey that idea,” he is clearly misinformed.
Everybody says “a beautiful woman”; nobody
thinks of saying “ a beautiful-to-look-at woman.”
* * * •
So that one has to watch Tolstoy as he comes,
and accept him or reject him sentence by sentence.
Then he is able to do you good. Quite rightly he
rejects the theory that “beauty” is essential to
“ art.” It is true that such a notion hardly needs
slaying, since the conception of “beauty” is
obviously arbitrary—varying from age to age, and
race to race, and individual to individual. In
essence, “beauty” is the result of a physical
harmony with exterior impressions, the harmony
varying in kind and degree. But art may take
root in a physical discord with exterior im-
pressions. There are pictures to shudder at,
stories which nauseate you, yet in causing the
shudder or the nausea they have given proof of
their artistic power. Still, Tolstoy’s data are inte-
resting. He quotes Schelliug as saving that “ the
chief characteristic of works of art is unconscious
infinity’’—-which (barring the “chief” and the
“unconscious”) is precisely the local dogma ap-
plied iu a recent notice of Joseph Conrad’s stories —
The all-human reference, the accurate judgment , place-
ment, of every detail in due proportion and relation to
the widest conceivable synthesis is the secret of imper-
ishable Art, which sees never a flower without the tree,
never a tree without the landscape, never a landscape of
the eye without the illimitable landscape of the creative
imagination.
An English reviewer had the same idea when he
called Conrad’s “ Nigger of the Narcissus ” “epi-
sodic ” —an epithet which aioused Stephen Crane’s
indignation, and partly (it is likely) for the reason
that his own work is “episodic.” His “Pictures
of War ” deal with special incidents, limited fields,
particular individuals. And this is the chief fault
to find with Albert Dorrington’s (“ Alba Dorio.n ”)
stories in The Bulletin —stories technically more
brilliant than Kipling’s ; the most brilliant penned
in Australia. Kipling’s prose, unlike his verse, is
usually subjective in character: he deals less with
figures and landscapes than with ideas and emo-
tions, or uses figures and landscapes chiefly for
conveying ideas and emotions (probably “uncon-
sciously,” as Schelliug has it, because he is built
that wajr). But in Crane’s and Dorrington’s work
(and it may be partly on account of the very
brilliance of the work) the characters seem more
sharply defined, are more detached from humanity,
have more sensibly a frame around the picture.
Maupassant is like Kipling: he doesn’t obtrude
the frame; and the result is you are more in
harmony with _ his work: the “unconscious-
infinity” tendrils bind writer and reader in
a common human sympathy. Personally, I read
Kipling with the greater pleasure, Crane with
the greater admiration ; I think Dorrington by
far the better writer, yet nothing he has written
touches me quite so intimately as Lawson’s “ The
Drover’s Wife.”
* • • *
And, returning to Tolstoy, we hit upon Solger
and Hegel—two other fellows whose merit was in
having Red Page ideas first. Said Solger—
The idea of beauty is the fundamental idea of every-
thing. In the world we see only distortions of the funda-
mental idea, but art, by imagination, may lift itself to
the height of this idea. Art is therefore akin to creation.
If he had said that “Art is Nature raised to the
manth power” he would hardly have expressed
himself more judiciously, as “Compton” (who
now represents in N.S.W. Parliament the consti-
tuency of Art Criticism, having triumphantly
defeated an unsesthetic opponent at the recent
election) will perceive. And when Hegel re-
marked that “Beauty is the shining of the Idea
through matter” he merely stumbled upon the
glorious local truth that “to see is human; to
think is divine.”
• • • •
And again Vischer (1807-87) pointed out that
the highest form of the Idea is personality, and
therefore the highest art is that which has for its
subject-matter the highest personality. And
Herbart (1776-1841) laid just stress upon the sub-
jectivity of the idea of beauty.
There is not, and cannot be, any such thing as beauty
existing in itself. What does exist is only our opinion,
and it is necessary to find the base of this opinion. Such
bases are connected with our impressions. There are
certain relations which we term beautiful, and art con-
sists in finding these relations, which are simultaneous in
painting, the plastic art, and architecture, successive and
simultaneous in music, and purely successive in poetry.
In contradiction to former writers on aesthetics, Herbart
holds that objects are often beautiful which express
nothing at all, as, for instance, the rainbow, which is
beautiful for its lines and colours, and not for its mytho-
logical connection with Iris or Noah’s rainbow.
(Which is an enlightening passage; though the
plain beauty of the rainbow au naturel is not, to the
writer’s mind, as potent as the beauty of the rain-
bow trimmed with mythology, garnished with
memory, sauced with association). Then we come
to Bergmann (1840), who repeats that bea.uty is
only perceived subjectively, and therefore the
problem of aesthetics is to define what pleases
whom. According to Taine (1828-’93), beauty
is the manifestation of the essential characteristic
of any important idea more completely than it is
expressed in reality—once more Nature plus Man.
And Guyau (1854-88) stresses again the univer-
sality-idea—“art lifts man from his personal life
into the universal life, by means, not only of par-
ticipation in the same ideas and beliefs, but also
by means of similarity of feeling. ” And the most
scientific definition of all comes from Veron, whose
book on aesthetics I myself prize especially—
Art is the manifestation of emotion transmitted ex-
ternally by a combination of lines, forms, colours, or by a
succession of movements, sounds, or words subjected to
certain rhythms.
Which, in the local opinion, is as near as anyone
will ever get to defining art. No definition which
has reference to “beauty” is worth a rap, unless
the conception of “beauty” is widened.
* * •*
Tolstoy calmly states, after quoting Veron, that
in spite of the mountains of books written about art,
no exact definition of art has been constructed. And the
reason of this is that the conception of art has been based
on the conception of beauty.
Then he coolly seizes Veron’s definition lock,
stock, and barrel, phrases it less neatly and less
scientifically, and gives it as his own answer to
the question “What is Art?”
Tolstoy’s Definition op Art (After Veron).
To evoke in oneself a feeling one has once experienced,
and having .-voked' it >ll -seif, then, by means of move-
ments, lines, colors, sounds, or forms expressed in words,
so to transmit that feeling that oihers may experience
the same feeling this is the activity of art.
Art is a human activity, consisting in this, that one
man consciously, by means of certain external signs,
hands on to others feelings he has lived through; and that
other people are infected by these feelings, and also ex-
perience them.
And Tolstoy adds a corollary which shows the
teleological twist in his brain —“Artis ... a
means of union among men”—which by-and-by
leads to the discovery that the function of Art is
to promote Tolstoyism, and to inculcate faith in
God and the Remarkable Russian Peasant.
But obviously the essence of Tolstoy’s definition
is the essence of Veron’s—“Artis the transmission
of emotion.” That is the kernel and kingbolt.
You can find emotions so undignified that they
appear unworthy the name of Art; but if there is
emotion, and if it is transmitted, you have rudi-
mentary art, and tree and sapling spring, from the
same seed. It is a nice point whether .Art has
actually to be transmitted before it becomes Art—
does a sun cease to shine because its light does not
reach us ? Tolstoy says where art does not act on
others it is not art; but surely Mozart’s requiem
was Art when only the composer’s ears had heard
it ? And Tolstoy, on his own showing, misrepre-
sents Veron, whose definition insists on the trans-
mission just as much as Tolstoy’s. But T. says
justly—
We are accustomed to understand art to be only what
we hear and see in theatres, concerts, and exhibitions ;
together with buildings, statues, poems, novels . . .
But all this is but the smallest part of the art by which
we communicate with each other in life. All human life
is filled with works of art of every kind—from cradle-
song, Jest, mimicry, the ornamentation of horses, dress
and utensils, up to church services, buildings, monu-
ments, and triumphal processions. It is all artistic
activity. So that by art, in the limited sense of the word,
we do not mean all human activity transmitting feelings,
but only that part which we for some reason select from
it and to which we attach special importance.
This is well put—as Veron well put it before.
• • * *
Then Tolstoy, having established his standard
of art, brings “what is commonly called art ” to
his standard: and the idols fly all around the
market place ! When he comes to ‘ ‘ decadent ”
art lie passes in review some of the French sym-
bolist-poets—much as Nordau does in his “De-
generation”; but in “Degeneration” Nordau
reckoned that Tolstoy and the symbolists were row-
ing in different places on the same rotten boat. Now
Nordau’s Satan is reproving Nordau’s Sin —not, it
must be owned, with the best poetical taste. To
the value of atmosphere and suggestion Tolstoy
seems blind : he wants everything said right out
with a loud voice at mid-day. And he criticises
Wagner as The Bulletin criticises local melo-
drama. He went to a performance of part of “The
Nibelung’s Ring ” at Moscow—
When I arrived, an actor sat on the stage amid decora-
tions intended to represent a cave, and before something
which was meant to represent a stnii.a’s forge. He was
dressed in trico-tights, with a cloak of skins, wore a wig
and an artificial beard, and with white, weak, genteel
hands (his easy movements, and especially the shape of
his stomach and his lack of muscle revealed the actor)
beat an impossible sword with an unnatural hammer in a
way in which no one ever uses a hammer; and at the same
time, opening his mouth in a strange way, lie sang some-
thing incomprehensible. The music of various instru-
ments accompanied the strange sounds which he emitted.
From the libretto one was able to gather that the actor
had to represent a powerful gnome, who lived in the
cave, and who was forging a sword for Siegfried, whom
he had reared. One could tell he was a gnome by the fact
that the actor walked all the time bending the knees of his
trico-covered legs . . . Siegfried was brought up by the
gnome, and therefore, for some reason, hates him, and
always wishes to kill him. . . . Siegfried seizes one of the
pieces of what is meant to represent the broken sword,
saws it up, puts it on what is meant to represent the
forge, melts it, and then forges it and sings: Heiho!
heiho ! heiho ! Ho ! ho! Aha ! oho ! aha ! Heiabo !
heiaho ! heiaho ! Ho 1 ho 1 Hahei 1 hoho ! hahei! and
Act I. finishes.
This is not unamusing reading; and there is a good
deal of justification for the standpoint. Con-
sidered apart from the music, the bulk of Wagner
libretto is ridiculous. You have to disentangle
the cardinal motives from a heap of rubbish; but,
this done, the music gives those motives un-
equalled impetus. For the impression of Wag-
nerian opera on a sympathetic mind read the
description of “Tristan and Isolde” in “The
Triumph of Death.” Tolstoy seems as lacking in
musical sympathy as in poetical.
* • • *
With Wagner go Tolstoy’s own artistic produc-
tions—“l consign them to he category of bad art,
excepting the story ‘God Sees the Truth’ and
‘ The Prisoner of the Caucasus.’ ” With Wagner
tumble “classics ” and “decadents ” alike'.
Every false work extolled by the critics serves as a door
through which the hypocrites of art at once crowd in. It
is solely due to who in our times still praise
rude, sav ige, and, for us, often meaningless works
of the ancient Greeks : Sophocles, Euripides, Aeschylus,
and especially Aristophanes, or, of modern writers, Dante,
Tasso, Milton, Shakespeare ; in painting, ail of Raphael,
all of Michael Angelo, including his absurd “ Last Judg-
ment ; ” in music, the whole of Bach, and the whole of
Beethoven, including his last period ;—thanks only to
them have the Ibsens, Maeterlincks, Verlaines, Mal-
larmes, Puvis de Ohavannes, Klingers, Boklins, Stucks,
Schneiders; in music, the Wajriiei's, Liszts, Berliozes,
Brahms, and Richard Strausses, &e., and all that im-
mense mass of {rood-for-notirin.sr imitators of these imita-
tors, become possible in our (1%.
All swept into the dust-heap of false Art—was
there ever such a besom ! And in the very next
paragraph Tolstoy is sanely and forcibly objecting
to tile glorification of the deaf-period Beethoven.
. magnificent mixture his book, but it is not
Let this notice close with an example
ol lolstoy m his better mood
If Art has been able to convey the sentiment of
reverence for imaires, for the eucharist, and
for the king’s person ; of shame at betraying a comrade,
devotion to a flag, tho necessity of revenge for an insult,
the need to sacrifice one’s labour for the erection and
adornment of churches, duty of defending one's honour
or the glory of one’s native land—then that same art can
also evoke reverence for the dignity of every man and for
the life of every animal ; can make men ashamed of
luxury, of violence, of revenge, or of using for their plea-
sure that of which others are in need ; can compel people
freely, gladly, and without noticing it, to sacrifice them-
selves for the service of man.

Bards of the Backblocks.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Border Gate.
Dawn gilds the spiders’ bridges;
Morn mocks the shadows’ rout}
A mile back on the ridges
They put the head-lights out;
A red-topped coach from Nor’ward
Comes down with clanking bars:
The waking Day lies forward,
Behind—the drowsy stars 1
A foe no floods can ruin,
A force no droughts abate,
The night-mail from the Tuen
Swings through the Border Gate 1
A still world—faint and swooning
Beneath a fevered sky ;
Before the great wheels’ groaning
Slow bullocks snailing by;
With dusty, cursing drivers,
And roaring fall of whips
Comes rocking from red rivers
The cream of Queensland clips.
“ Gee off, there ! Clown and Drover t n
The brown loads creak and grate.
“Stand over, there ! Stand Over ! ”
She clears the Border Gate !
A sunset-fired horizon
Beyond the dust-wrack dense,
A draft from Jimmy Tyson
Slow-feeding to the fence ;
A rush of long-horned leaders,
A tramp of feet below,
A ripping of red “ bleeders ”
And “ Woh, there! Woh, boys! Woh-h!”
The posts are strong—with reason.
Which stand to such a weight;
The finest stores this season
Crowd through the Border Gate!
The old gay life is over ;
We’ve left the great North Road ;
The red dust wraps the drover,
The gray dust hides the load ;
Though I’ll no more go through you,
Old Gate of Memories mine !
I wave a brown hand to you
From long leagues down the line . . .
Wherever Time shall speed me
Before the winds of Fate,
I know my dreams will lead me
Back to the Border Gate !
W. H. OGILVIE.
“After Thompson’s Party.”
Cornin' 'ome from Thompson’s party,
Me an’ Joe fell out;
Joe an’ me got narked,
Cornin’ ’ome from Thompson’s party.
Mates we’d alius bin since so-’igh ;
I says “ yes ” w’en Joe says “ yo-hai”;
Stuck to one-another ’earty
Till that night of Thompson’s party.
Name the bart as made us quarrel ?
Name the bart yerself 1
Twenty quid yer carn’t!
Share our only pound or fifty ;
Neither one’s bin over-thrifty.
“ My mate says so,” was our moral:
Barts would never make us quarrel!
Drink ! W’y we’ve been drunk together
Time an’ time agen !
Seen each other ’ome ;
Camped in the same bunk together;
Mates w’en sober, an’ good liquor
On’y made our friendship thicker.
Bound for Sheol “ L ” for leather,
Neither one, or both together I
Neither wine nor women done it.
Nor it wasn’t coin :
Joe and me got narked—
It was Joe’s fox-terrier done it.
Joe says my pup set ’is poodle.
I says “ Plank the blanky boodle !
Don’t care w’o the ’ ell begun it;
Let ’em fight! ” An’-—well, we done it t
At it went them dorgs, like blazes 1
Ts’n’ got the grip :
My pup chewed ’s lip :
Chewed an’ yelled an’ fought like blazes !
First the one on top, then t’other !
We was breathin’ like to smother,
Dodgin’ round in twenty places,
Soolin’ of ’em on like blazes 1
Joe says my pup weren’t jonick l
Couldn’t suffer that.
“ ’More ain’t yours ! ” says I.
(’Struth ! to say my pup aint jonick I)
“ Lie ! ” says ’e, an’ I says “ Crawler !”
Biff ! comes Joseph’s gentle mauler.
To we sets, a regular tonic,
’Cos ’e says my pup aint jonick !
Cornin’ ’ome from Thompson’s party,
Me an’ Joe fell out—
Plugged each other blind !
Cornin' ’ome from Thompson’s party.
Beer an’ women couldn’t part us,
But that dorg-fight ’ad to start us
Quick an’ lively, ’ot an’ ’earty,
Coinin’ ’ome from Thompson’s party.
S.A.

Special Notice.
The whole contents of this newspaper are copyright by
the publishers. The right to reproduce or translate any
article, tale, sketch, poem, paragraph, illustration, or
any other part of the contents of this number, is hereby
expressly reserved by the publishers.
The Editor will carefully read and acknowledge in the
‘ Correspondence ” column all contributions submitted—
whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,
Verse, Short Tales or Sketches (those dealing with Aus-
tralian subjects, and not exceeding two columns in
length, or, say, 3000 words, are specially acceptable),
Paragraphs, Letters, or Newspaper-Clippings. All
communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.
The Bulletin will return all unsuitable MS. when
the address is specified and stamps are enclosed. But we
desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that
we are not responsible for its preservation or transmission.

Anyone supplying to The Bulletin original humorous or
other topical drawings in line or wash, of sufficient merit
and interest and adapted for direct reproduction, or any
interesting photograph illustrative of a current event,
will be paid for same on acceptance. Any picture con-
taining a good idea, but unfitted for direct reproduc-
tion— i.e., any picture which has to be re-drawn —will be
paid for as a suggestion only. The conditions under
which such artistic contributions will be received are those
applying to MS.

SATURDAY, SEPTEMBER 24, 1898.
Ferguson’s Amendment.
Incidentally, in the course of the late cen-
sure debate, the New South Wales Labor-
party, speaking through the mouth of
Ferguson, M.L.A., defined its Federal
attitude At least, Ferguson, M.L.A., pro-
fessed to speak for the party, and the party
didn’t repudiate its professed spokesman,
and, considering Ferguson’s position as one
of the most intellectual men of his crowd, it
seems reasonable to assume that he fairly
represents the Labor section of the New
South Wales Assembly. And, according to
Ferguson’s deliverance, the Labor men’s
idea of Federation is about as parochial as
the ideas of most other parties in New South
Wales and in Australia generally. The fol-
lowing, according to Ferguson, is the party’s
ultimatum :
The Labor-party was the strong Federal party in the
House. They never had said that no Upper Chamber,
placing the railways under Federal control, federating
the debts, or that the capital should be in this colony,
were essentials of Federation—only that they were desir-
able. The mass Referendum they, however, did regard
as essential, and also that a simple majority should pre-
vail at a joint sitting of the two Houses was essential. It
was said the small States would not accept a simple
majority in such cases, and they were not likely to whilst
New South Wales had such representatives as Messrs.
Barton and Wise telling them it was not necessary, and
that Federation could be obtained without it. National
questions were what the Federal Parliament would have
to deal with, and they should be dealt with in a
national spirit—that was by a simple majority. He
admitted that State rights should be protected. They
should lay it down distinctly in the Bill that no territory
should he handed over by or taken away from any Slate
unless with the consent of that State, and it should be
laid down that no fresh power should be given
to the Federal Parliament unless with the con-
sent of the States, and no power should be taken
away from any State unless with its consent. The
crucial point would come when they were asked
to state definitely what sacrifices they were prepared to
make in order to obtain Federation. That was the time
when they would be able to distinguish the real enemies
and friends of Australian union. There were, to his
mind, only two essentials to Federation—a simple
majority in the joint sitting of the two Houses and a
mass Referendum for alterations in the Constitution con-
fined exclusively to those subjects which were handed
over to the Federal Parliament to deal with. He would
not accept Federation with a thousand amendments un-
less these two were granted. New South Wales had a
riffht at this juncture to state the prioe she was prepared
to pay for union.
• * • •
So the Labor-party goes back on its Refer-
endum just as Premier Reid did when he re-
fused to trust the majority with power to
decide the acceptance or rejection of the
Federal Constitution. According to Fergu-
son, M.L.A., the Australian majority is to
boss the show in a few things only, and there
is to be majority rule with strict limitations.
The present generation—the one which is
reared in the traditions and the atmosphere
of provincialism—is to define the powers of
the Federal Parliament and those of-the
State Legislatures. And it is to enact that
after it is dead and gone, and the winds have
blown the forgotten dust of Ferguson,
M.L.A., over the Barrier and away across
the Milparinka plains, the power of the
majority shall be strictly limited through
all succeeding ages by the bounds fixed
for it by Ferguson and his contem-
poraries. The New South Wales Labor-
party, according to Ferguson, M.L.A.,
proposes in the most important respects to
make the alleged cast-iron Constitution of
the late Convention Bessemer steel. That
Constitution, as drafted by the Convention,
is a painfully narrow affair. A scared race,
with the old provincial shackles on its soul,
was so badly afraid to trust the Australian
people with the control of Australia that it
placed the Federal authority under severe
limitations. It gave the Federal Govern-
ment no definite power to take over the
public debts and railways, to make a uniform
criminal code, or to readjust the purely arti-
ficial boundaries of the various sub-divisions
of the country, however necessary such re-
adjustment might be ; and it provided that
all new questions that might arise should be
questions to be decided by the State Legis-
latures. Instead of defining the powers of
the States and leaving the Federal authority
to deal with everything else, it made the
great mistake of defining the Federal autho-
rity and leaving everything else to the little
State Legislatures and the aldermanic func-
tionaries left therein. But when, later on,
the nation has lost some of its parochial
instincts, and the inevitable question of
enlarging the Federal power comes to the
front, the Convention Bill makes such an
enlargement at least reasonably possible. It
can be effected by a majority of the voters
who go to the poll and a majority of the
States. This is the cast-iron constitution of
the Federal Convention, and, as cast-iron is
brittle, the adjective about expresses the
situation.
* * * c
Ferguson, on behalf of the New South
Wales Labor-party, proposes to alter this by
requiring a majority vote in every State. It
is obviously impossible to enlarge the powers
of the Federal Government without reducing
those of the State Parliaments. And the
proposal that no powers shall be taken from
any State without its consent means that the
smallest majority in the smallest State can
block the amendment of the Federal Con-
stitution in the direction where amendment
is most likely to be needed ! The fact that
the people at large are to have power
to vary the Constitution in a few things
is small compensation if a majority of one
in Tasmania is to override the practically
unanimous vote of nearly 4,000,000 Austra-
lians in many things. If the Federal system
is a success the people will almost certainly
desire to extend the powers of the one
Legislature which represents Australia as a
whole. And by that time Ferguson will
probably be dead, and yet the dead hand of
the Ferguson party will reach down through
the ages to deny the Australian people of the
future the right to decide their own destiny
in this matter. It will decree that the
smallest majority in the smallest and least
progressive province—and there always is
one unprogressive province which lags be-
hind the rest—can prohibit all growth and
expansion ; and there won’t even be the
satisfaction of knowing that the dead pro-
vincialists who devised this scheme gain any
joy or benefit thereby. When some future
generation decides that it is absolutely neces-
sary, for the good of the state, to transfer
certain powers from the provinces to the
Federal Government, it is obvious that the
transfer, to be effective, must be made by all
the provinces. And if a million majority in
New' South Wales, Victoria, Queensland,
South Australia, and Westralia says yes, and
it is blocked by a majority of a dozen
noes in Tasmania, then it would be interest-
ing to know where Democratic principles, and
the rule of the majority, and the Referendum,
and the popular right of self-government
have gone to.
* * ♦ *
The proposal that no amendment shall be
made in the Federal Constitution, except
with the assent of the majority of the pro-
vinces, was a compromise dictated by the
fears of the small provinces, lest the big pro-
vinces should swallow them up. But there
is no need to make the situation worse than
it already is. The proposal that State boun-
daries shall not be altered, except with the
consent of the province which it is proposed
to subdivide, is one which past and present ex-
perience shows to be a ridiculous fooling with
a weapon that is loaded. The old, unwieldy
province of New South Wales didn’t consent to
the secession of Victoria and Queensland,
and if there had been no higher authority to
interfere the most probable result would
have been civil war Queensland and Wes-
tralia have each a similar problem ripening
now. And it is seriously advocated that,
when an outlying part of a province is robbed
and exploited for the benefit of an over-
grown capital city, as Port Phillip was, and
as North Queensland is, and as Northern
Westralia probably will be by the Perth and
Bunbury gang when it is rich enough to be
worth robbing, there shall be no remedy
except civil war. When the difficulty anses
the people of that day will probably be much
better fitted to deal with it than any dead
and gone Ferguson of 100 or 500 or 1000
years back. At all events it is their own
difficulty, and even if the politicians of to-
day are so much wiser than anybody else
that they are capable of providing for such
contingencies to all futurity the live people
of the period have the right to settle their
own problem according to their own wisdom
—or their own foolishness. And they have
also the same right to decide as to the ex-
tension or limitation of the Federal power
in their day as the present Fergusons have
in theirs. If the "bare majority can be
trusted in one case it can probably be trusted
in the other also.
• * * •
The amendments that are most wanted are
all in the direction of enlarging the Federal
powers. The central authority—the one
which represents the people of Australia as
a whole —should have absolute power over
the debts, railways and education system,
have power to make a uniform criminal and
civil code, to adjust interprovincial disputes,
and to subdivide States if the people of any
part of a State can show a tolerably unani-
mous desire for separation and the Federal
Legislature sees no good reason to the con-
trary. Also, deadlocks should be settled by
a bare majority at a joint sitting of the
Federal Houses, and amendments or ex-
tensions of the Federal authority should be
made as easy as possible. If, owing to pro-
vincial jealousies or suspicious, this idea is
impossible, it should be approached as
nearly as can be. But to enact that there
shall be no extension of the narrow Federal
authority except with the consent of all the
provinces—that the smallest majority in the
smallest State can block all progress and
expansion until the union is amended by
brute force or burst up by violence —is retro-
gression and Toryism. The present idea,
which requires the assent of a majority of
the provinces, in addition to the assent of a
majority of the people, is bad enough. The
one proposed in its stead is many times worse.
The Impure-drug Question.
A MAN who knows writes on a matter urgently
needing State attention :
Ed. Bulletin. —In issue 10/9/’9B “ H.G.” opens up an
interesting- question re the purity of the drugs the public
is so fond of pouring into itself. This question is occa-
sionally ventilated in medical journals, but, after a brief
and technical discussion, it drops again into obscurity.
The average medico who prescribes drugs knows vaguely
that some preparations differ considerably in strength in
different specimens, but, as he is already expected to work
24 hours a day in the service of club-patients who pay
from 12s. 6d. to £1 per annum for his attentions, he has
not got time to make exhaustive quantitative and qualita-
tive.analyses for their benefit. The chemist doesn’t care
—his troubles 1 so long as he gets his ss. per annum—•
and he contents himself with a superior smile when the
puzzled medico unburdens his soul on the subject of
Mrs. Jones’ sudden and fatal idios.vncracy for morphia.
Let me set out a few facts throwing, light on the question.
* * * *
Chlorinated lime (the allegedly-uncertain snake-bite
antidote) should contain 33 per cent, of free chlorine.
Twelve specimens secured from different town and coun-
try pharmacies gave from 1-3 to 15'8 per cent.—the
maximum. On the presence of this free chlorine depends
the efficacy of the drug. Hydrocyanic acid, of which
your correspondent speaks, should have, according to the
British Pharmacopeia, two per cent, by weight of the
pure acid. It lands here never stronger than 1-7 per
cent, and perishes to nil. Sciieele’s acid (made in Ger-
many) is better, its normal four per cent, remaining fairly
constant, through good preparation and careful packing.
Opium, again, the Pharmacopeia alleges to contain 9(4
to 10)4 P er cent, of morphine. Twenty analyzed speci-
mens obtained in Australian cities gave a range of from
3 to 14'2 per cent. As the tincture (laudanum) is very
often manufactured by the chemist himself in the good
old rule-of-thumb B.P. way (by macerating an ounce and
a half of opium in a pint of proof spirit) the chances of
hitting off the official strength of 1 gr. in 14£ minims are
about as remote as Federation under a Reid Government.
* * • •
Turn to antiseptics. Puerperal fever is on the in-
crease, despite careful anti and aseptic technique at
parturitions. Carbolic acid is the favorite antiseptic here,
as perchloride of mercury ruins instrument plating, and
accuracy of solution strength is naturally necessary.
Most ordinary shop specimens of so-called pure carbolic
run from 20 to 50 per cent, under stated strengths.
lodoform, the most widely-used of surgical antiseptics, is
about half flour in most chemists’ specimens ! If the
manufacturers must adulterate it, they might at least
use boracic acid, which is nearly as cheap as flour and it-
self a good antiseptic.
* • * *
The impurity of chloroform is a crying evil, of which
laymen and most general practitioners have no concep-
tion. The B.P. tests for purity are moderately satis-
factory, but not one chloroformist in a hundred applies
them before use. ’s chloroform is perhaps
the only reliable preparation shipped, but as the bottles,
when stoppered, often sweat off a third of their contents
during the voyage they have to be filled up on arrival.
The filling is often done with German chloroform ; and,
regarding German chloroforms, Hewitt, the English
authority on anaesthetics, gives a lurid accountof samples
obtained from a “ respectable German firm.” Here’s one :
“ It had a most disagreeable odour of nitro-benzol and
tobacco, and when inhaled produced giddiness and head-
ache. After 48 hours the smell was replaced by that of
benzoic acid. When the residue was heated and partly
evaporated the remainder turned brown and evolved a
smell like burnt india-rubber. The chloroform from
which the residue was obtained had caused alarming
symptoms in one half of the cases in which it had been
employed.”
Those “in the know” get their chloroform in sealed
flasks to private order. The only efficacious method of
purifying chloroform is by Pictet’s crystallisation freez-
ing process, and by special arrangement this can be done
before shipment -but it is far too expensive for every-day
use.
To sum up. Hospital and lodge committees hold
Economy above all things, thereby producing a demand
for cheap drugs. Cheap drugs mean adulterated drugs,
and as there is no fixed standard of adulteration, any
change of the source of supply may cause serious conse-
quences. Anyone will understand this who has spent a
night lugging an insensible 18st. patient with pin-point
pupils and icy extremities round a waid just because the
hospital committee had got a new and conscientious
chemist the day before. Next committee-day some pom-
pous ass will arise and ask for a vote of censure on the
(sweated) doctor and (underpaid) chemist.
• * * *
There is only one solution for these difficulties, and we
may live to see it. The appointment of a well-paid
expert in each capital, with an efficient staff under his
control, and the power to take samples from all imported
and locally-made medicinal preparations wheresoever he
thinks necessary, would go far towards stamping out the
evil. A little legislation to the effect that any person
selling drugs condemned by that expert, or adulterating
articles he had passed, should he visited with heavy
punishment ; including fine and deletion front the roll ot
licensed chemists —would go further. We pay a quaran-
tine department to keep out foreign diseases, and \\ hy
should we not have one to testify to the purity of the
agents necessary for combating those we have at home .
—Piiyyaka.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Wonderful G. Reid.
Premier Reid at Kempsey, as reported in last
Monday’s Telegraph
Some people had said that the financial clauses in the
Convention Bill were those which he took special ex-
ception to, but that was not so. He took greater ex-
ception to the clause which fixed a three-fifths majority
in the joint sitting. Under that clause when the joint
sitting of the two Federal Houses took place the minority
of 38 could override the majority of 56.
Quite so. And it was Reid’s colleague, Carru-
THERS, who proposed the three-tilths majority,
and according to the damning record on pages
974 and 975 of Convention Hansard (21st Sep-
tember, 1897), Reid, Brunker and Carru-
THERS, all of the CoD-fearing Ministry, voted
for the three-fifths majority most persist-
ently. The division on page 974 shows
the curious result that every N.S.W. delegate
present voted, with Reid’s assistance and cor-
dial approval, for the undemocratic three-fifths
majority. On the other hand, eight out of ten
Victorian delegates voted against it, also
three South Australians, and two Tasmanians.
Another quaint circumstance relates to S.A.
Premier Kingston’s proposed insertion of the
national Referendum in the Convention Bill.
The division bv which the Referendum was
scornfully cast out on its ear is recorded in the
Convention Hansard of 21st September, 1897*
(page 967). It shows that out of seven N.S. VV.
delegates present six (including Reid, Car-
ruthers, and BitUNKER) voted against the
Referendum, and the only N.S.W. delegate
who supported it was B. R. Wise. (Curi-
ously enough, some of the beautiful
political wrigglers who voted against the
Referendum themselves now accuse Wise,
the one N.S.W. delegate who voted for it at
this critical moment, as being an opponent of
the Referendum.) Eleven votes were cast for
the Referendum altogether ; the Victorian con-
tingent, which kept pretty steady on the
Democratic side, supplied six of these ; S.A.
furnished three, Westralia one, N.S.W. one
(B. R. Wise). Of the 30 votes cast against
the Referendum, Tasmania supplied nine,
Westralia seven, N.S.W. and S.A. six each,
Victoria only two. Therefore the N.S.W.
Ministers in the Convention voted solidly on
this occasion for the three-fifths majority and
against the Referendum, and it was this vote
which caused Carruthers’ accursed idea of
the three-fifths majority to be first inserted in
the luckless Bill, where it remained ever after-
wards. If Premier Reid and his following
had stood by the Victorian contingent—the
only one which was reasonably unanimous
against the undemocratic three-fifths idea—
there would at least have been a very small
majority in its favor ; possibly no majority at
all. It is records like these which make The
Bulletin regard Premier Reid as the most
brazen political adventurer in Australia. It
doesn’t much mind him voting for the three-
fifths majority if his convictions lay that way ;
but when he did all he knew to insert that
unlucky idea in the Bill, and then declared the
Bill impossible because that idea was in it, and
denied, or practically denied, that he had sup-
ported it, he gets pretty low down in the mire
of political degradation.

The House of Culverts.
The corrupt road-and-bridge vote is being
worked by the Reid Ministry for all it is
worth to down Barton at Macleay (N.S.W.).
S. Smith, the lately rejected Minister for
Vegetables, is contesting the electorate in the
anti-Federal interest, and Minister Potato-
Young, of the Works Department, the gentle-
man who looks after roads and bridges and
culverts, is stumping the district along with
him. Minister Potato- Young practically calls
on the people to remember that ex-Minister
Smith has got the ear of the same Cabinet
which lately built “a very successful bridge”
up there, and spent much money on a local
harbor. Also, he implies very distinctly that
they ought to elect Smith because, if he gets
in, he will chew the ear of the Government
over a local drainage matter. And he points
out to them that Barton, if he is elected,
“ would not lose his time in going round to the
different departments to watch over their in-
terests ” —in other words, he distinctly implies
that Barton would not get them roads and
bridges where Smith would. All this boils
down to a very simple proposition. Minister
Young bosses the distribution of roads and
bridges. The people pay for them exactly the
same whether they are represented by Barton
or Smith, and public works are supposed to be
distributed with absolute impartiality whether
the people elect Barton or Smith. If it will be
better for the people, in the public works line,
that they should elect Smith, then that can
only mean that Young is a shamelessly crooked
Minister who uses the public money to buy
political support for his Ministry ; or that he
is an incompetent fool who gives public money
when he shouldn’t do so to mollify the depart-
ment-trotter, or doesn’t give it when he should
do so in cases where there is no department-
trotter to mollify. And when he points
to the money which has been lately spent
in the electorate as a reason why the people
should support the Reid Government, it
apparently means one of two things—either
that the people are expected to be grateful
because they got more than their fair
share of public money to the injury of other
localities, or that they are expected to be grate-
ful because they weren’t robbed by Minister
Young’s department of their fair share of public
money, it being a department which is quite
capable of such a thing. Also, there lies beyond
the possible inference that they had better wake
up and vote for Smith, lest the Works Depart-
ment should either rob them in the future, or
else give up robbing other localities for (heir
benefit : or lest, Young, being a man who needs


Smith to tell him his own business, shouldn’t
know how to do it, so far as that electorate is
concerned, for want of Smith. When Sydney
Telegraph publishes a statement of the money
spent by the Reid Government in Macleay
electorate, that is also a pretty straight hint
that the money was meant to buy the Macleay
vote, and that the supply may he stopped by a
corrupt Government if Smith doesn’t get in.
And when Premier Reid writes to the Macleay
electors that Smith will “work for all their
legitimate local needs with unswerving devo-
tion,” that, is another pretty straight reach-out
for the local drain vote, and an encouraging
hint that the Reid Government will listen to the
local cry for works —provided it comes through
Smith. Reid, and the Telegraph and Young
and Smith can divide these alternatives among
them. Anyhow, the whole disgraceful busi-
ness lends additional weight to The Bulletin’s
contention that there should be a Second Cham-
ber elected by the people on a manhood suffrage
basis, with the whole province as one electorate,
so that it couldn’t be elected on the local cul-
vert vote, and the people could send there the
men who are too big and honest and indepen-
dent to peddle miserably for votes on the
bridge and reclamation ticket, and whose only
qualification is that they want to help along
the country in general—not the hamlet of Boppy
Mountain alone. Young’s case against Barton
is practically that he is too big an Australian
patriot to keep his eye always on small local
jobs, and he urges the electors to vote for
Smith on the ground, to all intents and pur-
poses, that he is a paltry little commonplace
man who won’t let great Australian questions
come between him and the mud-reclamation
contract. The Daily Telegraph’s attempt to
wake up the old sectarian row by alleging that
Barton —a broad-minded gentleman of Protes-
tant birth —has the local Catholic vote, and
therefore (by implication) that the Protestant
Christian should vote for Smith out of a pious
loathing for the Holy Roman Christian, is such
an old, familiar disgrace that it hardly attracts
notice. But Minister Young’s mode of elec-
tioneering has brought a comparatively new
variety of disgrace into the Macleay contest.

Tottie in Politics.
Apparently, one of Victoria’s urgent needs is
a medical inquiry into the mental condition of
Campbell, M.L.C., which extraordinary pillar
of the State has, by his own account, employed
a detective to discover the reason why prosti-
tutes clamor for the suffrage (if they do clamor).
And the answer furnished to Campbell,
M.L.C. (so he says), by the detective, was as
follows (Argus report):—
Women of the unfortunate class said they frequently
had members of Parliament under their thumbs, and, if
they fret a vote, they would have double power over them.
Later on he said that :
His (Campbell’s) own wife told him that, if he voted
for the Bill, she would fro for a divorce.
Now, even the dottiest legislator surely knows
that his wife can’t get a divorce on such
grounds (if he has previously given her legal
cause, and she has agreed to condone the
offence provided he votes against the Bill, that
isn’t a case in point). And, as regards the
other part of his argument, the only way the
possession of the suffrage could give the alleged
Tottie; “double power” over the M.P. who is
already in her clutches, is that it would give
her power to vote against him. There are
245,000 voters in Victoria ; with female suffrage,
there would be about 490,000; and, if the
Tottie vote would count for anything in
that crowd, then Victoria must be the most
Tottie- infested country on earth. Campbell,
M.L.C., represents one forty-eighth, or say 2
per cent., of the legislative obstructive force
in Victoria, and Lis yarn about himself, and the
policeman, and the Totties who have dark
designs upon the ballot-box, is a public matter.
Therefore, considering that no intelligent male
person believes the Campbell, M.L.C., yarn,
or can see what possible difference it could make
to the Woman Suffrage principle if it were true,
or would willingly give Campbell, M.L.C.,
credit for being a fictionist with malice afore-
thought, the question as to the state of Camp-
bell, M.L.C.’s, brain is important. If he is
anyway wrong in the head lie ought not to have
the worries of political life inflicted upon him.
The interests of humanity demand that Camp-
bell’s intellect should be overhauled by a
specialist; also the specialist, whilst he is on
the job, might as well go all round the Leg.
Council and report upon the general queerness of
the capitalists there assembled. The Bulle-
tin doesn’t suppose that Campbell, M.L.C., is
any “dottier” than most of them. Probably
be has his good nights and his bad nights, and
this happened to be one of his worst.
State Leaseholds.
South Australia, which leads the way among
the six Australian States in most reforms, is
considering a measure which some of its neigh-
bors should think hard over. It provides
that no more State lands shall be sold except
in completion of existing bargains ; future
transactions are to be by way of lease only.
Also, there is to be no trafficking in the State
leaseholds granted under the new law. Any
person who desires to transfer his lease must
first satisfy the Commissioner that the con-
sideration only equals the value of the improve-
ments thereon and no more. Also he must
have the Commissioner’s permission to sell.
This clause is of a lot more importance than
appears at first sight. If the Stale grants a
long lease at £lO a year, unforeseen circum-
stances may presently raise the value to £lOO a
year, and if the lease is saleable then it generally
is sold, and it keeps on passing from hand to
hand till the very last day of the State’s bad
bargain. The only lease that comes hack pre-
maturely on the hands of the State is the one
where the value has fallen. But if the added
value is non-saleable, then the lessee may hold
it for his own life, and his heirs may also hold
it, if he has any heirs. Should he become
bankrupt, however, or want to sell out, the
State will simply exercise its power to refuse
assent to any sale of the improvements to a
private individual, and buy them itself, and
resume possession of the property. This power
makes the State leasehold practically non-
mortgageable, and will prevent the State lease-
holds being swallowed wholesale by banks and
usurers; it will cheek dummying; and it will, in
many cases, enable the State to get back a
portion of the “ unearned increment.” There
is also provision for granting perpetual lease-
holds, but a terrifying knock to banks, mortgage
co.’s and private land-grabbers is dealt in a
clause which provides that the unimproved
value of all the agricultural land which one in-
dividual can hold under perpetual lease, or that
combined with all other forms of tenure, cannot
exceed <£sooo. And, if it is rated as pastoral,
the utmost amount which one individual can
hold under perpetual lease, 4 or that and other
forms of tenure combined, is pasturage for
5000 sheep. The big landholder doesn’t come
in anywhere under the perpetual lease system,
but, if it is intelligently worked, it should raise
a valuable race of small mortgage-proof agri-
culturists and pastoralists in S.A.
A Gridiron Syndicate.
The Great Midland and West Coast Railway
Bill, at present before Tas. Parliament, is a
modest little proposal on the part of a gridiron
syndicate and deserves to die comparatively
young. The co. asks for 300,000 acres of land
mostly decent agricultural land at that, 20,000
effective horse-power of water, the right to sell
water, light, and power, the right to erect
telegraphs and telephones, and the right to
compete with adjacent Govt, railways. All
this in return for building and owning as its
own private spec, some 60 miles of railway
from the handiest spot in a settled district
to some vague, indefinite point “within
the western mining division of Tasmania.”
Furthermore, the co. specifically bargains
that the State shall not have the right
to control the tolls, rates, or charges, which
may be fixed at any figure the syndicate
pleases. Also the syndicate is to have
a right, under certain conditions, to more
land grants, over and above the 300,000 acres
already mentioned. It is to have prac-
tically unlimited powers to take water from the
Mersey, Forth and Leven rivers. There is
already a tolerably thick settlement along these
rivers, so the blessed syndicate will probably be
able to confiscate the local water supply and
sell it back to the local inhabitants at a high
figure, though its powers in this direction are
not quite certain as. yet. Back, General
Manager of Tasmanian State Railways, presents
a damning report, one clause being as follows :
The company proposes to construct 60 miles of railway,
the cost of which is not ascertained, but f r the purpose
of comparison may be set down at £200,000. If the
company can make full use of the concessions asked for in
the bill (and if they cannotdo so there is nothing to pre-
vent the rights being locked up from the public for years)
the}' will receive an annual income exceeding the primary
cost of the railway.
Therefore a responsible State official reckons
that Tasmania is going to give to this shocking
swindle .£200,000 a year or more, in considera-
tion of its expenditure of .£200,000 all told.
But the syndicate-mongering members of Tas.
Parliament—and they are many—have appar-
ently quite made up their minds to get the Bill
through by hook or crook despite the Railway
Manager’s crushing report. One very big rea-
son for Federal railway control sticks out here.
A very small local Parliament which doesn’t
pay its members a living wage, is altogether
too amenable to cash insult or insult in other
forms which are equivalent to cash.
Legal Cruelty.
In connection with proceedings in Divorce, the
view the law takes as to “ cruelty ” is one which
greatly exercises the lay mind. During the
present sittings of ISf.S. W. Divorce Court a wife
was petitioner, and, according to S.M. Herald,
His Honor Judge Simpson made the following
remarks (an indignant lady correspondent for-
wards the clipping to The Bulletin) :
His Honor : Legal cruelty has not been proved. Black-
ening a woman’s eyes is not legal cruelty.
Mr. Fraser : She had to go to the hospital.
His Honor: That does not matter. She had two
stitches put in the cut, and there was an end of it. It
was gross brutality, no doubt, but not legal cruelty.
This, at first sight, seems a shocking slate of
affairs, and the public finds it hard to under-
stand the distinction here drawn. Yet there
is some ground for it. Legal cruelty lies in the
cumulative ill-conduct which the history of the
married life discloses, and in Oliver v. Oliver
(12 W. W.) it was defined as—
Such conduct on the part of the husband as has injured,
or is calculated to endanger the health or safety of the
wife, or to cause a reasonable apprehension that the
health and safety of the wife will be endangered if co-
habitation be continued. ... It is unnecessary that any
physical violence should have been indicted or appre-
hended, if the conduct of the husband, although un-
accompanied by blows, be such as to injure or be calcu-
lated to endanger the health of the wife. . . . There must
be something which renders cohabitation unsafe or likely
to be attended with injury to the person or health of the
party complaining.
Here the ground of the interference of the Court
is plainly laid down. It may need altering,
but the distinction drawn is perfectly intelli-
gible. A husband who strikes his wife once, or
once in a way, is not guilty of “ cruelty,” while
one who never raises his hand to his wife,
but is yet guilty of conduct “ so harsh and irri-
tating, so calculated to lacerate the feelings of a
wife and outrage the instincts of a mother as
to endanger her health,” will find that his
behavior falls within the law’s definition of
cruelty. It is to be observed that this
is the view of the Divorce Court only, and
refers to cruelty as a matrimonial offence.
Though a husband may not be amenable to
the Divorce Court for acts of brutality as dis-
tinguished from legal cruelty, a wife is not
without a remedy for personal injuries inflicted
by her husband : she may summon him for
assault in the same way as she may summon a
stranger. It must be admitted, however, that
Sydney magistrates seem to be imbued with
the idea that a husband who bashes his wife
commits a very trivial offence, as the reports
of the sentences imposed on wife-beaters will
show.

A little while ago 14 members of Victoria’s
House of 48 Rich Landlords went out by
effluxion of time, and 12 of them were re-
elected without opposition. The other two
capitalists retired through old age, or fatty
degradation, or some: hing, and Iho two Tories
who were put up in their stead were also
elected without opposition. There wasn’t one
contest in all the fourteen electorates, and
there hardly ever is one. Nothing can rouse
the Victorian people to take any interest in
Council elections ; no Democrat ever puts
up ; nobody ever asks a Democrat to put
up ; and, as things are, all hope of libera-
lising the dreadful establishment which chucks
out all promising legislation seems deader than
the dodo. And the visible reasons for this
utter deadness are these five : (1) The property
qualification for members, which keeps poor
men out; (2) the non-payment of members,
which does ditto ; (3) the restricted franchise,
though the franchise isn’t much restricted, and
this is only a minor cause ; (4) the fact that
there is no chance in the Council of rising to
any important Ministerial office, and little
chance of initiating legislation therefore, able
and ambitious politicians keep away ; (5) the
fact that the Council doesn’t dissolve like the
Assembly, and go to the country in a time of
red-hot political excitement, but merely has a
sleepy little bye-election of its own once every
two years, when nobody is looking. And, while
these circumstances continue, the only promising
way that The Bulletin sees to mend the 48
Rich Landlords is to put a liberal supply of
prussic-acid in the refreshments.

Interesting little facts re New Guinea land-
grab. The Govt, surveyor has selected land
(says Gov. M'Gregor) which is ready for trans-
ference to the syndicate ; 1900 acres on the
Geiinauri is described as first-class land, suit-
able for sugar, coffee, maize, or cocoa-nuts.
Another patch of 5000 acres on the Aroana,
mostly fertile plain, could be cleared at a cost
of some 20s. per acre. Another corner of 4500
acres on the Robinson River is described as
first-class country, capable of growing anything
—and so on ad lib. Evidently the very pick of
Papua has been got ready for the hand of the
grabsters. Most of the 31,000 acres surveyed,
we are informed, “ has been bought from the
natives by the Crown.” Indeed ! and with,
whose money? What funds have the Adminis-
trators of Papua other than those supplied by
the Australias ? By-and-bye, under the insane
agreement which gives the British shindykit
first call on all available lands at 2s. an acre,
no matter what they cost, we may see the
Papuan Government reduced to buying land
from the natives at 10s. an acre (paid for with
Australia’s money) to hand it over to the Bull
shindykit at 80 per cent. loss. We shall yet
learn that Australian funds have been used to
fortify Port Moresby against any invading
marauders who hail from Queensland, N.S.W.,
or Victoria.
Cosme Paraguay—committee recently re-
ported on the failing prospects of the settle-
ment in this wise :
Failure is due to misfortunes with crops, drought, and
inability of British comers to adapt themselves to the
principles of communism, teetotalism, life-marriage and
color line.
Just notice : You may get a polygamous folk to
be teetotal: you can get a beer or wine-drink-
ing people to be monogamous ; but teetotolism
and monogamy together are too tough a morsel
for the average human. Furthermore, only a
group of survivors from the Golden A could
possibly accommodate themselves to the above-
quoted modest little programme and they
would, probably, owing to cosmopolitan sym-
pathies, trip now and again over the “ color-
line.” The apostle, William Lane, has actually
drawn up a stiffer code than even Christ,
Mahomet or Moses ever thought necessary or
practicable, and his failure has naturally proved
as gigantic as was the success of the other
prophets. Fancy trying such an experiment
with a lot of loose-living, easy-going Western
men !
Some essence of the yarn told by John W t allace,
M.L.C., in that extraordinary Melb. law-case
last week :
I have been a member of the Vic. Leg. Council for the
past 24 years. lam sole trustee of the property—worth
about a million sterling—bequeathed to my children and
other relatives by my late father-in-law, Thomas Monahan.
I did not understand Mr. Monahan’s will at all, I did not
understand that the boys were to get their money at the
age of 21. (N.B —He threatened them with his stick
when th y asked for it.) I will not swear I understood
that the property was all to be invested on first mort-
gages in Victoria or in Government securities. I thought
that, because of a certain forfeiture clause in the will, I
need not give any of my children a shilling unless
I liked. I didn’t appoint a second trustee, because
I thought it was an advantage to have the estate in
my own control, to do what I liked with it, and I
acted as freely with the estate as if it were my
own, and (hotly) no freer (laughter). I did not look upon
money taken from the estate as borrowed money. I
thought it was all a family affair, and what belonged to
the family (Wallace has married a second wife since
Monahan’s decease) belonged to me, and what belonged
to me belonged to the family. I thought that what was
theirs was mine, and what was mine was my own—(cor-
recting himself in the reply) No, I don’t mean that.”
A lot more essence of Wallace, M.L.C., might
be given, but the foregoing suffices to show that
Premier Reid, when lie spoke of the Vic. House
of 48 Rich Landlords (at the Federal Conven-
tion) had a good grip of his subject. Said Reid :
‘•There are Houses which we know of in
Australia which have developed a spirit of
arrogance and self-sufficiency quite beyond that
of the House of Lords.” The only visible
excuse for John Wallace’s alleged ignorance
and stupidity and arrogance and self-sufficiency
in dealing with the Monahan estate was the
fact that he had already been a member of the
Leg. Council for 15 years when he started to
fulfil the conditions laid down in a document
which, by his own statement, he didn’t under-
stand. Wallace and his 47 colleagues in the
Council are the trustees of the People’s will.
They don’t understand the will at all, but they
know they have sole control of a lot of national
business, and they deal with it as they darn
well like.
Storey, M.L.A. (N.S.W.)
As for the boundaries of States it goes without saying
that they should not be interfered with in one iota except
by a majority vote of the people of the States interested.
But, then, it doesn’t go without saying. Suppose
North-Central Queensland has 400,000 people.
They have been tacked on to South Queensland
without any consent of their own. And they all
want separation, because their land revenue is
spent in the South, and their country is flooded
with Asiatics and niggers and leprosy because
the southern owners of N.Q. lands want cheap
labor. Suppose South Queensland, with 600,000
people, refuses to let the North go because
it is to their interest to hang on to it
and exploit it. Then Storey’s logic is that
N.Q. must be kept in eternal bondage
because by a mere accident the votes of North
and South must be counted together, and the
South has a majority. Storey’s logic, carried
to its legitimate conclusion, would give the
political majority power to hang the political
minority. Also, by the same logic, what is
the Christian minority in Turkey kicking up a
row with the Mahommedan majority for, and
demanding separation? And why does the pious
crowd to which Storey belongs sympathise
with Cuba ? Spain and Cuba are one State
just the same as North and South Queensland,
and the majority, which is Spain, refuses to let
the boundaries of the State be interfered with,
and insists on the Cuban minority staying in-
side the State. And Storey, and the Reid
party to which he belongs, defend this principle
when applied to Australia—Heaven only knows
why !
The withdrawal of the Workmen’s Lien Bill—
the first radical Govt, measure introduced into
Q. Legislature since the advent of the Labor-
party and Griffith’s apostacy —points a moral
that every “ thorough ” politician should print
on his shirt-front and recite with his prayers.
The Bill proposed to abolish attachment of
wages in cases of bona-fide manual workers.
Premier Byrnes, it is no secret, introduced the
measure in defiance of the majority of
his supporters and against the more or
less emphatically expressed wish of his
Tory colleagues. Knowing these facts, the
Opposition might 'wisely have refrained from
any attempt to get more than the Government
reluctantly offered, and from further embarass-
ing the author of the Bill. Instead of acting
with discretion, however, the Democratic Op-
position immediately proceeded to show up the
anomalies in the proposed Act,, and to amend it
by bringing clerks and sundry others within its
provisions. Then the opponents of the measure
on the Government side—willing to assist any
amendment likely to consign the measure to
perdition—sympathised with the Opposition,
favored and voted for its suggestions
—and accomplished their avowed object in
getting the Bill withdrawn. The Demo-
crats, therefore, in their anxiety to get a
perfect Democratic enactment from a hostile
Assembly, succeeded in killing the only Demo-
cratic legislative bantling presented to Queens-
land by the Coalition Ministry. The old
“whole hog or none” policy, with the old
result of no hog at all.
*
Brisbane Courier lifts its voice in protest
against the high rate of Queensland death-
duties. The Courier’s main argument ap-
pears to be that high death-duties “lessen
the motive for making money inasmuch
as they lessen the possibility of keeping
money.” The assumption that the death-duties
either take a man’s money while he is alive or
prevent him keeping it when he is dead, is mag-
nificent. Has the Courier yet to learn that the
typical money-grubber makes money not for his
wife,children or descendants, but simply to gra-
tify his greed for acquisition? Moreover, the fact
of a millionaire’s son having a part of his father’s
wealth taken aw’ay from him won’t stop his
acquiring or attempting to acquire—it will only
make him peg in vigorously and furiously to
acquire some more. It’s the young heir who
steps into possession of a ready-made million
who never thinks of accumulating—he’s far too
busy squandering. (Vide the Yankee proverb :
“Three generations from shirt-sleeves to shirt-
sleeves.”) Further, the Courier remarks that
the high death-duty system is an absurdity in-
asmuch as it “ kills the goose that lays the
golden eggs.” But aforesaid goose (millionaire,
of course), is already dead when the duty is
demanded, and so the State is only taking the
golden eggs from under a defunct goose that
cannot possibly produce any more such glitter-
ing ova. And, as to the millionaire’s son—in
nine cases out of ten—in place of conserving
his patrimony, he promptly proceeds to waste
it in every conceivable foolish fashion. No,
Courier ! The State that wants to encourage
acquisition and production should so frame its
laws that every young man will have to hustle
vigorously for a living instead of waiting idly
for dead men’s shoes and cursing his stars
because the old man doesn’t die earlier !
Dear Bulletin, —Seems to me absurd to con-
demn the police authorities for setting traps
for the evil-doer. The crime cannot be pre-
vented. These men are only dealing wdth the
detection and capture of know'll criminals.
Suppose a wild dog made frequent predatory
visits to your sheep-farm would you consider it
abetting the dog in the commission of the
offence to place a nice lamb where he would be
likely to see it; and lie low for him, close by,
with a gun? Wouldn’t that be a lot better
than strolling around on the off-chance of some
day catching him killing your 1000-guinea
ram? The abortionist makes a livelihood of
crime same as the dog, and, like him, is mighty
hard to catch. Therefore, we are as much
justified in the one case as in the other in set-
ting a snare for the enemy’s undoing
and our own protection. And please re-
member this : No man will fall in with the
disguised ’tec’s proposals unless he is an
abortionist. You don’t make mistakes by this
process and send innocent men to gaol. And
the other way convictions are often got on
dubious and largely circumstantial evidence. I
know' one man who did a long sentence in
Maoriland as an abortionist on very dubious
evidence. If the local detectives had hired
their minions to lay a trap for him, I believe he
would have rejected their overtures so indig-
nantly that they would have decided that he
wasn’t in the business, and that his accuser was
simply bent on blackmail. At all events, the
laying of such a trap would (if he w'as innocent,
as I firmly believe) have furnished powerful
evidence in his favor;— Papanui.
R. E. O’Connor, when N.S.W. Minister
for Justice, wrote a minute forbidding the admis-
sion of newspaper reporters to view 7 the flogging
of prisoners. The minute still works.
