The quaint opera re the man who wedded
a doll, which proved to be a live meat woman
ii Her Majesty’s at present to the door,
t he doll of Dorothy Vane is the principal cause of
ihis. Since Gilbert’s vein of humor ceased to
[lay crushing expenses there have been few new
operas of the lasting kind, and “La Poupee ” will
probably prove as fleeting as many others. But
while it lasts, and Dorothy Vane is the doll, there
is a great deal of joy to be extracted from it. She
is such a quaint and pretty piece of mechanism,
and she has brought the art of dolldom to such
high perfection, and she creaks so naturally
in her highly commendable joints, that “La
Poupee ” should run a good while for her
sake. The man who made the music for
the new opera isn’t absolutely a genius, and the
world will not make pilgrimages to his grave and
lay blossoms thereon, but he is quite good enough
lor all everyday purposes, and has a splash of real
ability here and there ; and when he is dead some
>ne else will doubtless fill his place just as well.
The new co. is largely the fine old co. over again,
and where fresh material has had to be put in it is
very good material on the whole. If only some
one will rise up now and write a few first-class
new operas, the Firm’s co. and its stage-manage-
ment are quite good enough to do their share of tne
work admirably. If nobody else appears the pre-
sent scribe may get angry enough to write a few
first-class operas himself.
** * *
The “ Fatal Card,” by Raddon Chambers
and co., is proceshing gorgeously through its
second week at Sydney Royal. It is so named
because the card isn’t fatal, though the absence of
it would be. The story all hangs on that rather
staggering document, and there is a boss mystery
about the “Card” which this paper has vainly
tried to clear up for a long time past. The hand-
some bad man has an unpleasantness in Colorado
and a committee urges him to climb up a tree by
his neck, when his old friend, the good man, in-
tervenes and gets him a chance to run for his life.
Then the bad man tears up a card and gives half
of it to the good man that they may know each
other when they meet again, and having done this
he leaves with the air of one who is pressed
for time. They meet again not long after
looking just, the same, but despite their pre-
vious acquaintance neither has the faintest
idea of the other’s identity. Everybody
else knows each other at sight, but these two have
to be in a state of baby-ignorance so that the card
may drop on them, at the critical moment, like an
unexpected grindstone in the eye. The bad man
is head of a co. which does a large business in
burglary a,nd manslaughter, and carefully stuns
the capitalist at his own residence. Large business-
interests lead this syndicate to tie up the good man
on a barrel of powder, with a slow-match appended.
Then, when they have all gone, except the hard
case from Colorado, who has been left behind to
canter gaily round the victim and scolf at him till
the last moment (when the syndicate has time, it
always scoffs at its victim before execution), the
card drops out, and he is saved. If the bad man
had forcibly undressed the good man at their first
meeting, and found the card on him, it would have
saved much anxiety to both parties.
* * * *
The lighter element of the story is supplied
by a lady who steals Bland Holt’s clothes on ac-
count of her unrequited affections, and leaves him
bathing and waiting for night to disguise his flight
home. The gallery loves this episode so much that
the “Card” might stay a good deal longer than
the two weeks allotted to it, if it happened to feel
that way. But it has to make room on Saturday
for “Sporting Life,” a new drama with certain
horse and other effects which are expected to strike
the public with great force in the pocket and else-
where. Two men who were partly responsible for
“White Heather” and “One of the Best” have
collaborated to produce “Sporting Life,” and the
property-man and scene-painter have both lost
their natural rest over it for some time, so good
results should follow.
** * *
That solid, business-like, old-fashioned
horror, “ Evidence,” has come out for two
weeks at Sydney Lyceum that the dust of its
sepulchre may blow off it, and the worm may have
a day out. Taking a comprehensive view of it,
“Queen’s Evidence” deserves an occasional resur-
rection. It is a pleasant old-time essay on the
boiler-plated indestructibleness of the Incriminat-
ing Document. In the ancient type of melodrama
the whole story hinges on the Incriminating Docu-
ment. If it can’t be found the good man must re-
main in gaol for life and be hanged frequently
in between times, and the whole physical force
of the villain is bent on its capture and des-
truction. In the course of years it is stolen and
recovered and stolen again. It is run away with
by dogs and rent by wild elephants ; the villain
planks it in the fire and goes away, and the funny
man dashes in and rescues it; children play with
it, and it is blown over dry wildernesses;
and the sea devours it and casts it up again
on the shore. And at last the hero, away at the
far end of nowhere, finds something blown into
his open mouth as he sleeps under the stars on a
dark night, and, when he spits it out, it proves to
be the Incriminating Document, as good as new.
“ Queen’s Evidence ” has got the Document bad.
Said Document is almost always a letter
written by the first villain to the second villain
before they quarrelled, and the foolishness of it
lies in the way the first villain goes into needless
particulars. It generally reads something like
this :
Dear Bluggins.— I murdered the old gentleman as per
arrangement, and buried the body in the back-yard. His
gold watch, which had his name on it, will be found in
the Mont de Pi6t6. The man there knows me, and will
be able to swear that I pawned it. I have hired Smith to
swear that John Jones committed the murder. We will
murder Smith afterwards so that he can’t give us away.
I left the old man’s clothes under the floor in my oid
lodgings. Two people were looking over the fence when I
buried the body and will probably remember the circum-
stance.
All the same, The Bulletin has a sort of love for
the grand old Incriminating Document, and will
be sorry to see it disappear out of dramatic
literature. And it mentions, with a kind of
regret that on the coming Saturday “ Under the
Southern Cross,” a new drama partly written by
Parson Walters, of “Joseph of Canaan,” will
be staged at the Lyceum. In “ Joseph of
Canaan ” there should have been a comic Egyptian
't ° !*, tb® document that would have cleared
Joseph’s character under a pyramid, and caused
Potiphar to remove the whole pyramid by night in
his efforts to find it. And. as there wasn’t, this
paper has only a moderate opinion of Parson
Walters.
** * *
A quantity of adjectives which The Bul-
letin was saving to discharge at Professor
Kennedy’s mesmeric show (now occupying Sydney
Criterion) unfortunately blazed up by a spon-
taneous-combustible process and were destroyed.
Probably the Professor wouldn’t have liked to
hear them, anyway. Kennedy lately assured his
audience that he wouldn’t think of using his
alleged power in any way calculated to degrade
humanity, and, having finished this remark, he
made one of his subjects swallow the loathsome
kerosene-and-salt mixture as usual. If he holds
that the public drinking of kerosene doesn’t de-
grade humanity, then the awful Professor has
either a very low opinion of humanity or a very
high opinion of kerosene. The shortest way to
get rid of the latter is to drink the stenchful oil
himself.
** * *
Biondi is still the boss wonder of Sydney
Tivoli, but there are serious doubts as to whether
the most wonderful part of his show is appreciated
as it deserves. His impersonations of celebrated
musicians, with his lightning changes from youth
to age, and his mysterious openings and shuttings
of himself—the musicians apparently vary so much
in size that one could swear Biondi opened and
closed as rapidly as a worn telescope—are the
cleverest thing in his multifarious entertainment.
But then Sydney doesn’t know these musicians,
and, however amazing may be the sudden change
from the person you never saw to the one you
hardly even heard of, this is a disadvantage. The
recent addition of General Booth to the collection
of characters is a decided improvement. Sydney
remembers at least one man of that name, and re-
calls an eager, red, burnished nose that used to
bob over tne plate, and a pair of famished eyes,
too closely set together, that took stock of the
takings. That nose used to rush at The Bulletin
in its dreams every night for many weeks.
** # *
R. S. Smythe’s tours have generally been
so long that his stars are not like sands on the
seashore for multitude, therefore it is remarkable
that two of them, both eminent violinists, died in
the same week. On the very day on which old
Horace Poussard was buried in Waverley Cemetery
(N.S.W.), Lady Halle was killed in the Alps by a
falling rock. _ The Much-Travelled personally had
little to do with the Halle tour, for it was managed
by Carlyle Smythe, who was quite equal to the
occasion. The titled instrumentalist being a friend
of the Princess of Wales, in the same way as
Albani was a friend of the Queen, occasionally put
on frills; and, on the first night of their second
season in the Sydney Town Hall, she declared she
would not play if a popular contralto, whom
Smythe had engaged to support her, appeared.
The son of the Much-Travelled assured her that,
if the concert had to be postponed, he would go to
the orchestra and announce the reason, and Neruda
Halle went on and played furiously. The Halid
concerts were the most successful ever given in
Sydney.
•* • *
Newbury, who starts a series of concerts
in Sydney next month, has secured Melb. Exhibi-
tion building for the Xmas holiday dates. With
good luck the big-little tenor will make a lot of
money in Australia, after all, as the result of his
wisdom in accepting an engagement from Rickards
a couple of years ago. Musical people called it a
come-down for a first-class tenor to warble in a
variety show, but it was at “ Rickards’ ” that New-
bury caught the ear of the nimble shilling crowd
and won a lucrative following. At the present
time he is about ten times better known to the
feneral public than any other concert tenor who
as ever travelled this country.
#* * *
At Melbourne Princess’s they had a pretty
well-crowded house for “The Prisoner of Zenda”
on Saturday, and the tale of the red-headed hero
whose great-great-grandmother went astray should
be duly appreciated for six nights only. Mrs. Maes-
more Morris is the present g.g.g. in the prologue,
and she strays in a young and graceful manner.
Also, she has a sufficient excuse for giddiness in
her husband (Lissant). owing to the serio-comic
deportment and utter objectionableness of that
ancient British identity. Nevertheless, the
effect of the “ Zenda ” prologue, despite its beauti-
ful stage set and the glory of Julius Knight’s lace
shirt, is always disappointing. It might be so much
better acted. The mounting of the play through-
out seems a little too good for the cast, to tell the
truth. Julius Knight is not quite the ideal Ras-
sendyll, although he gets nearer to excellence than
several other people who have fine, effective parts
to play. Miss Ferrar was a capital Princess
Fla via last year, but on Saturday she appeared to
be in ill-health ; her voice lacked the old ring of
enthusiasm, and there was no warmth to speak of
in the leading lady’s love for the wrong Rudolph.
If the play and the scenery were not first-
class, the faults in the performance would
pass unnoticed, but, seeing that “ Prisoner
of Zenda ” might have proved a brilliant
success in Australia, and didn’t, and considering
that playgoers, as a rule, are not inclined to ana-
lyse their impressions, the above remarks are
offered as a suggestion of what’s the matter with
this strong show. .It suffers from the want of
more strength in the principals. That’s all.

On Saturday next (24th) the Firm will
stage “Under the Red Robe,” which, being like-
wise a romantic play adapted from a well-known
novel, is calculated to boom. A lot of new
scenery and fittings “ regardless of expense ” will
give tone to the production.
** * *
The late Lady Halid, after hubby’s de-
cease, was unique in her ability to play, without
having the music in front of her, sundry tiresome
sonatas and things which nobody else troubled to
commit to memory. Some of the stuff that the
Halles played in Australia was of the purely
scholastic order. It had no real charms for even
the musical public, and to the general public these
heaviest of the Halid performances are quite
meaningless.
** * *
The droll pantomime-drama at Melbourne
Royal goes meandering on its way with a firm
determination to atone for the mistakes of its
youth. “ Round the World ” has learned a good
deal from the experience of four weeks and three
matinees, and if the management decides to hang
on a while longer to a moderate success the
receipts wall probably improve, rather than other-
wise. Meanwhile “Sons of the Sea,” a nautical,
spectacular, ballet-laden festival in four acts and
one huge tableau is ready for production at the
Royal. The days of the dead Rajah, and the fish-
like serpent, and the elephant which was built on
the premises, will be definitely numbered this
W'eek.

Rickards is to hoist his flag once again at
Melb. Bijou for a period of three weeks. This
time the prices will be wisely reduced to 35., 2s.
and Is, and the season will open with Pattie
Browne as “Jane.” Also the season should end
with “Jane,’’for that huggable young woman is
one of Pattie’s best characters, and the Bijou is
the only place in Melb. where farcical comedy fills
a long-felt want. Anywhere else it isn’t wanted
at all.
** * *
Peggy Pryde, with her strong-minded
brolly, and her grandmother whose two remaining
teeth are both opposite to one another (thank
Gawd!) is a reliable attraction at the Opera
House. Ada Reeve, on the other hand, is not re-
liable, for sometimes she takes place and some-
times she doesn’t. She is probably figuring on the
programme this week, and anyway the airy little
Ada can be counted upon as a certainty for next
Saturday afternoon, when she is to take a farewell
benefit. The Bulletin has never yet heard of
any actress being severely indisposed on such an
occasion.
** * *
Coghill’s variety co. appears to get along
comfortably at the Gaiety, and another co., of a
songful and various character, is making
Saturday efforts at St. George’s Hall. But,
for the present, Newbury’s “Pops” in the
Town Hall supply all the concert that Mel-
bourne wants on the last night of the week.
Miss Rose Musgrove and M‘Nicol Turner are
contributing a harmless necessary “sketch ” to the
Pop programmes, and J. Madden, the Adminis-
trator of the Govt., has given his patronage,
which amounts to a certif. of Respectability for the
show. Up to last week there had been a doubt in
some people’s minds as to whether any shilling
entertainment could be quite respectable, but
J. Madden’s patronage dispels the doubt.
At Poverty Point.
“ G. 8." : The death of veteran violinist Poussard recalls
his first visit to this part, in company with Douay. The
two artists, of whom Douay was the superior, made some
sensation. Fiddlers of excellence were not then common.
Miska Hauser, Hungarian player, raked in a fortune in
gold-rush times. To-day, it is probable he wouldn’t earn
hall-money—we have so much more culchah now. But,
anyhow, poor Douay, Poussard’s partner, went off his
head—the victim of an inherited decadence. It was a
pitiable case, which interested medical experts of the day,
but was not a topic one would choose to discuss in mixed
company. The poor wretch went home, and was confined
in a lunatic asylum in France, where he was often visited
by the late Empress, who had known Douay when he was
apparently hale, and pitied him when thus undone.
“ S.S.” : Peggy Pryde—a clever little woman, but with
more confidence than ability. Yet it may be that that
capacity for letting oneself go is ability. All successful
burlesque actresses and variety girls have it in a greater
or less degree, notably Alice Leamar, who, more than
any other, invites comparison with Peggy. In her own
Cockney line, the impertinent and vigorous Peggy is
almost deserving of all The Bulletin has said of her, but
she has the woman's insatiable desire to look nice, and
indulges it even though it gives her away, and makes
evident her lack of versatility. Many a good comedienne
has been spoilt by the woman in her, and Peggy, who is
only amusing as a plain plebeian, will persist in spoiling
impressions by trying to prove that she, too, can be “ aw-
fully naice ” when she likes.
Who is “Victor Dougal, of The Bulletin”?
When Geo. Rignold was playing in “ Romany Rye,”
once he made the extraordinary demand, “ f/Vicut these
cords!” Later on, as he was being carried up the
ricketty staircase by two well-known actors, one of whom
sometimes looked upon the beer when it was long, George,
eotto voce, accused the latter of being drunk. He was
quickly silenced with a remark, to which George’s own
avoirdupois lent considerable weight, “Now, you just
shut up—or we’ll let you drop like a load of bricks ! ”
Melbourne thusfar can’t easily believe that Peggy
Pryde— vide advt.—“ crowded Sydney Tivoli for 15
weeks.” They want to know whether the Poluskis, not
to mention George Wood and others, didn’t account for
some of the boom. The Poluskis were particular favorites
at Melb. Opera House during their not very long stay,
and will be welcomed back with a roar.
Westralian weeklies begin to speak in an airy, familiar
way of stage people and things. As, for instance : “ This
province knows a good thing when it sees it, and can pay
for it, too or “ Binks, Jinks, Spinks, and others well
known in W.A., are appearing at Sydney Tivoli.” In a
few months’ time they will probably mention that “ the
favorite Westralian actress, Maggie Moore, intends to
open at Melb. Royal shortly.”
Dear Bulletin.— Three photos, published in last issue—
Dorothy Vane, Carrie Moore, and Francie Adler—remind
one that the Australian operatic stage is suffering 1 from
another epidemic of “ small goods.” They are so diminu-
tive and fleeting, these ladies who dance and sing to us
nowadays. Like Flora Graupner and Violet Varley of
yore, they haven’t come to stay. In the struggle for popu-
larity a charmer of the Nellie Stewart stamp can begin
ten years earlier and be still holding her own ten years
after “a little bit of a thing” is forgotten.— Seasoned.
The Potter-Bellew firm seems to have been dissolved,
in a managerial sense. Mrs. Cora P. is booked for a part
in Beerbohm Tree’s new version of “ The Three Muske-
teers,” and Bellew has accepted an engagement at another
London house.
Charles Arnold told an interviewer lately that he is
under contract to return again to Australia, but owing to
the success of his new piece, “ What Happened to
Jones,” he will endeavor to postpone his tour. Any
Australian contract that Arnold may have in hand just
now is not likely to be very binding, one would think.
It would be interesting to know how much George R.
Sims has made in author’s fees out of “ The Harbor
Lights.” Eight years ago, when the Ibsen boom was on
in London, Sims acknowledged in the Referee that the
play had brought him in over £30,000, and doubted
whether Ibsen’s whole dramatic writings had earned him
that sum oranything like it. And “ The Harbor Lights ”
has been running in various parts of the world constantly
ever since, and has only just, finished a fresh run of about
a month to big business in Melbourne.
Mario Majeroni is coming back to Australia with the
Broughs—a 12 months’ engagement. His brother George
stays on at the Princess’s, and will play “Clou,” the
dumb man, in “ Under the Red Robe.”
At a recent bakblox performance, a local official enacted
a policeman whose sole biz was to rush in, arrest a man,
partly draw a gin-bottle from his tunic, and say “ See,
here is the staff of justice!” then, sot.to voce, “No—
that’s the gin-bottle.” He rehearsed nightly for a whole
week, and when his cue was given he bounded on,
scruffed his man, pulled the bottle out, flourished it round
his head and yelled, “ See, this is the gin-bottle ! ”
Clever little Ada Reeve and Bert Gilbert, unquestion-
ably the best burlesque pair Australia has had since the
days of Fred Leslie'and Nellie Farren, are announced to
return to London a week from date, but with so mer-
curial a couple it is always hard to even suspect their
movements an hour ahead. Up to the time of our going
to press, however, they were living happy ever after.
“H.”: I saw the performance of mesmeric Madame
Sibley, in Melb. one night. After at] amazing show by a
young fellotv, who sang comic songs under the “in-
floonz,” mewed as a cat, fancied he was going to be
hanged, and allowed himself to be made a pincushion of,
a gentleman got up and asked, “ Did I not see this young
man go through the very same performance in England ?”
Madame : “ And pray what of that, if it be so ? Is it not
the more wonderful ?” Gentleman : “But .” (Boohoo !
Shame! Sit down I) An audience is as illogical as an
editor, with the last word.
Melba is always reported by returned globe-trotting
Naice people, from Melb. and Sydney, to be extremely
snortful and haughty with them. But, to fellow-artists,
she shows the bright side of her character. Miss Ridley,
a Melb. musician, who studied her art in tuneful Germany,
tells how, when in London, she called on the prima-donna
at the Savoy, and Melba besought her to come again, and
plied her with questions about “ dear old Melbourne,”
played accompaniments while Miss R. sang to her, warmly
praised her voice, and gave her a letter to Marchesi.
Phil Langdale, the bassoonist, has been all through
Canada with Dan Godfrey’s band, and was, at latest
dates, blowing his weird, peculiar instrument in New
York. Old friend Phil seems to have been the most
popular item of the programme. It fell to his lot to lash
the Yanks into patriotic fury with “ Yankee Doodle,”
arranged (by himself) as a bassoon solo.
Taney’s American book on training learned elephants,
dogs, horses, pigs, shows that it is all done with the eye.
The goose is one of the most intelligent birds.
Little Foley Westwood, the eleven-year-old boy violin-
ist, who gave a quite artistic performance at one of New-
bury’s “Pop” (Melb.) concerts, the other Saturday, is,
sad to say, in danger of losing his eyesight.
“ Pro.”: Harry Sefton visited Australia one timp,
and many can judge that he was not in it with
glorious little John Coleman. He is not to be confounded
with Harry Sefton, who was one of the unfortunate
demised Deacon Skinners, the others being O’Brien and
Redwood, after which the plague stayed.
“ Tell ” : Several tenors recently here have made
liberal use of the falsetto, and they excuse themselves by
saying that everybody in London does the same. Yet the
change from chest or head note to virtual soprano is most
inartistic, and vocalistic visitors to Australia should
not forget that their audiences are generally composed
almost entirely of people who understand the difference
between pure singing and “fake.”
A bill of the “ Octoroon ” is posted up in the dressing-
room of a certain M.L. theatre. It relates to an amateur
performance, and states that the “ Octoroon ” is a “ five
act drama, written by and Dion Boueicault; the
fifth act being the sole production of our local amateur,
.” A professional co. came along and played the
Zoedrama, but the audience lingered after the fall of the
baize, fancying that the local man’s fifth act was still to
come. The manager went on : “I hear you are waiting
for the fifth act, ladies and gentlemen, but, I’m sorry to
say, there ain’t none. Boueicault only wrote four acts;
but when he writes another I shall be happy to play it—
unfortunately Boueicault is dead at present. Had he
‘ived to see the local fifth act, Mr. would prob-
ably be dead also. Under those circs., we’ll have to let
her slide at the fourth act.” And she slid.
A few years ago, in Sydney, an actor, who has since
attained celebrity, made his first appearance in “ Henry
V.,” and was entrusted with the important line, “I hope
they will not come upon us now,” but, instead of speaking
the same in an appropriately subdued tone, he thundered
out the line as if he wanted to lift the roof off, utterly
wrecking the quiet spirit of the scene, with its sacred
musical accompaniment. Whereupon, the only Henry V.:
“You thief! We’re in God’s hands, brother, not in
theirs!’” the preliminary amenity being almost as
audible as the “ book.”
“ Rick ” : Re Billy Hoskins and the super (8., 10 , 9/’9S).
Heard of a panto, super who —when shot up through a
trap—had to say, in answer to “ Who art thou?” “I’m
but a wretched gnome.” But, unfortunately, on the first
night they shot him up too suddenly, and so demoralised
him that he forgot his words and answered—under the
painful necessity of saying something, “ I’m only a blanky
dwarf! ” The prince who gave the cue promptly told
him to “Get hence, thou evil spirit!” and boosted him
into the wings, where, after several more boosts from the
stage-manager, he reached the treasurer’s box, was paid
and fired !
Dear Bulletin. — Re latter-day Thespian’s complaint
that the decadence of taste prevents anything going
down with the public but legs. Mark Twain says of
the old masters that the whole lot together only
painted one picture of any historical consequence—The
Rape of the Sabines—and that subject was chosen for the
sake of the legs and the busts.— Cherub.

Ed. Bulletin. — Glad to see you direct attention
to the matter of creamery-managers’ wages. I have beet;
connected with the dairying business for 14 years, ann
know what I am talking about. Why, the wages you
quote in your issue 10/9/98 are real good compared with
what are paid in some Q. districts. 1 know half-a-dozen
places where men have taken charge of creameries—find
fuel (i.e., wood) and water, and cart cream to railway
station (averaging in distance from 4 to 13 miles) once ;<
day, and glad to get the job at from 225. 6d. to 325. 6d
per week! What a farce is the Factory Act! In a town,
the boilers used in factories have to be inspected ; and in
town it is far more likely that a competent man is etn
ployed, and that the boilers will be in good order.- The
country is the place where inspection of boilers ought to
be insisted upon. Fancy a man who has watched a
brother creamery-man run his plant for a week applying
for the charge of a creamery where a boiler is used. All
he knows, may be, is that he has got to keep up 601 b. ot
steam to run his plant; as for practical knowledge oi
steam, he has none. I know several places where I, as a
mechanic, would not put 101 b. of steam on a boiler, anu
yet they run them cheerfully at 501 b
Apropos the above. The representative of a big
machinery firm sold a threshing plant to a co. consisting
of German and English farmers, and as soon as the plant
was delivered at the owners’ place be had to put it under
steam. He naturally filled the boiler (portable engine)
from the plug in top of boilei, and when sufficient water
was in put the plug in again and got up steam. As soon
as sufficient pressure was on the boiler he started the
plant, and, as he wanted to catch a train, he left when the
first wheat came through. As soon as one of the owners
noticed that there was hardly any water in the water-
gauge glass he conferred with his fellow-owners as to what
was to be done, whereupon one observant genius said,
“ Oh, I saw do it, and we can easily put water in
the way he did.” Then, taking up a monkey-wrench, he
started to unscrew the plug on top of the boiler. For-
tunately there was only about 101 b. of steam on, because,
when he had got the plug three-quarters of the way out,
away it went, wrench and all; and for the next twenty-
minutes all you could see was several frightened farmers'
faces looking out at the engine from the neighboring hay-
stacks. Next day the firm got a wire : “ Boiler burst—
send a man up.” Solemn fact.

A Vic. miner writes from Klondike :—“There
is a lot of wild talk about the cold in these regions, but I
have never found it as bad as a tough winter on the
Dargo High Plains, in Gippsland. Not one-fifth of the
claims here are on paydirt, and the gold is the poorest I
have struck, realising only from £2 10s. to £2 135.”
■* * * •
After Sydney G.P.O. had, by a stupid blunder,
given the demonetised stamps another year’s lease, and
after having—before perceiving said blunder—wrongfully
taxed hundreds of letters bearing superseded stamps, it
is now graciously pleased to announce that, “on account
of numbers of old stamps being still in the hands of the
public, their validity has been extended for prepayment
of postage for the present.” The present! The Post
Office’s own Guide of July says stamps are good to June
next — i.e., June, ’99.
* * * *
Sydney G.P.O.’s implication that it has power
to repudiate old issues of its own stamps is ridiculous.
Does the law that authorises the postage-stamp contem-
plate the demonetisation of that stamp? The courts
would be very loth to read so immoral a power into the
Act. Strangely enough the public very mildly submits
to such injustice. Fourpenny-bits circulate very little
in Australia now—and the idea is that by some order
they have been reduced in value to threepence. The
banks never give more for them. Nevertheless, the groat
is still a legal tender for fourpence.
* • • *
The 10-head battery at the Beacon Gold Mine,
Bucca Creek, N.S.W., ran continuously for 13 months
without a stop, and has now put through 10,500 tons ore,
and only had one short stoppage for overhaul. This bat-
tery was manufactured and erected by the Austral Otis
Engineering co., of South Melbourne, who are well-
known makers of all kinds of mining and other machin-
ery. They have lately purchased the plant and
stock of the long - established firm of Langlands
Foundry co., now in liquidation, and, as they
have all the patterns, can supply duplicate parts of any
machines made by Langlands. *

“ Yarrum ” : Know an old woman who runs a
N.S.W. Post-office for which she receives (or should re-
ceive) £lO 10s. per annum ; but, for present quarter, she
is being paid short 3s. for “ bond premium.” Now, as no
moneys pass through her hands, and as the few stamps
she keeps are her own property, why should she have to
find “ bond premium ? ” Twelve shillings a year is a con-
sideration to a struggling old woman.

Sydney D.T’s London correspondent remarks
that Calvert’s and other boomsters ill-luck “ has never
touched Mr. D’Arcy, the Mt. Morgan millionaire who
recently entertained the Lord Chancellor, and half the
peerage, and had Melba there to sing.” How pleased
D’Arcy must be to be thus associated ! He is not a
boomster, and never was, or could he one. He made his
money out of a solid mine, not in a boom-rigged market.
Nine men out of ten who make big money rapidly in the
sunshine of a boom lose it as fast in the frost which surely
follows. D’Arcy could only lose his by absolutely throw-
ing it away, and he would have to keep on throwing or it
would accumulate again in great heaps. Perhaps not
generally known that some three or four years ago he
invested heavily in some of the biggest Rand mines, and,
*tis said, made as much money there as out of Mt. Morgan.

The Bulletin
Lord Kilmorey recently showed his bond-fides
as regards his big S.A. outer-harbor scheme by depositing
£25,000 earnest with the Treasury. Outside the solid
Labor vote, Kilmorey’s greatest opposition comes from
capitalists interested in Port Adelaide. Moreover, he has
tempted fate by introducing the Bill in the Leg. Council,
risking the chance of its being shelved in the Assembly at
the fag-end of the session.
* • • «
Last session of Tas. Parliament, Hobart Gas Co.
(which is like all other gas co.’s and that is saying a lot)
brought in a Bill to enable it to make and supply electric
light. An amendment was made in the House that over
6 per cent, profit the co. should share any surplus takings
with the Hobart corporation, in return for the use of
its streets. Everyone expected that “6 percent.” meant
“perannum,” but when the Bill was passed it was found
that the co. meant per six months, when it made up its
books. Mulcahy, M.P., tried the other day to correct
the mistake, but the Speaker gave his casting vote towards
confirming the blunder.
• * • •
The Land Mortgage Bank of Victoria reports,
with a certain justifiable pride, that it got through the
half-year ended 30th June, 1898, with an increase in its
accumulated loss of only £1673. The loss on the ordinary
business was really £10,915, but £9242 was realised by the
redemption of £46,357 of the co.’s debentures at a fraction
over £85 —in other words, that amount was made by pay-
ing the bank’s creditors only 17s. in the £. Still, it is
obvious that if the Land Mortgage Bank can raise enough
money to repeat this operation regularly, and its credit
only keeps bad enough so that it can get its debentures
back cheaply, it may make sufficient by this device to
greatly ease its downfall. The losses on the bank’s ordi-
nary business and on sales of property for the last eight
half-years have been :
December, ’94, Loss.. £6038
June, ’95 ~ 6617
December, ’95 ~ 8847
June, ’96 „ 9696
December, ’96, Loss £10,494
June, ’97 „ 14,425
December, ’97 ~ 8508
June, ’9B „ 10,915
Against this the redemption of debentures at a discount
yielded £25,706 for the second half of ’97 and £9242 for the
first half of ’9B.
The best feature about; the Land Mortgage
Bank is that, in the last twelve months, it has succeeded,
out of the proceeds of calls, in reducing its debenture
liability from £900,000 to £736,738. If a reasonable pro-
portion of the still unpaid calls can be collected a
further reduction to £600,000 seems possible, but
that appears to be about all the prospects at
present in view. The bank is still confronted by these
troubles : It has to pay 3j per cent, on its debentures
rising again, at the end of 1901 (unless further conces-
sions can be obtained) to 4J per cent., and after paying
working expenses it earned last half-year about 2£ per
cent. After allowing for losses on property sales it earned
not much over 10s. per cent. The loss, too, seems to be
getting worse instead of better. The liabilities,
apart from those to shareholders, are £751,978, and
the paper assets are put down at £1,076,768, but
over a million are mortgages and real estate
reckoned at the old boom figures this paper valuation
is very little satisfaction. Last half-year the Land Mort-
gage Bank’s investments produced a gross return of about
£2 4s. per cent, per annum on their alleged value, so their
present selling value is probably somewhere between
£500,000 and £600,000, and just now it seems that
the Land Mortgage Bank is in a very tight place
indeed. A revival in property, if there ever is such a
thing, and some kind of luck in the next re-arrangement
(in 1901) with the hard-hearted debenture-holders, may
give it a chance to revive. But the most conspicuous
feature at present is that the interest burden is £7OOO less
than it was at December, ’96, and depreciation seems to
have swallowed up all the saving.
* * * *
The Australian Deposit and Mortgage Bank
(Melbourne) shows a profit of £1207 for the half-year
ended 30th June, 1898. Said profit equals between ss,
and 6s. per cent, per annum on the paid-up capital, which
certainly isn’t much. And, as the gross earnings (£10,655)
only equal a fraction over 1 \ per cent, per annum on the
alleged paper value (over £1,400,000) of the hank’s invest-
ments, it is impossible to avoid the assumption that the
said investments are an even worse lot than those of that
luckless brother institution, the Land Mortgage Bank of
Victoria. If it is assumed that mortgages and other pro-
perties should yield even so small a gross return
as 3 per cent, on their real value, then the alleged
'£1,400,000 of properties are just now worth not much
over £700,000. If they should yield 4 per cent, gross, then
they are not really worth much over £500,000. But luck
and’ other things have stood by the Australian Deposit and
Mortgage concern. It obtained a re-arrangement by
which 70 per cent, of its deposits were renewed for 15 years
at 3J per cent., and the other 30 per cent, were repayable
by instalments extending up to January, 1901, without
interest. And its load of deposits is comparatively small,
while its capital is large, therefore it can pay its way and
show a small profit, and hang on to its assets while waiting
for better times. It has already managed to accumulate
enough cash to pay the 1899 instalment of its deposits,
without making any sacrifice of its properties. The slow
but steady improvement of things since the last recon-
struction is shown by the profits
June, 1896 .. £69
December, 1896 .. 978
June, 1897 .. 911
December, 1897 .. £lO5l
June, 1898 .. .. 1207
During the half-year the Australian Deposit
and Mortgage Bank has reduced its deposit liabilities by
£20,000. With the slow reduction of liabilities and the
very, very gradual accumulation of profits, and the fact
that everything comes to the bank that can wait, the old
losses should be all wiped off some day and then the
A.D. and M. can make a fresh start. At present, bad as
things are with it, it is about the luckiest of the few land
banks which still survive.
V * * *
The West Australian Bank still stands high as
a profit-earning concern. The half-year ended 30th June,
’9B, produced £18,542, equal to £l3 9s. Bd. per cent, per
annum on the shareholders' funds (capital, £lOO,OOO ; re-
serve, £175,000). Some recent profits of this institution :
June, ’94 .. £8,387
December, ’94 .. 9,623
June, ’95 .. 10,315
December, ’95 .. 10,972
June, ’96 .. 12,284
December, ’96 .. £16,703
June,’97 .. 19,685
December, '97 .. 18,251
June, ’9B .. 18,542
The profits, which showed a slight tendency downwards
at the end of ’97, have again risen a little. The West Aus-
tralian Bank has a cash reserve of £663,927, equal to
nearly 40 per cent, of all liabilities to the public, which is
one of the biggest cash reserves in Australia- Its reserve
fund, in proportion to its capital, is the biggest in Aus-
tralasia, being If times the amount paid up by
the shareholders. (The Commercial Bank of Tas-
mania holds second place in this respect). And
it pays the biggest banking dividend in Australasia
(17£ per cent., now paid for the ninth year in succession),
yet this only' absorbs £8750, and leaves £9792 to be added
to the profit and loss balance. If the W.A. Bank liked to
spend all its earnings in dividends it could, according to
its balance-sheet, have paid 36 per cent., but it wisely
sticks to the old figure, and, considering the great chances
of a fall in profits, it is wise to do so. The reasons of
this unusual prosperity appear to be : (1) the boom ; (2)
the fact that the business is large in proportion to the
capital; (3) the comparatively high interest rates hitherto
obtained in the West; and.(4) the enormous proportion of
deposits (about two-thirds) which bear no interest.
Still, before placing absolute reliance on all this
glory The Bulletin would like to hear of a vigorous State
audit, by independent experts brought for the purpose
from N.S.W. or Victoria, or Maoriland, of the West Aus-
tralian Bank. For that quaint, old-world institution
never publishes an auditor’s certificate. So far as is
known it is never formally audited at all—this paper
believes that it has never been formally audited in all
the 57 years of its honored existence. And though no one
seems to have heard of any breath of suspicion on the
eminently prosperous-looking W.A. Bank, still a scientific
overhaul might furnish a job for some deserving person.
• • • *
The nature of the ghost which the old private
management of the Bank of New Zealand left behind it is
shown by the annual report of the Assets Realisation
Board just published. When the Government came to
the rescue of the B.N.Z. it set aside the great mass
of dead assets—about (allegedly) £2,731,000 worth of
properties (more or less bad), and placed them in charge
of a Board for realisation. The alleged £2,731,000 worth
of properties were really only worth, on a careful valuation
then made, £1,879,000, but, by way of easing the shock,
the Board issued £2,680,000 in 3J per cent, debentures
in payment for the properties, the interest thereon being
about £93,000 a year. And, by way of a counter-
balance, the Bank agreed to pay £50,000 a year out of its
profits, if available, to help the Board to make up losses.
The result of this complicated arrangement is that the
Board gets the assets at £1 13s. per cent, per annum
on the paper-value at which it took them over. And, ap-
parently, it can’t make that amount out of them. Last
year it had a shortage of £6629, and it also seems
to have lost £55,924 on sales of property which
didn’t fetch the inflated book-values. So far, £469,000
worth of properties sold seem to have realised
£140,000 less than their book values, and already there
appears to be an accumulated deficit in the assets of
over £200,000. At this rate, unless prices go up,
there should be an awful shortage at the end of the
liquidation, and the Assets Realisation Board, unless it
gets assistance from somewhere (it is difficult to say
where) won’t be able to pay anything like the nominal
value of the debentures it gave in payment for its pro-
perties. And as these debentures are reckoned as assets
by the holders such a contingency must needs be exceed-
ingly unpleasant for somebody. It is a somewhat tangled
situation all round.
* * * *
The big Modern Permanent Building Society
(Melbourne) reports that it has got through another year
(to 31st July, 1898) with a loss of only £10,348. Of this,
£8677 arises through sale of properties below their old
boom-time book value —the Modern had a re-valuation of
its properties some time ago, but it didn’t write them
down to that value, possibly because the figures were too
shocking, and it was considered easier and pleasanter to
wait and hope for a revival. The rest of the loss is caused
by the general inability to make ends meet. The
Modern’s progress (mostly backwards) for the last seven
years has been this :
July, 1892—Profit, £16,752
„ 1893 „ 51
„ 1894—Loss, 13,800
„ 1895 „ 2,630
July, 1896 —Loss, £11,431
„ 1897 „ 7,511
„ 1898 „ 10,348
The reserve fund, which, in 1892, stood at £70,000, is now
down to £14,831; so within another two years, at the pre-
sent rate, the society will begin to eat large holes in
its capital. Last year the Modern sold _ £43,631
worth of property, and the loss on this com-
paratively small transaction was £8677. If the rest
of the houses and lands are similarly over-valued there is
another loss of about £70,000 or £BO,OOO to face before the
Society gets anything like right again. Whether it will
be right then or not is an open question. At present it
has a nominal capital of £199,000, and it is staggering
under an awful load of over £422,000 deposits and deben-
tures. It has had the use of this money for several years
now at the very cheap reconstructed rate of 2f per
cent., and this concession has just been renewed
indefinitely. A co. which gets the use of so vast a
sum at that low figure should be able to pay working
expenses even if it is considerably dry-rotted, but the
Modern fails to do so. Still, it has a patient crowd of
creditors who have agreed to wait till 1902 before they
start discussing the painful question of repaymt it, and
the wooden tenement'and the mud allotment may be
more saleable by then.
f * * *
Scone (N.S.W.) parish, over which Archdeacon
Coles Child presided in 1864, made a thrifty deal for the
future. The parochial council of that dace insured Mr.
Child’s life for £lOOO with the object of permanently en-
dowing his successors. For this purpose they valiantly
paid a yearly premium of £39 3s. for a period of 20 years.
The trustees were by that time all dead, but Mr. Child
lived on. In ’B6 the parish found its original thrift-energy
exhausted and kicked against paying further premiums.
The accumulated bonuses, however, kept the policy alive,
and when Mr. Child died the other day £1350 Is. sd. was
discovered at the credit of the old Scone parish. The
object of the dead of 34 years ago has therefore been
attained.
• * * *
“ Ess ” : The most puzzling goldfield in Aus-
tralia is Tibooburra, N.S.W. The color can be got in the
street, in back-yard3—anywhere. Gold all over the
locality—mostly washed, a great deal rough—yet it’s im-
possible to guess where it came from, being, as it is, in
“ made ” ground. Round the town are cone-shaped piles
of gibbers and rugged hills of sandstone, broken slate and
granite. Some think the whole has been erupted ; others,
that it was the centre of rapid whirlpools in pre-historic
times. After a shower of rain men, women and children
go out “ speckin’,” and pick up bright specks all over the
place. There must be a big reef close by ; and some day
will see those desolate hills alive with miners. Just now
they come by fits and starts, chase a will o’ the wisp for
a few days and peter away as they came. As for the
fossils who’ve lived there for years, they’re tethered to
within cooee of the pubs.
* • • *
Dear Bulletin. —The boom Mareeba-Chillagoe
railway syndicate offers pick-and-shovel-men, &c., 7s.
a-day, and 9d. a yard for side-cutting (piecework).
Latter means picking, shovelling, and carting the stuff
away in wheelbarrows ! Considering the exorbitant prices
of rations here—to say nothing of climate—7s. a-day
hardly equals 4s. a-day in the South ! As for the piece-
work, the best navvies we have consider it impossible to
make much more than tucker at it, even at 10 hours
a-day! Yet the Hughenden-Winton railway-contractors
are advertising in Townsville, Cairns, &c., for navvies at
Bs. a-day 1 Every boat from the South unloads an exten-
sive assortment of navvies, spielers, axemen, miners,
platelayers, carpenters, &c. Pitiful to see the number of
married men who’vcl>roken-up homes in the South, and
are stranded here with wives and children ! The worst
of it, there’s no back-country here, and to look for work
in the surrounding mining districts is absolutely useless.
Most mines at Herberton, Girofla, Hodgkinson, Chilla-
goe, &c., are idle, under six months’ exemption —to allow
“ scrip-gaffing.” S.S. companies, and Cairns, and Mareeba
are having a bit of a spree just now, but what kind of a
recovery will they suffer in the morning I —6 xB.

Ed. Bulletin. — Randolph Bedford seems to
divide his time pretty equally between writing resplendent
poetry and booming pars —being, as a highly imaginative
young man, eminently well fitted for both occupations.
But when he attacks others as boomsters, really he draws
public attention too openly to his own weakness. The
Chillagoe Prop, directors should suppress the Reckless for
the sake of the common weal. He curses me for speaking
about Chillagoe without sufficient personal knowledge,
and then proceeds to explain all about Keelbottom, a
place he had never heard of in his life till he met one of
the owners in Townsville and was utterly flattened out
with a glimpse of some local specimens. Just to rectify a
few of his misconceptions : In Keelbottom lodes have been
proved for a distance of five miles ; a lot of prospecting
has been done. Deepest shaft 150 feet, with good lode in
sink ; another 80 feet with 11 feet lode, nearly half native
copper and good gold. Anybody can ascertain that.
Reports of prominent Southern and English experts call
the Keelbottom formation igneous (no filter stains), true
fissure lodes, and predict a great future. As to my per-
sonal interestedness in Keelbottom and consequently
biassed opinion—what is wrong with the beam in Mr.
Bedford’s eye, anyhow? Yours, etc.—S.V.K.
♦ * * *
Editor Bulletin. —So much has been written
as to the relative richness of Chillagoe, Keelbottom, and
other copper centres, that one would think copper only
existed in places virtually inaccessible. Such, however,
is not the case, for we have within a couple of hundred
miles of Brisbane, in a district seamed with railways, num-
bers of copper-lodes, the whole of which carry the noble
metals also. For instance, the New Moonta, about 20
miles from Mt. Perry, in the early Seventies turned out
from 300 to 400 tons of ore averaging 22 per cent, copper,
and a parcel of 23 tons taken out recently yielded up to
21 per cent., beside 14dwt. gold, and 260 z. silver per ton.
Can any of the Chillagoe mines show any such returns as
these ? Another, the Miva, shows enormous siliceous
iron-capped outcrops, in one place over 130 ft. wide stand-
ing out of the ground several feet. From a shaft sunk
near this assays showed 31 per cent, copper, 4oz. gold;
and a small parcel, about 4 or 5 tons, yielded 14 per cent,
copper, loz. 16dwt. gold, 15Joz. silver per ton. Another,
the King Bomba, has four lodes, one of them 30ft. wide,
from which rich assays have been obtained; and there
are many others lying idle for want of capital. “ Distance
lends enchantment to the view.” —Queenslander.

“The Copper-headed Juggins ” : Leave Chilla-
goe fields to do the talking—that’s a very good suggestion.
The silence of Randolph B. will supply a long-felt want.
For my own part, I never professed to know aught about
the Chillagoe field, but I think I knew something about
the Chillagoe “ boom ” when I opened the attack. At
that time, the original Chillagoe shares were quoted at
£lBOO. Now they are—well, what are they? £2 shares
in the new Proprietary co. have dropped to 16s. 6d., any-
how. The market has been talking for some time, and
its conversation is more eloquent than R.B.’s “ ravings.”

Croydon items : Croydon Consols management
is spending a good round sum of J. Bull’s money in putting
pumping machinery on the mine. Work will be resumed
in deep shaft when the machinery is erected —probably in
December. At latest No. 5 and 6 N. Golden Gate were
raising 12oz. stone. Waratah co.’s Tabletop low-grade
ore quarries reported to give profit of almost 7s. per ton
by a fern-head experiment! Late crushings : Diamond
Jubilee, 75 tons, 2730 z. ; 3 and 4 S. Golden Gate, 60 tons
for 2oz. 14dwt. per ton ; Homeward Bound No. 3 North,
45 tons, 920 z.
* * • *
Croydon August returns: Mills, 2555 tons,
24450 z. ; cyanide, 1015 tons, 7890 z. As Rogers’ No. 1
Golden Gate only cleans up quarterly, its results are not
included in these figures.
* * » •
If water holds out (unlikely) the end of the year
should be a record for Croydon. Present month’s results
will be increased by a 500 ton crushing for No. 7 N.
Golden Gate, estimated to yield a 5000 oz. cake for exhibi-
tion in London. Many lines are showing increased pro-
spects of payableness. Reported that several big
Bull co.’s are now taking preliminary steps towards amal-
gamation, with a view to more systematic and energetic
prospecting and development.
So far every month of ’9B in Queensland has
shown an increase on the corresponding period of ’97. The
total for Aug. was 81,5820 z.— an increase of 18,4690 z. over
the output for Aug., ’97—figures highly satisfactory to all
concerned. First six months of this year are in advance
of corresponding division of last year by 18 per cent.
Towers’ increase, however, is principally due to cyaniding
operations. Gympie shows up splendidly again.

Melb. Metrop. Board of Works has carried a
resolution in the teeth of Boss Fitz Gibbon, Autocrat of
the Sewers, binding the board to the use of Vic. coal for
the future. Only "five members beside Fitz. were found
to vote against the proposal.
Not many Westralian or other mines can show
a better result than this of the Peak Hill, Murchison dis-
trict : 6890 tons crushed for 31,9220 z. The tonnage may
be slightly more ; some earlier records were kept imper-
fectly. The last crushing was a parcel of 860 tons, from
which an average of 3oz. 2fdwt. was got. Present output
passes IOOOoz. a month, and it is estimated that there is
well on to 18 months’ stone showing. Mostly English, of
course.

General manager Kohler, of the new Fremantle
smelting-works—an interested party, no doubt—reckons
that, with the railway facilities offering, goldfields stuff
running 1)4 0Z - over can best be treated away from
the fields. The Kanowna pug he pronounces suitable for
smelting. The plan for the immediate future is to use
Broken Hill concentrates as lead flux ; but the co. has
got hold of some of the Northampton lead mines—worked
years ago by Mitchell, now M.P., and looks to them for
most of that class of material by-and-bye. In the old days
the Northampton lead was sent to Europe as wool-ship
ballast.

Kalgoorlie wire : “ Rumored that a big find of
telluride and free gold has been made in either the Star
of the West or Mt. Charlotte North, adjoining the Golden
Zone and Reefer’s Eureka. The managers, while admit-
ting that both mines have a nice-looking sulphide lode,
deny the presence of telluride, or very rich ore.” Now,
what does this mean? Why publish a rumor at the same
time as the managers’ denial ? This looks like a new way
of getting long-wearied shareholders to stump up for un-
paid calls. “ A nice-looking sulphide lode ” ! Was ever a
nasty-looking sulphide lode heard of ?
• * * •
Hutchison, a S.A. Labor M.P., is the author of
a common-sense amending Companies Bill now before
S.A. Assembly. He proposes the appointment of a Govt.
Inspector to examine the books and affairs of every re-
gistered co., and also wants to compel every mining co.
to file a statement showing nominal capital, names
and addresses of shareholders and number of shares ht'a
by each. Also the Bill requires the furnishing of particu-
lars as to prices paid to vendors, and the periodical pub-
lication of balance-sheets. The Bill would hit wild-cu n
boomsters pretty hard, particularly in Adelaide, whies
has fathered many mining swindles.

Harvest prospects are so good in S.A. that tin*
railway commissioner has given the Barrier silver-minin»
co.’s notice that he probably will not be able to answer
their truck demands promptly between November am
March. To which the co’s reply that, seeing they hav*
kept S.A. out of the muck through many bad season-.,
it is hard lines that they should be made to suffer as soon
as ever a good season comes along. Wanted, federal rail
way-control and uniform railway gauge. Then some 01
the spare rolling-stock of N.S.W., which is abundant! >
supplied, could be shunted round S.A. way to fill the
temporary void.

Standing by an open grave in a big ceme-
tery near Sydney, the other day, the clergyman
refused to read the prayers until his fee (7s. fid.)
was paid. The husband of the dead happened to
have the amount in his pocket, or —well, it’s not
clear what might not have happened to the woman
in the coffin.

They talk, talk, talk in Adelaide of a
public-private hospital in opposition to the King-
ston institution. One sawbones offers £ 100 for
five years upon many conditions; and an “ex-
matron ” sends along her little offer of 12 months’
gratis services—though a year’s board-and-lodgiug,
with a leg-in for a possible life-billet, . may be
worth something these times. At the bottom of
all the real desire there is for a new hospital is the
feeling that South Australia ought to have a
medical-school again.

A furious resident of Exhibition-street,
Melb., writes to the Argus, demanding to know
why it has freshly been labelled as “Stephen.”
The authorities seemingly do not believe that a
rose by any other name will smell as sweet. But
this is a loyal outburst. It was Stephen in the
Duke of Edinburgh’s time.

* * *
Force of habit works
peculiarly. A fireman’s
wife, in Sydney, was dan-
gerously ill last week, and
had grown so accustomed
to the clanging fire-alarm in her room that it was
not thought necessary to disconnect it. Yet her
husband hears it in his sleep and is always in the
street fully dressed before it stops ringing.
» * * *
N.S. Wales letter-carriers never act as
news-distributors re deceased persons on their
different beats. Correspondence addressed to
persons who have died is simply endorsed by the
letter-carrier, “Left,” and in due time returned
to sender per Dead Letter office. Unless a red-
coat actually secs a person dead he is not allowed by
the department to mark “Deceased” on the cor-
respondence of a defunct. Letters from “ Home ”
to dead relatives out here are regularly returned
endorsed “Addressee left,” although “addressee”
may have, within general public knowledge, been
deceased for years. If a letter arrived addressed
to Capt. Cook, the N.S.W. letter-carrier would
simply endorse it with the statement that Capt. C.
had “Left.”

A Brisbane (Q.) lawyer expressed himself
willing to take on a young lady typist and steno-
grapher-certificated in both arts. “ What
wages?” asked her intermediary. “7s. Gd. a
week.” “ But she can’t live on that.’’ “Well,
all I can say is that I can get 20, 30, or 50 cap-
able girls on those terms.” “ What sort of girls ? ”
Oh, principally daughters of civil-servants,
traders, and billeted parents gen ‘rally, who keep
them in clothes, p ovide them h good homes,
and let them use their salaries as pocket-money.”
Always the well-to-do who elbow-out the needy.
A poor woman’s worst enemy is her more comfort-
ably-placed sister.
* * * *
Recently-deceased John O’Brien, of Port
Melbourne (Vic.), provided by will for the con-
struction of a monument over his grave, at a cost
not to exceed £5OO. Also, he devised £2OO to fix
up suitable memorials over his dead parents’
graves in old Ireland. Which suggests that
although John O’Brien was assured of his own
superior importance, he was not utterly insensible
to the fact that his parents had, in some remote
degree, contributed to its existence, and that some
tangible appreciation of their good offices in that
direction.was, therefore, owing to them. The self-
sacrifice of the deceased merits admiration. Had
he been altogether unmindful of the claims of his
dead parents, he could have gone in for £7OO
worth of personal memorial. As it was, he took
but £5OO worth for himself, and handed the bal-
ance to the other less important shareholders.
VV hat a lesson to the irreverent native youth, who
so constantly reminds the authors of his being that
it is about time they were pole-axed !

European names such as “ Charlie ” and “ Willie,”

the ghosts of those buried in a foreign land endure.
Think how destitute they feel when they meet at
the Annual Grave-Visiting Festival in our own
country and see the happy spirits of those whose
bones are safe in the holy' soil. Coldness or hunger
or the absence of the women we love is nothing to
the terrible feelings of those poor spirits, made all
the more miserable by the happiness of the other
spirits. So please send some money and your
ancestors will bless you. If your crops have been
good or if you have been fortunate with your
Tattersall send more than you would have done
otherwise. Do not send postage-stamps and let
your letter be registered.”
* * * *
This cutting from Perth (W.A.) Morning
Herald needs no remark further than that it is
no skit on the speed of Westralian trains, but is
given as a sober piece of news :
Accident to a Railway Guard.—A railway guard named
Richard Gildard had a narrow escape last night. He was
in charge of a train which was running to Perth from
Midland Junction. When approaching' E. Perth station
he noticed that the door of the dog-box had become un-
fastened, and was swinging open, and he attempted to
walk along the footboard of the train in order to close it,
but in getting from one truck to Another he missed his
footing and fell. The train was crossing an embankment
at the time, but Gildard rolled to the bottom without
sustaining injury, and was enabled to catch his train at
the E. Perth station and bring it into Perth.

“ Melb.” : The Church of England Gram-
mar School has just been in the throes of produc-
ing anew head-master. A Sydney man comes
out on top. The school ought to have stuck to
Morris. As soon as he married Higinbotham’s
daughter he was booked and cooked. Higgy
egged him on to lecture against land monopoly.
Such a fellow could not possibly be trusted with
the intellects and souls of the young tallowfats.
Since then the school has been on the wallow, in
the trough of the ocean.

At recent N.Q. Anglican Synod, when the
question under discussion was the spiritual value
of a bishop’s presence, a young shepherd electrified
his brethren with an anecdote- “ During his
.Lordship’s last visit to my parish ’’—the following
conversation was overheard between two miners
occupying same room in local hotel. “ ‘Gobi’ to
church to-morrow, Bill?’ ‘Not me!’ (emphati-
cally). ‘But Barlow’s goin’ to preach.’ ‘Don’t
care a d—n who’s goin’ to preach.’ ‘ Oh, yes, Bill;
let’s go and give poor old Barlow a bob ! Then
everybody tried hard to focus “ poor old Barlow ”
without appearing to do it.
THE INSCRUTABLE.
What wondrous worlds those eyes of thine !
What mysteries dwell within!
At eventide, with these ashine,
Who would not win to sin !
Of mornings, tender, dewy, bright—
How different, then they seem !
Twin orbs, with God’s own peace alight—
Is Love itself a dream ?
L. R. MACLEOD.

was to keep “tally,’ in the days of the System,
out West.

S.A. Advertiser’s artless London man
announces that the annual S.A. dinner has been
postponed until the arrival of Glov. Buxton, who
will then take the chair. If the committee has
any horse sense it will hurry up that dinner and
get it over ere the evangelistic brewer arrives.
H. Ex. is the hollo west frost in the shape of a
chairman that Adelaide has yet experienced, for
he sits nearly all the time in one attitude and with
such a rigid, Calvinistic, Nonconformist expression
of abject misery on his countenance that beholders
are almost moved to tears.

“ A.P.” to The Bulletin :
Regarding the fizzled-out agitation against the N.S.W.
Govt. Law clerks’ privileges, two very important points
were overlooked, viz., (1) That the new rules re the ad-
mission as attorneys of such clerks apply to a very
limited number of persons whose tenure of office usually
runs to 10,15 or 20 years, with the consequence that on
an average hardly one clerk will present himself for
examination in two years ; (2) The new rules are merely
a revival of the previously existing provisions enabling
the Supreme Court and Crown Law office to send up cer-
tain well-trained clerks for examination. The present
prothonotary, Mr. C. R. Walsh, the Registrar of Probates,
Mr. Thomas Garrett, Mr. Theo. Powell and Mr. T. E.
Murphy came in under similar rules, and I think they
compare very favorably, to say the least of it, with the
rank-and-file of the profession who have been writing
superior-toned letters to the press on this subject vaunt-
ing their own attainments and deriding the capabilities
of the Government drudge. Meanwhile the Judges hold
their own opinions.
“S.S.” : Happened to be on Spencer-
street railway-platform when the Christian En-
deavor excursion arrived from Adelaide, and
beheld an edifying spectacle'. The majority of the
elderly brethren in the train had little, cheap blue
flags fixed on sticks, and these they fluttered out
of the window, with much splutter and vacuous
giggling, and their wide grins and goatish gambols
were very suggestive of a Sunday-school infant-
ebtss out on a skim-milk razzle. The reception-
committee, male and female, decorated with blue
tags, were bursting with importance and gratuitous
benevolence, and brethren received “ sistern ” and
‘‘sistern” brethren witli a babel of rapturous
gasps, and squeals, and saintly. expletives, and
the racket was deafening for nearly 10 minutes.

BEGGAR: “ What I out up all that wood for a bob—
d’ye think I work for pleasure, mum? ”
LADY: “ Oh, dear, no; for beer !”
Little Dr. Maloney, in the Vic. Assembly,
pleads for the right of every child born out of
wedlock to be registered as the child of its parents
(when both parents are willing) without reference
to their unmarried state. Also Maloney asks that
the registration of a “ legitimate ” baby shall not
entail a confession of illegitimacy as regards the
previous offspring of either parent.

* * * *
Within the last few weeks Toowoomba and
Roma have turned out a pair of suicides, aged
respectively 22 and 35, victims of unrequited
love. And only last week a man of dissipated
habits returned from the North to Brisbane and
found his one time paramour living with another
individual, whereupon he settled his troubles by
cutting the woman’s throat and then his own.
Eros, the gentle, the tender, the beautiful . . .
the raving maniac, the blood-stained butcher !
The still-unsettled question as to whether
theN.S.W. Inebriate Asylum, the site of which
was gazetted the other day, shall be subject to the
Comptroller-General of Prisons or to the Inspec-
tor-General of the Insane should be promptly
determined in one way. Principle and practice
will both be confused if the institution be handed
over to the Prisons Department. The inmates
will be depressed by contact, if not diseased by
contagion, and the motives underlying the new
movement will be stultified.
• » • »
The Victorian Government has decided
that it can't prosecute the respectable-looking old
gentleman, who professed to be an up-country
J.P. when he wasn’t, and who peacefully took his
scat on a Melbourne bench the other day, and
helped to sentence a few people. There is no law
to deal with the bogus J.P. This opens up a very
gladsome prospect, indeed. The J.P. is invulner-
able. No one can know all of him by sight, and
he can step into a court almost anywhere in the.
province, and discharge his friend with a caution,
or amuse himself by giving somebody three
months, with impunity. The present J.P. was
bowled out because he foolishly gave a name which
didn’t appear on the list of cheap and nasty
beaks ; if he had chosen the designation of some
real J.P. with an address away in far
Croajingolong he might have been undiscovered to
this day. Is he first of the bogus beaks, or the
seven hundredth, or what ?

* * * »
The N.S.W. Art Society’s recent smoke-
night was a most enjoyable function. Some of
the best musicians of Sydney played and sang—
Lemmone, Harry Weir, “Daddy” Hallewell; and
Harry Leston and Lawrence Campbell recited.
Then there was Price, M.P., who did the inevit-
able “ Man from Snowy River.” Lightning-
sketches were to the fore, but not so remarkable
as in previous years ; Souter being missed. The
company was miscellaneous —politicians, musicians,
lawyers, literary men, rival artists, high and other
officials. J. B. Suttor - , Critchett Walker, John
Arthur Barry, and Favenc were there. John
Lemmone, who played divinely on the flute, is one
of the few artists in the world who can cause a
Philistine to discriminate between that instru-
ment and a tin-whistle. There were quiet corners
in the rooms remote from the concert-room, where
the meditative sat and gazed dreamily at pictures.
Arthur Collingridge gave his annual rend ring of
the “Marseillaise,” though this year without the
blouse and sword which were formerly its in-
separable properties.
«• * * *
There apparently isn’t going to be much
of the threatened exodus of S.A. farmers to the
Darling Downs and other available parts of
Queensland. Farmers who have settled, and
others who have been over to spy out the land,
report wonderfully fine soil, but too uncer-
tain winter rainfall, and too certain a tropical
down-pour when crops are ripening. And freights,
it is added, put really material assistance by
dairying out of the question at present. Mr.
Donald Murray, whose word counts for a good
deal in the northern S.A. areas, says this, among
much else.

Among legal anomalies awaiting the vigor-
ous whisk of a reforming broom is that which
prescribes that a physician shall be paid a fee for
attending N.S.W. inquests,’but that a chemist
shall not.
The niceties of the law in N.S.W. Charge
of using insulting language “ whereby a breach of
the peace might have been occasioned ’’—summons
to the effect on the ordinary printed form. The
Victorian (Bendigo) case of Wakley resuscitated
—also Clarson v. Blair, to support the contention
that the information should have stated that the
breach “may be occasioned.” Case dismissed.

pnnocent after all?

The thirst for litigation ! Damages were
lately claimed in a Brisbane Court for the
injury done to property by a trespasser who had
rolled himself in a piece of bed-ticking and slept
in a house from which the last flood had driven
the occupants. Judge Real smiled a weary smile,
and gave complainant damages to the extent of
sixpence.
• * * *
St. Helena, Queensland’s chief gaol, shines
by comparison with other Australian prisons.
During last year it witnessed no executions and
no floggings; and insubordination was a thing
almost unknown. You can always have a chance
of reforming your gaol-bird if you first reform
your gaoler.
* * * *
Lovely woman is to the fore again. During
the recent M.L. Parliamentary stonewall, a
motion was passed excluding strangers and conse-
quently the press. Some time afterwards, in the
a.m., a member pointed out to acting-Speaker
Guinness that a woman in the ladies’ gallery was
busy taking notes. Guinness, however, was
equal to the occasion, and ruled that by English
precedent he couldn’t see the ladies' gallery, and,
therefore, couldn’t see' the lady taking notes ;
much as a Judge can’t see a lawyer till he has his
wig on. Consequently the only report of the
stonewall comes from the lady in question who
stuck to her post all night; and very good read ng
it is. She describes the snores of sleeping mem-
bers as ranging from a grunt to a nasal drone ;
and “all that could be seen of the Govt, whip
was a blue blanket and a pink sheet of blotting-
paper.” After describing various others, she goes
on, “Fortunately, I could not see them all.” In
future, no doubt, the mere male pressman will
have to disguise himself in skirt and bonnet, or,
perchance in bloomers, and betake himself and his
flimsy to the ladies’ gallery.

“ The Peerage is getting more mouldy and
moth-eaten every day,” writes The Bulletin’s
London correspondent. A certain young duke
has had to be put in irons, orbed, on board his
own yacht in the Channel through omitting to
shoot the skipper. Regretting his bad aim, he
brought up an armful of six-shooters which he
distributed amongst the crew, urging them to co-
operatively corpse the captain. But at under
forty, “lor’ bless yer, sir, these dooks suffers from
their teeth ’’ —so they locked him in his cabin and
sent for the police. The rest of the mildewed
nobility are walking about in secret places cursing
Hooley and looking for another of his kind.

“ BULLETIN ” DEFINITIONS.
Law—One man’s meat, another’s poison.
Newest thing in the world—To-day’s newspaper.
‘Oldest ditto—Yesterday’s.
Court —State of man before and after marriage.
Tip—Unnecessary necessary payment.
Advice—Gratuitous waste.
Vice—That which redeems man from the com-
mon-place.
Politeness—The Whited Sepulchre of Nature.
Woman —A useful article but expensive luxury.
Kisses —Stepping stones to hell.

Editor Bulletin. —Be recent Sydney Aus-
tralia Hotel Sunday-selling case in which, though
the manager was fined, the license was not. en-
dorsed, nor the three “gentlemen” concerned
hauled up and fined for being on licensed premises
during illegal hours. If defendant had been a
small, struggling publican with only one bar,
instead of the controller of the splendid Aus-
tralia, the license would have been endorsed,
the common, vulgar customers fined, and sharper
police supervision ordered. Again, one law for the
rich and another for the poor. Never been fined
myself, so have the right to growl.— Publican.

There’s a cheerfully-frank M.D. in an E.
Coast village of the Land of Lots o’ Time. He re-
cently visited a mining-camp in company with
a rather pious storekeeper. All hands as-
sembled on the departure of the
twain, and as the tea-and-sugar
saint mounted his horse he earn-
estly spoke unto the miners for a
few minutes, and wound up with,
“ Good-bye, my friends, and .may
the Lord bless yers.” Then Saw-
bones, with well-copied earnest-
ness : “ Good-day, chaps, and may
the Lord see fit to strike you all sick.
My address is Nodville.”
