A WOMAN’S LETTER.
„, Sydney, September 20, 1898.
My Dear Myee—
Race week went off gaily, with a bigger Mel-
bourne contingent than usual. Pretty Mrs. Percy
Chirnside dressed herself uniquely all along the
week, and Mrs. Edward Miller was another
remarkably well-constructed figure. The daughter
of a big Melbourne draper who has “climbed,”
startled Rand wick with a red silk blouse and white
skirt one day. But I noticed a score of imitators
next race meeting, and presume that Sydney is
not going to quarrel with any sort of Melbourne
lead just now. Saturday’s racing drew a
tremendous crowd with a wide license in frock-
ing. White piques looked as happy as silks,
and shirts and skirts abounded. Hysterics
threatened in the Steeplechase, when Parnell’s
jockey picked up his fallen horse, and raced into
the third place, with blood streaming down his
face. As a rule, jockeys’ accidents don’t stir any-
one, but this was different. The horse came in
third, you see.
The two Government House balls came off on
1 uesday and Thursday, Tuesday’s ball being quite
another story. To be asked on Thursday amounted
to almost a blunder. Miss Ruby Madden, in
black net with long sleeves, and steel armor laid
on thick, glittered as brightly as anyone in the
ballroom. Brides and debutantes were every-
where. Mrs. Livingstone Learmonth, who was
Mass Mnrcia Cox last month, wore her embroidered
bridal robe, and Mrs. Vallange also made frivolous
use of her ceremonial gown.
Two mild scares livened Tuesday’s hop. Some-
body in the room was said to have measles, and
everyone craned to get a glimpse of the red spots
oh the unconscious neck. She was surrounded by
a crowd two-deep all the evening, curiosity killing
alarm. The one other sensation was the gentleman
who had been handing chairs in a big city shop
that morning. Sydney doesn’t understand how to
treat a case of this kind yet, and crude cuts, foz
he used to be in “ our set,” resounded through the
air. I heard a, lady, who came out of a beer-
barrel, pathetically whisper to her spouse, “ Go-
vernment ’Ouse ain’t what it hused to be.”
The most graceful woman in Sydney was a gentle
symphony in cream satin and yellow-hearted roses.
Little Miss Winnie Austin, in black satin and a
beauty-spot, and Mrs. Walter Allan, in pink and
rosebuds, were two particularly attractive items.
Mrs. Rennie’s black frock, with its sequin decora-
tions, suggested the cut of foreign scissors. Lady
Rupert Clarke produced another gown from the
sea, with jewels crusting the bodice like scales,
and frills where the feet ought to be. Mrs. W. B.
Mitchell, whose frocks might fill a folio, was in
grey sequined tulle one night, lit up with vivid
bouquet and streamers, and another night blazed
brightly in a cerise satin polonaise, cu t up over
white chiffon flounces.
Sydney wiseacres are snaking their heads over
His Ex., and saying that Her Gracious would not
be too pleased could she know of the high jinks at
Govt. House last week. Considering that the
Gov. is Royalty’s representative, and that while
the balls were at their height Royalty at ’Ome was
mourning the Empress of Austria, the wise folk
are probably right as regards the etiquette of the
thing.
The macrons of North Sydney managed one of
the best balls of the season on Monday night.
1 he element of the brass button had something to
do with its success, but every other element that
goes to make up a good hop was also present. Two
aides shed their light on the scene for an hour or
so, and I noticed that there was a new
alacrity stirring up masculine wall-supporters.
Dances are now so extensively arranged by
telephone before a ball begins, that men who want
a partner are obliged to hustle round a bit. It’s
all very well to be a wallflower when there are
plenty of imploring eyes dumbly asking you to
dance. But it’s a different thing “when all the
women have their programmes full, and don’t take
any notice. The hostesses included Mrs. Burton
Dibbs, in black satin and the new coiffure ; Mrs.
Barton, in lemon-tinted pink ; and 16 other North
Shore matrons. The belle of the ball has gone out
of fashion, but the nearest thing to it was Miss
Irene Marks, in white satin and a silver butterfly
m her golden tresses. For the rest De Groen’s
band made music, and the supper was a dream.
The University girls have settled their diffi-
culties at last and a dance at Riviere College,
Woollahra, is to be the result. This is the first
time the University Association has entertained
away from the University, but if the people make
the place and not the place the people it should be
a success—moreover, it is a very nice place.
i Has it ever struck you how like Society is to
Gaol ? lam not accusing you of ever having been
m either, but judging from the look of things. All
race-week I had my eye on a score of Sydney
social leaders, who are as much in chokee as that
bowled-out female shop-lifter. I have watched
them take their one little .toddling walk down the
race-lawn, with eyes strained and mouths snaky in
the effort not to miss anybody they ought to see,
and not to see anybody they ought to miss. And
I have seen them stand a whole evening in the
ball-room with the same fear poisoning their ex-
pressions and nipping their "feelings like a mud-
shark. They won’t dance with anybody, for fear
they might miss somebody taller. And they
won’t eat any supper for fear they might be
thought common, otherwise hungry. Ah, well,
there’s this about being poor: the rich pay for
their misery, the poor get theirs for nothing.
It seems as if the wicked quip about there being
no flirtations on the P. and O. boats, because the
mails are all tied up in bags, is going to turn out a
fact. Cause, the dinner-party that is accused of
having wrecked the China—now once more afloat.
Yes, brass-buttons, captains, officers, doctors, all
the lovely men, are threatened with an isolated
dming-table, where no fair charmers are admitted.
They are not even to be allowed a flutter between
meals, and social sea-doings are to be kept right
out of their way. These changes are to endure
until—well, until the Board discovers the falling-
off in the passenger-lists.
And there will be the chance for the captain’s
wives to come in. Isn’t it a primeval superstition
that won’t allow wives to travel by their hubby’s
boats, for fear the latter will neglect their duties !
More enlightened minds believe that rocks would
not have the least chance, or dinner-parties a
danger, if all the wives were given free passes.
But I forgot—the steamship-owners say that no
vessel can carry two captains, and that is why Mrs.
Captain is not allowed to go to sea with her
husband.
The social side of these big steamers has been
sating its head off for a long time. To seek peace
m a sea-voyage was once a popular scheme, but,
nowadays, nasty little social microbes get on at
every port, and fester the air with balls, and con-
certs, and bazaars, and theatricals, until there
isn’t even time to be wholesomely seasick. Out
among the infinities, with only the sea, and the
sky,, and an elemental whale or so looking on—
that’s where social status matters. It’s even more
important there than in the Victoria Salon. A
boat went ’Ome, the other day, with a lot of
students travelling second-class, and a wealth of
Blood and Money in the first. First-class gave
balls, and icily excluded second. Second-class got
up theatricals, and wrote their -programmes in
Greek characters, winding up with “ No first-class
passengers admitted.” And first-class used to
stroll past miserably trying to decipher the mystery.
For first-class didn’t know Greek.
At last the concert strain is slackening. Soon
there will be silence in the halls of song. Amy
Sherwin, who has been drawing splendid shilling
houses all the week, leaves for London this month,
and, so far, there is no word of anyone new.
Clever little Adela Verne got together a fine pro-
gramme and a fair house at the concert she pre-
sented to Lemmone last week. The heaviest part
of the programme fell on her own white muslin
shoulders, two huge concertos forming the bulk of
her work. A heterodox query, but do people care
for concertos ? And should they, if they don’t ?
Music isn’t music when it wearies, when the brain
begins to be strained, and the creation doesn’t
send out any emotional messages, any thrills or
throbs. When it has no meaning it’s a noise. And
the ordinary listener isn’t educated enough to fol-
low all the subtle undercurrents of thought that
move beneath the music’s complications. One
concerto an evening, if you like, but two tighten
the system until the nerves kick, and the audience
feels about as comfortable as a grasshopper in a
sovereign-case.
Oh, this charming “waitawhile” land! Mr.
Guenett, of ‘Melbourne musical fame, received
verbal permission from M. Wiegand, during his
recent visit to Sydney, to play on “the greatest
organ in the world.” When he had been playing
for about an hour one morning, some persons
approached and told him he must either have per-
mission from the Mayor and aldermen to go on
playing or pay ten shillings. Mr. Guenett accord-
ingly left off. When he had been back in Mel-
bourne about a fortnight, and had forgotten the
occurrence, an official document reached him bear-
ing the signatures of the Mayor and all the city
councillors and giving him gracious leave to try
the organ. He thought of wiring in reply, “ Can’t
stop, can’t stop.”
The year 1898 seems to be an unlucky one (or
lucky ?) for the musical world. In Sydney alone
three prominent professionals have gone to play
harps of gold, of whom the first two, Harry
Hyndes and Miss Pedley, were only beginning
middle age. Poussard, though old, was even more
suddenly cut off. Lady Halle’s tragic death has
stirred the world, and serious illnesses and acci-
dents are troubling many of our well-known local
musicians.
Gossips are already busy coupling the names of
a certain highly-placed widow with more or less
eligible suitors. Lord Rosebery was the first name
mentioned, but he can’t spare the time to come
out ; next, a doctor who lately attended the lady
after an accident; next, as being very suitable,
Melbourne’s leading surgeon; finally, an aristo-
cratic member of a certain vice-regal staff. The
last-named, however, is said to have had his
aspirations nipped in the bud by a sprightly
relative of the lady herself. Yet a third lady is
supposed to have had some claim on the attentions
of this gay lordling. This is a well-known Melb.
society girl, but even the proverbial grain of truth
is now expelled from this story by the announce-
ment of the lady’s engagement to an officer from
India.
English newspapers announce the return to Aus-
tralia, in February, of our open-handed mil-
lionairess, Miss Eadith Walker. Engliseeman 10
catchee ?
Speaking of engagements, the youthful curly-
haired scholar, just appointed to a certain head-
mastership, had a rather embarrassing pre-matri-
monial experience in Sydney. Several otherwise-
unemployed persons so persistently announced his
engagement to his chief’s daughter that the
harassed lady one day remarked desperately,
“ Why, yes, of course we are engaged.” Far
from stopping the persecution, congratulations
began to pour in on every side, in the Very midst
of which the pedagogue escaped to England,
whence he returned a few months later with a
blushing bride.
One of the month’s benedicts has not allowed
history to repeat itself over his second marriage.
His first engagement lasted nine years, and was
followed by eleven months of matrimony. Two
months covered acquaintance, courtship and
engagement in the second case, and forty-two
years of wedded bliss should result by inverse
ratio.
It must certainly feel strange to be bridesmaid
to your father’s second wife, yet this is what
actually happened at a fashionable Sydney
wedding last week.
I went to a dear little unsophisticated party the
r . given by the Boy Authors. It wouldn’t
be fair to tell who they are, but they all took it in
turn to read their own inspirations. Did I say to
ro; Mj • I mean to mumble, remonstrate, shuffle,
sniff, snort, cough, gabble, choke, whisper, stutter,
croon, make noises m all the unused parts of their
throats, and, finally, obliterate their dear dream-
children’s faces with a rush that could have put a
girdle round two worlds in twenty minutes. There
were some ten Authors present, and every one
began with: “ Just-a-little-thing-I-wrote-no-good-
only-a-sketeh. ” Even writers with a Name. But
nobody noticed. If you are going to read your
own inspirations presently, it’s wonderful what
you can endure. Everybody applauded. Won-
derful what you’ll applaud just before your turn
comes. That’s the only way to keep a little sect
like this together. Let everyone read something
he’s written.
There’s a move on just now to edge together all
literary and artistic people, and make them smoke
and drink at a given signal, and talk shop at in-
tervals. But Sydney isn’t big enough. In London,
where there are plenty of big people running along
different big lines, artistic clubs have a meaning.
But in Sydney, one pressman reckons he knows as
much as another. As for artists, who can tell
what an artist knows ?
That fashion is a fickle goddess is a remark so
true that it will bear oft repeating. Only last
season, to buy a dressy blouse for a few guineas
was reckoned quite a soft snap if one could run up
any sort of a skirt to match. Now, it is the
correct caper to pay the same price for a be-flounced
and a be-frilled skirt and match the stuff for a
plain bodice.
Other times, other waists. A corset-maker,
who comes to Sydney every year for orders,
showed corns all over her hands the other day.
These come, she alleges, from straining at the
strings of Sydney corsets, and she tells the sad story
of how the seventeen-inch beloved of corset-
makers has widened into twenty-four in the
fashionable world.
Do you know that men are not altogether the
culinary connoisseurs they’re painted. In a small
N.S.W. town where the city method of raising
funds for local religious bodies is by means of
social suppers and bun-worries, was a man with a
record for “surrounding” pastry, especially mince-
pies. He never failed to attend these gatherings,
and one day the ladies who ran the show put a
piece of leather through a sausage-mill, and mixed
it with the mince in an especially large pie. Brown
always began on the biggest. Conducted to a seat
opposite the huge mince, he devoured it first, and
then, having eaten the others, paralysed the ex-
pectant jokers by asking for the “recipe for that
there big ’un ” !
Writes my Melbourne correspondent:—
“A mob of women welled up to Vic. Leg.
Council-chamber to hear the Fat Men debate
women’s suffrage. They completely filled the big
galleries, but the chamber looked more animated
than smart. Dress is not the strong point of the
average suffrage woman, and a sombre array of
rusty black, faded browns, and shabby greys cer-
tainly did not tend to enliven the place. A few
Councillors’ wives, daughters and friends occupied
the lower gallery—a plain lot, with the exception
of a handsome dame of plump and pleasing coun-
tenance, who managed to keep up a smile of
amused scorn for two hours.
“The debate was shabbier than its female
hearers, The absolute mental bankruptcy of those
who supported the Bill was only excelled by the
senile indecency of its opponents. Even a suffrage
woman can be pardoned for feeling ashamed
of the wretched arguments advanced in
support of the Bill. The quality of Minister
M‘Culloch’s introductory speech—delivered in a
mumbling, incoherent monotone —would not have
been good enough to persuade a cat to lap cream
or a donkey to eat thistles, much more to induce
the Tory Council to pass a constitutional reform.
Altogether the proceedings were intellectually and
in every way doleful, and Fatman Campbell’s in-
sulting lie that the supporters of woman’s franchise
were either agitators or the unfortunates of the
city, was the only thing about it that rose above
the level of the veriest commonplace.
“ The audience at the Princess’s never turned a
hair on Bert Gilbert’s last night. His own ad-
mirers probably had been choked off lately by
their non-appreciation of ‘Harbor Lights,’ and
people who came in crowds to sniff the briny
drama knew not Bert, except as a new acquaint-
ance, and gave all their claps to Knight and
Ferrar. All the same, the whole thing would
have been as dull as ditch-water but for Tom
Dossiter. Florence Terriss, as Peggy Chudleigh,
softened down her angles a shade as time went,
but at first her lack of grace took from the success
of the joint scenes with Gilbert.
“Patrons of last Saturday’s ‘Pop’ must give
Newbury credit for a liberal shilling’s-worth. A
young baritone, Victor Dougal, in ‘When bright
eyes glance,’ delivered his warning against
feminine snares in a solemn and didactic spirit.
Maggie M‘Cann, rendering ‘l’m ower young to
marry yet, ’ was apparently taken quite literally
by some of her hearers. A nice young Christian
Endeavourer next to me hissed ‘ Absurd! ’ so
fiercely that I couldn’t screw up my courage to
ask whether he suspected the singer of a ‘ wicked
story, ’ or merely condemned her ditty as frivolous.
“Yet the ‘ Pop ’ seemed the very place to divert
a young Christian, sated with a week’s bun-fight-
ing ; but you never know. In due course Philip
Newbury appeared, with his curls combed into his
eyes as usual, to sing two songs quite deliciously.
When Rodda Perry’s turn came, his sleek, lank
hair was all plastered over his’ forehead, and ears
too. More shame for Newbury if the poor boy
learnt his thatch methods from the former. One
yearned to lasso the pair of them off the platform,
shear the monkey-fringes from their ‘foreheads
high and white,’ and shoo them back to duty
again. Boy Rodda, the newly-discovered songful
Bendigo miner—a white-faced, lean lad—looks
very young for 24. He hangs both his arms
straight down in front, bends his knees, and sings
without any feeling in a high, shrill voice. , He
doesn’t fulfil one’s conception of a miner a single
scrap : in fact, it seems cruel to think of him doing
such hard work.
“ That skit on a Xmas pantomime by clever
Rose Musgrove and M ‘Nicol Turner was the snap-
piest item in a breezy programme. Miss Mus-
grove, looking bright as a button in pink frock,
apron-skirt, chiffon sash, and pink fillet in her
hair, ‘took off ’ the prevalent coon-song con-
vincingly, and succeeded admirably in numerous
bits of song-and-dance nonsense, and was realistic
as the twangy child-fairy of the stage. Mr.
Turner, lacking his colleague’s voice advantages,
was nevertheless gay and laughable, though, in
my opinion, not built for knee-breeches. That
discrepancy of shin-outline, or whatever it was,
recalled Gilbert when, in response to Maid
Marion’s defence of her bloomers, ‘ Oh, they’re all
right, dad,’ he replied, in wilful misunderstand-
ing, ‘ Well, your right’s all right, but your left’s a
bit cronk ! ’
“A party-of Austral Salonites presided over the
elocutionary debut of Miss Osmond, local pen-
woman, and a sort of white-headed girl with sister
clubites. The recitation was ‘ The Sentinel’s
Alarm ’ a little piece of descriptive writing pre-
sented to Miss 0 by Bland Holt ’! Did the
donor write it himself, I wonder ? If so, he should
have pruned it liberally before turning it loose on
a confiding lady friend. Such lots of unneces-
sary twaddle ! ‘ The tired horses slept, and we
could hear their heavy breathing.’ Who
wants to know about animals snoring when
thrilling incidents are, or should be, pending?
‘ What is that spot ? Has the spot a body ? It
has a body ! ’ —is a roundabout way of introducing a
red-skin ! Then there were ‘whirrings’ of crickets,
‘ wah-hooings ’ of night - birds, and responsive
‘ wah-hooings ’ from night-bird’s mate. Some
hearers laughed rudely at these imitations, but I
didn’t, because, not having been to red-skin land,
I can’t say whether these insect and bird sounds
were fair to the originals or not. Yes, I blame
the author, rather than the elocutionist. However,
the latter got lots of flowers and a recall, so that’s
all right. Mdme. Spada, in blue satin, white
gauze and pink roses, held her own against all-
comers.
“Melb. Mayor McEacharn’s ball-invitations
differ materially in wording from those of his pre-
decessors, and are especially composed with an eye
to real ‘Naiceness.’ The company of the invitee
is not baldly requested as heretofore; he is in-
vited ‘ to meet the Administrator of the Govt, and
Lady Madden at a ball, ’ etc.
“Re the murdered Empress, Janet Laay Clarke
and others have been showering impassioned
epistolatory sympathy on the local Austrian Con-
sul in a sort of personal way, as if they thought
that patriot’s bruised spirit must break if not ten-
derly treated. As a matter of fact, Mr. Pinschof
seems to be bearing up, as indeed why shouldn’t
he ? After all, take his consular dignities as
seriously as you like, the home-quitting importer
probably wasn’t absolutely a tame cat in Imperial
circles. Besides, there are always consolations.
Mrs. Pinschof (Madame Wiedermann), besides
being a masterly vocalist, is one of the best ama-
teur cooks in Melb.—which means a good deal to
a Benedict, who, in his wooing days, is said to
have forwarded with every billet-doux from far
Australia to his be trot lied in the home-land a
love-token in the shape of a cookery-book.
“Talking of all-conquering nurses and their
subjugated patients, one of those youthful Wallace
litigants led to the altar, a while back, a charming
capped-and-pinnied ministrant, somewhat his
senior, who had just seen him safely through
a long illness. Another goldfish in the bag of the
Florence Nightingale brigade.
“ Old adherents tear their hair re cne Chalmers
Church sale and consequent removal of memorials
of Dr. Cairns, its original pastor. After all, Dr.
Cairns’ very own connections and double-dyed dis-
ciples held the first torch to the elderly taber-
nacles’s funeral pyre, so to speak, when, after
their Idol’s death, they pig-headedly led forth a
secession from his successor’s congregation and
started that rival firm, ‘ Cairns Memorial Church.’
The new house cut into the business for long
enough to help (mildly speaking) towards the ruin
of the senior branch, and then, having accom-
plished its ruin, sank into poverty itself.
“ The unhappy parson who suffered througn the
split in funds and followers was, apparently,
weighed in the balances and found wanting by
rabid Cairnsites whenever he deviated by one jot
or one tittle from his dead-and-gone predecessor’s
methods—not that the latter had been one of your
meek and lowly sort. Among Vic. Presbyterians
there are a band of warriors, able men most of
them, who have devoted themselves principally to
stoning (speaking metaphorically) offenders into
repentant corners or out of the fold altogether.
“Doctor Morrison, in his anxiety to have a
R.C. girls’ school in Chalmers Church, next door
to the Scotch College, rather than a detrimental
hospital, apparently doesn’t trouble about the
sister institution to his own academy—the Presby-
terian Ladies’ College. The latter won’t care for
a ladies’ high school poaching on its preserves.
And _E. Melb. covers are none too flush of pupils,
as it is, owing to emigration of families to other
suburbs.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Syme enlarges the Leader —a tribute to
the skill with which W. Carrington has wrought
up the Weekly Times.
Cardinal Moran completed his 68th year on
Friday, 16th September. He has been 14 years
Archbishop of Sydney.
Johnny Wallace, M.L.C., of Monahan
fame, never wore a coat in Melbourne up to 20
years ago, or less. He always sported a jumper.
Rougemont, who ought to be translated
Unblushing, is, of course, only a re-incarnation of
Capt. Lawson, whose New Guinea travels were
published a while ago.
Apropos the impending visit to Australia
of a certain alleged nobleman. How about an en-
largement of the scope of the Vic. Undesirable
Immigrants Restriction Bill ?
Latest news tells that Rev. L. M. Isitt has
had his pocket picked in London. One of his
yarns in Australia was that his most edifying con-
vert was an ex-pick Docket. A fresh graduate—or
a relapse ?
Adelaide citizens are becoming inclined to
resent the way in which Chief Justice Way
attempts to monopolise the principal offices in
connection with every public movement and in-
stitution.
Ex-Secretary Russell, so long and favor-
ably known in connection with Sydney Hospital—
both the old wooden one and the new palatial
structure —has now the ill-luck to occupy a
patient’s bed in one of the wards.
Mrs. John Grant, who lately died in
Wangaratta district, just under 99 years of
age, spent 96 of them without a doctor’s help.
Then the sawbones was only called in because the
old lady had fallen and injured a joint.
An attempt is to be made, judging by
evidence given before the House of Lords’ Copy-
right Bill Committee, to compel writers of
“colonial ” books to forward copies to the British
Museum and Bodleian Library, free of charge.
Mysterious cutting from Brisbane society
paper:—
The Earl of Yarmouth, who has matte a name for him-
self in Australia, is about to undergo, in London, an
operation, which it is hoped may relieve him of a source
of some anxiety to his friends.
Earl De La Warr, of Hooley renown, is
not a stranger to Australia. He was pearl-fishing
in the Far North when the drowning of his elder
brother, Viscount Cantelupe, made him heir to
the De La Warr peerage. Also he is known in
Westralia.
THE LATE HORACE POUSSARD.
44 Bun Tuck ” —the words, somehow, sug-
gest a Sunday-school feed— is the latest nick-name
bestowed upon the stalwart Premier of Maoriland.
Rather, it is claimed to be the revival of a long-
defunct title —the story, told in all seriousness to
Parliament, being that it was the name by which
Seddon was known when he was partner in a
mining claim with a couple of Chinamen, some 20
years ago. According to the apocryphal yarn,
Dick “squeezed ” the almond-eyed brethren, and
sold the mine to “ white devils.” In other words,
Bun Tuck “ tuk the bun,” which the story )s fairly
entitled to do as a piece of Parliamentary fiction,
skilfully woven and supported by a Chinese docu-
ment and sworn affidavit.
During a recent shivoo at Melb. morgue,
Zox, M.L.C., was talking to Dr. Brett, one of the
post-mortemists, when Brett asked Zox whether he
had seen “the chamber of horrors ” —photographs
of unclaimed bodies which are kept for possible
identification. Thinking that the gruesome gallery
was the apartment where he stood, the short-
sighted Zox turned to a frame of pictures on the
wall and glanced up and down one of the rows
with the eye of an expert. Suddenly he developed
a smile of satisfaction as he picked out one face
and said, “I’ll swear I’ve seen that chap dozens
of times.” Knowing that the portraits which Zox
was examining were those of the members of the
Yic. Parliament, Brett went over with a grin and
found that Zox’s supposed recognisable corpse was
no less a personage than ex-Premier Gillies. But,
after all, Zox wasn’t so far out. As a politician,
Gillies has been dead and decomposed for years.
Major-General Kitchener and Holled-
Smith, of Victoria, were for some years com-
panions-in-arms in Egypt. Smith also succeeded
Kitchener as Gov.-General of the Red Sea littoral,
and believes that had he not been transferred to
Victoria the glory of avenging Gordon would have
fallen to him.
If there is any man who ought to have a
fair idea whether M. deßougemont, who is telling
the tall tales of adventures among Australian
aborigines, has the truth in him, it is Harry Stock-
dale. Stock dale began his connection with the
north-west part disastrously nearly a quarter of a
century ago, and has continued it more or less ever
since.
“Melb.”: “Demise, Charles Ryan, of
Macedon, father of Dr. “Plevna” Ryan. His
Macedon garden is a paradise, universally'allowed
the finest private garden in Australia. There one
can realise the feelings of Satan outside Eden.
Ryan was a squatter, who came successfully out
of the fight with Democracy.
On September 30 Agent-General Saul
Samuel finally passes from the public life of N.S.
Wales. In 1854 he was elected to the old Leg.
Council, and in one capacity or another (mostly
remunerated) he has represented N.S.W. interests
ever since. His leave of absence on full pay,
which terminates on the date stated, stands in
lieu of pension and retiring allowance. On Oct. 2
he completes his 78th year.
“ Scribo ” : Bateman, entrepreneur of
“Round the World in Eighty Days,” at Melb.
Royal, is son of the former part-proprietor and
whole leaderist of Ballarat Courier They said
Bateman, senr., used to work all day on his lead-
ing article. Then he went to a restaurant and ate
two complete meals. Undoubtedly his persistent
articles were the ablest defence extant of the
M‘Culloch and Berry regimes.
Old G. W. Rusden, author of the “ His-
tory of Australia,” has a theological bent. He has
lately ordered 250 copies of Prof. Campbell
Fraser’s “Philosophy of Theism,” a thick, expen-
sive book, to distribute among the Vic., public
libraries and mechanics’ institutes. Rusden is a
Victorian pensioned civil servant, and is lucky to
be able to afford rather over <£loo in distributing
a book that few will care to read.
A nephew of that famous surgeon, J. Y.
Simpson, who, in ’47, popularised, even if he did
not actually introduce, the use of chloroform, died
in S. Australia, a few days ago—a Methodist
minister, bearing the great man’s name. Metho-
dist pulpits and obesity seem somehow incon-
gruous ; the Rev. J. Y., who inherited a tendency
to great girth, felt it so, and worried over it.
Besides, he was a Scotch humorist.
Bishop Samuel Ballarat
takes great pride in announcing
his blood-relationship to the suc-
cessful General Kitchener, who
is his first cousin.
“H.” : The first Melb.
schoolmaster was Brickwood, an
Anglican clergyman, who left
for an English incumbency,
which he has held ever since,
and he is still reported alive. A
surviving pupil is squatter Bond,
formerly an antagonist of Mor-
gan, the bushranger, in N.S.W.,
but now settled at Preston, near
Melb. Last year the few alive
of Brickwood’s scholars dined at
Bond’s, and then signed an ad-
dress of congratulation, which
was forwarded to their old
pedagogue. Brickwood was
highly esteemed, like Cape, in
Sydney.
Peggy Pryde’s “ Mother
Doyle,” who used to go bail for
all the “blokes” in trouble,
has a real, live prototype in
Melbourne—a little, quiet,
motherly - looking, spectacled
old woman, who follows the
calling of a professional “ bonds-
woman.” In appearance she
would make a splendid matron
for a benevolent asylum, but
there is only one public place
where her homely old phiz is
seen, and that is the witness-box
of the different police - courts
around Melb. when she arises
to swear that she is worth such-
and-such a sum over and above
all her just debts.
“ I think my brother Judges might show
sufficient consideration for my arguments to re-
frain from talking when I am delivering judg-
ment,” said Mr. Justice Bundey in S.A. Full
Court, the other day. Whereupon Way and
Boucaut became suddenly as silent as the grave,
but the rebuke was discreetly kept out of the local
press.
When old Tom Brassey gets back to Vic-
toria—in a few weeks now—the papers will be
interviewing him. There never was a more inter-
viewable man. The difficulty is, not for a reporter
to get to see him, but to get away again when the
scribe has finished. H.E. has been known to shut
the door, stand with his back against it, and prose
away for an hour, after the regular interview was
finished, about the navy, foreign politics, and any-
thing that affords an opportunity for being dull
and ponderous. In the case of a Governor a scribe
can’t say “ Shut up! I’m off ! ” though he may
think it hard all the time, hoping there is some-
thing in thought-transference.
“ Geolay ” : ne Ben Hall. He was shot—
as he lay asleep, according to “ Sir Watkin, the
black-tracker’s, version—in the scrub two miles
from his betrayer’s hut. The wife of the indi-
vidual who “ put him away,” some months after
the affair, gave birth to a child, a boy, on whose
body were reproduced many
marks like old bullet-wounds
(Hall, when picked up, had 32
bullet-holes in his body). How
is that for pre-natal influ-
ence ?

Recently there died at Navarre (Vic.),
Mrs. Stuart, who was born at Bulleen, near Tem-
plestowe (Vic.), in 1840. She was the daughter of
William Howard, ship’s who came
from Launceston with John Pascoe lawkner, the
founder of Melbourne.
AN.S.W. District Court Judge with de-
signs on the Supreme Court Bench was so irritated
at being passed over twice running that he wrote
out and forwarded his resignation. Friendly
counsel intervened, however, and he was permitted
to withdraw the document.
Speaker Mason (Vic.) is going it. He
gives dinners to members and Parliamentary
officials every week. It recalls the old Mat
Davies days—with the difference that Mason does
it out of real money, and his guests need not
feel that they are eating paper.
N.S.W. Chief Justice Darley entered his
69th year on Sunday last. In November he will
have been 12 years on the Bench. He was ad-
mitted to N.S.W. bar in June, ’62, and to the
Irish bar in Jan.,’s3—so that he has spent more
than 45 years of his life at the bar and on the
Bench.
Mrs. Way, wife of the S.A. Chief Justice,
was in her younger days a most accomplished
amateur actress and vocalist, and she gave many
performances round the Southern towns of the pro-
vince in “Our Bay,” “Milky White,” and other
oon>“dm“. She also appeared as plaintiff in “Trial
by Jury.”
The anarchists of other countries are fully as
mad as those of Italy, but instead of using a file or
dagger as Lucceni did, they trust to a revolver or
other firearm, and consequently merely scare the
intended victim. Sometimes the victim knows
nothing about the attempt, as the weapon simply
clicks. It is the national weapon and not in-
ordinate national bloodthirstiness which makes
the Italian murderer so often successful.
The secretaryship of N.S.W. Evangelical
Alliance (a very prayerful but purposeless institu-
tion) has just been vacated by the venerable Rev.
T. S. Forsaith, familiar in M.L. history as Pre-
mier of that province for a day-and-a-half. His
was the memorable “ Clean-Shirt ” Ministry
brought into office as the result of a chance-vote
in the days when even N.S.W. could afford to
laugh at the antics of Maoriland politicians.
THE SENSE OF COLOR.
The sky was blue, the bush was green and red,
When Reckless Randolph sang his songs erratic.
But now he’s turned quite eminently proper;
“One cannot live on poetry,” he said,
And so he feeds his taste poly-chromatic
On peacock ore and carbonates of copper !
N.Q. Von Kotzb.
While the portraits of Melbourne’s two
first coroners were being unveiled at the local
morgue last week, Coroner Candler stated that
during the Hotham regime Dr. Campbell, the
coroner of the day, was sacked for simply “talking
back ” to so exalted a personage as a Governor.
Gov. Hotham had written to Campbell asking him
what it would cost Government to bury stray
corpses on its own account, and, on the doctor
responding that “ he was not an undertaker,” the
unspeakable Hotham promptly dismissed him.
That was before the days of Responsible Govern-
ment, when Vic. and all in it were at the mercy of
t he whims of an Imperial popinjay.
Rumored that Deputy Super. Webb, of
Sydney Metropolitan Fire Brigade, is to be pro-
moted to be chief superintendent. Apropos of
which, it was said that Premier Reid secured the
“ fire vote ” (about 40) at King Division election,
by promising a pension to retiring Super. Bear.
“ Thorold ” : The only
Red Page critic who remains
cold to the charms of Daley
makes the absurd remark
that our poet “ is meant to
be the Herrick of Austra-
lia ” —and, apparently, no-
thing more. Certainly, Daley
almost touches Herrick’s
dainty fragility and Her-
rick could even make poetry
of a “ crimson stomacher,” or
a “ tempestuous petticoat”—
but wherever did the seven-
teenth century singer evi-
dence the imaginative depth
of “ Lethe,” that greatest of
all Daley's efforts, or the
splendor of “A Sunset Fan-
tasy ? ” How cruel, though,
to ask the average lover of
word-music to name the three
poems of his choice, when
Daley’s book gleams with the
gems of 15 years —when one
has to overlook his sensuous
fragments, his lightsome
“ King in Exile,” with its
appeal to our tenderest re-
membrances, and sonnets
such as “ The Gleaner ! ”

When Judge Macoboy and Villciieuye
Smith were on circuit together in Northern
Victoria they stopped one day at a roadside
boarding-house kept by a Frenchman, noted for
the excellence of his soups. Soup was served to
them from an iron pot in the fireplace, and was
pronounced excellent, but when French jr was
ladling out a second helping he fished up a piece of
rag, over which he swore lustily. “ What is the
matter ? ” asked the Judge in alarm. “ Diable ! ”
was the reply, “ dem navvees will hang der socks
in de fireplace to dry, no matter what I shall say.
Tableaux.
Victoria’s first Judge, E. J. Brewster, who
died at Capetown (S. Afr.) on 17th March last—left
£88,184 in England and ,£61,800 in Victoria, from
which province he had been absent over half-a-
century. Although bred a lawyer he died a par-
son—which somehow recalls the penitent thief —
and left £5200 to English religious and charitable
objects. He did not bequeath one cent to any Vic-
torian charity. After paying certain legacies he
left the balance of his property to his wife for life.
At her death it is to be sold for cash and given
chiefly to societies for the ’version O c Jews and
Roman Catholics.

The late William Henry Walsh, a pioneer
Queenslander, whose vogues were squatting and
politics, had a hospitable manner with guests,
after half-an-hour’s conversation, of saying, “ Well
I think a little spirit would do us no harm,”
therewith producing the decanters and glasses.
Another half-hour’s talk would bring—“ Well, I
think a little more spirit would do us no harm.”
And so on till the visit expended itself. The
writer was once taken by B. D. Morehead to see
“ Old Spirit’s ” epitaph. It was simple enough :
“ And the spirit returned to God who gave it.”
“ Bargo ” : Knew Ernie Brockman, hero
of Wide World lion - story— republished in
Bulletin of Sept. 10th, and erroneously credited
to the Transmitter, which had cribbed it from
the magazine named. He was here (Sydney) tele-
graph-operating for some time, but, having a fixed
idea that a man should not stay more than two or
three years in any one place, resigned. From
occasional communications dropped from Hono-
lulu, Panama, “the States,” etc., he seems since
to have successfully “ hustled ” round three
parts of the globe. I believe every word of his
story, for his manliness and integrity were con-
spicuous. Am not a bit surprised at “ Brocky ”
meeting with his horrible adventure—he had a
knack, amounting to positive genius, for getting
into all sorts of extraordinary scrapes.

“Snell" : The best mysterious disappear-
ance that came under my “ immediate eye ’ hap-
pened in Ballarat South some years ago. One
Kelly went out into his garden after tea to devote
a little time to his favorite vegetables, and sud-
denly disappeared from the face of nature and
the midst of things. From the moment Kelly
stepped from his kitchen door, he seemed to have
been simply wiped out, and an all-night search
resulted only in convincing Mrs. Kelly that Ned
had been carried up to heaven “ for his sins.” The
police were on the job all next day up till four
o’clock, dragging dams and searching in nooks and
corners, and they did not even discover an excuse
for a clue. At four o’clock Mrs. Kelly was weep-
ing in the garden when she heard her husband's
voice calling for help in faint, far-off tones. There
was absolutely nothing about that could hide the
missing man, excepting a few shrubs, and these
had been beaten a dozen times. Mrs. Kelly knew
it was the ghost of her old man calling, and she
went down the road, bare-headed, and yelling.
But the Kelly house-dog had more savee, and
when a neighbor arrived the tyke had solved the
mystery. Kelly had crept under a large, spread-
ing gooseberry-bush to cut away a lot of the
growth, and had gone through into an old shaft,
dragging a few of the boughs down in such a
way that the small aperture was quite hidden.
Kelly was saved from drowning by old timbers
jambed across the shaft, but was badly damaged
when brought to light again.

“ Takapuna ” : It is customary for a
young Maori wife, when nursing an infant,
to take a young pig from a litter and suckle it
with her own child. As soon as the little porker
is fat it is cooked and eaten. I believe this is a
well-known custom ; but I have not seen in print
the fact I now affirm after partaking of the
porker—to wit, that it is much sweeter and more
delicate than if reared in the ordinary way.
Still another of the joys of the bush. Back-
country pubs, mostly do their own “killing”;
and pretty often the only beasts offering are those
too bad to be taken along with travelling mobs
which pass that way. “ Don’t eat beef here,” an
old-established coach-driver once told the writer,
in a matter-of-fact sort of way, as a changing-
house was neared.
“ Timi”: The gigantic lizard
or kumi, reported by cable to have
been seen near Gisborne, in Auck-
land province, M.L., will probably
prove to be a myth. The Maori
traditions of the kumi, unlike
those regarding the moa, vary as
to the shape, size and other
characteristics. Some of the tra-
ditional accounts of the animal
make it a winged dragon, a de-
scription which apparently mixes
the kumi up with the taniwha
myth. Years ago, in Rotorua
district, an old tohunga pointed
out to me the dragon on a
sovereign, and said, “Kumi. 1 ’
In other parts of the country the
legends favor a crqcodilean shape.
I don’t remember hearing of the
discovery in Maoriland of the
remains of any large reptile be-
longing to any recent geological
era. The traditions of the Maoris
re the moa have been verified by
modern scientific research, and
unfossilised eggs and feathers of
the giant bird have been found
occasionally, showing that tho
moa has but recently become ex-
tinct. In view of the accuracy of
the moa traditions, and the hazi-
ness of the natives concerning the
kumi, it will be safe to regar
with caution the reported appear
ance of the reptile.

“ B.G.’' : Recent bunyip-
statements prompt me to describe
what I once saw, when a boy of
14. Travelling down the Lachlan
with my father, we camped just
above a waterhole, nearly a quar-
ter of a mile long. Going for the
horses, just before daybreak, about
30 yards from our camp I heard
a rushing among the reeds, and
saw something lurch out and
plunge into the water. It was, as
described by one of your corres-
pondents, like a Newfoundland
dog, with a shorter and thicker
tail, color a browny-slate. My
father only laughed at me, though he admitted
hearing the plunge.

put out their tongues. Once saw a camel in Cue,

driver dumped two thirty-sixes of beer on his
back - Bgcw t. ! After doing a 20-mile dry stage,
the camel had his head turned round, and his
tongue out, trying to get at the beer ! His affec-
tions had been transferred ! Human, wasn’t it ?
I vvuz wance the champion baker in
Melbun’,” said the shearer’s cook. He had been
dispensing indigestion amongst the shearers for
some days, and never failed to “skite” each

uster ’old one million loafs at a time, and me

and smothered ’im. Well, I comes back, an’

slippery them times), an’ I made all the loafs,

As the others vvuz all bakin’ I couldn’t go through

wuz sellin like smoke until the chap that got the
head-part put the perlice on me, an’ I had to cut.
What yer grinnin’ at, Ginger ? D’yer blanky
well disbelieve me ? ” Ginger protested that he
would sooner disbelieve the “gorspel,” as he
picked up a loaf and went out to the wood-heap

Snake-killing cats ? One Page, of Towns-
ville (N.Q.), has a cat known as a notable
snake-killer. A recent feat Qf puss was to rush up
a tree, bring down a 4-ft. brown snake, and do
battle with it according to approved cat-snake
rules. It dodged every attempted bite, and killed
the enemy. It has now a victory record of over a
dozen snakes.

“Bushman” : Only time a ’roo’s tail does
not touch ground is when hopping. About half
the tail is bare underneath, the skin of this por-
tion being much thickened by contact with the
ground when the animal is at rest or feeding.
When hopping, the tail moves up and down, but
does not touch the ground. A tailless ’roo can get
along all right, but can neither turn short nor
jump fences. When the ’roo is taking off at a
fence it will be seen that, as his body rises, the
tail jerks down —as a man’s hands do when he
jumps with weights.

“ S.S.”: Possibly I am wrong about the
night cry of the lyre-bird. The country I speak
of is the range at Hazlewood, about 10 miles from
Morwell, in Gippsland. There were many of the
birds on the hills, and it was quite common to see
them scurrying about on the side of the gorge
amongst the heath. I had the assurance of the
oldest inhabitant and other local authorities that
the lyres were I’esponsible for the gruesome cries
that made early morning hideous. May say lam
too familiar with the utterances of the ’possum in
all his stages to have been scared by a parliament
of joeys.

There is a freak at Brookville (Vic.), which
a .Melb. showman ought to “lay for”—a sheep
with the head of a native bear. The shape is
accurate and the head is covered with grey hair.
The rest of the animal is clothed in all wool as per
usual. The beast was discovered running with
strays at New Rush Creek. At Tongio and there-
abouts domestic eats with a dash of ’possum are
quite common, and one man boasts a ring-tail
cat.

“M.Gr.T.”: Be women working. In Q.
once saw a man and his wife and family travelling
with a wool-laden waggon and dray. The hus-
band drove waggon, and behind came the dray
driven by the wife, with the tribe of youngsters
stacked on top of the wool. She had seven horses
to handle along a heavily-timbered track, and
well she did it, too. On the camp of a morniug
also she always harnessed up her own team.

“Jeff”: The rams where I am shearing
at present, up Walgett way, are affected with a
disease I have never seen in sheep before. After
the animal is shorn, large, hideous gatherings are
visible, some of them as big as an orange—and
these, if struck hard with a sharp comb, will ex-
plode like a shell. How will the Sydney public
like to know that these rams are fathering mutton
for them to eat ? The disease isn’t the ordinary
“ yolk-boil,” which is not a circumstance to it.

“ 8.W.” : Be “Jinda’s” par (27/8/’9B).
Working up on Kiar Lake (Monaro district) we
made a canoe, and one night at knock-off time we
found that it had drifted to the middle of the
lake. I being the only one who could swim,
stripped and swam out for it; but, when within a
few yards of it, I became suddenly weak; and,
as I had had a hard day’s work, lost my presence
of mind, and began to swim back. Though I
did not dare to waste strength in answering my
mates’ shouts, I presently found that I could not
keep on swimming, and started to “ tread water,”
when I discovered that the depth only came up to
mu neck! Next morning, waded out to the spot
where the canoe had been the night before.

“E.S.S.”: The bottle-tree is one of the
most curious and important trees in Australia.
Inside the hard, rough bark is a soft, vegetable-
like substance on which horses and cattle fatten
quickly. A man may exist on it tor a long time when
nothing else is available. In times of drought it
has saved thousands of sheep and cattle, and
revived many a perishing traveller. One Q. squat-
ter saved his stock in a dry season by running the
water from these trees into troughs for their use.
But it killed a great number of his best trees.
Many are destroyed by being cut into before they
are old enough to contain water. An old hand
will never make this mistake. He looks on the
ground at the butt for the droppings of birds. If
he sees none, he knows there is no water in the
tree ; but if the ground is plentifully spotted—in
goes the axe, and out comes the water. Pity that
this tree is allowed to be so ruthlessly destroyed.
The bunya, or banyan tree, is rigorously pre-
served. Yet it is of far less importance than the
other. The bunya merely yields a limited supply
of food for the aborigine ; the bottle-tree supplies
man and beast with food and water. I should
like to learn what is the girth-measurement of the
largest bottle-tree in Australia, and where
situated ? What age does a bottle-tree attain
before it contains water? How <jo birds get at
the vvater in the trees ? What is the greatest
quantity of water taken from a single tree ?
* * * *
“ Wandywarra ” : The way niggers recover
from wounds is marvellous. In one fight among
the Roebuck Bay (Broome) niggers, one fellow got
a crack which completely bashed the top of his
head in, and gave him the appearance of a
youngster with his “old man’s” boxer on. The
blood spurted from his ears when he got the blow,
but, medical opinion that the ease was
hopeless, he is still about, and as lively as a soldier-
ant. [This is the quality of “ disvulnerability,”
which decreases with the increase of civilisation.]
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin. —Re drink, thrift, and
wage-cutting. In the more populous districts it
is certainly the “steady, saving, servile indi-
vidual” who does the trick every time. He is
mostly married, therefore a “ local bird ” ; has an
“ old woman ” who is a terror for work, a cow or
two, “ tailed ” about the roads by young
Johnny; also a few industrious pigs that make
their own living, and fowls. On Sundays he puts
on a pious expression, a cheap coat, pants that
speak of poverty, polished bluchers, and, as be-
comes his “position,” takes a backseat. After
the ordeal he sees Mr. Pinkothrift about dam
sinking, or Mr. Shillingshice about hedge-trim-
ming, &c. He affects piece-work, “ keeps the
boys going”; gets it low, lives low, works long
hours and makes a“do ” of it. This man isn’t a
passing circumstance ; he has come to stay until
the independent spirit of a free and purely Aus-
tralian nation gets its work in.— Ben Sun.

“Yarrumi”: Thousands of rabbits in
Riverina die every year in logs. If “bunny ’ be

he’ll turn it up and die right away. Did anyone
ever see a rabbit coming out of a log “mid his
tail in de front ? ” Writer never did, and he has
seen as many as the next man.

It was in a cattle-duffing country out West.
Michael Bullock, J.P., was adjudicating in one of
the cases peculiar to the district, and the evidence
against defendant was overwhelming. Conviction
was unavoidable, and the sympathetic heart of
Bullock was racked at the sad prospect ahead for
the evil-doer. Suddenly he burst out: “ Brown,
ye divil, ye’re guilty, and I fine ye two quid
which I’ll pay myself by way of showin’ the
feelin’s of a district that does a good bit o’ duffin’
itself when it ain’t otherwise okkypied.”

“ W.S.T.” : I have solved the bunyip
question to my own satisfaction, at all events.
The bugbear of Australian zoology and literature
is neither more nor less than an “ old-man ” Mur-
ray cod. I have lived in various parts of the
Riverina country, and wherever the bunyip story
prevails—from the Murray’s mouth to the far
away Warrego, there also is the omnivorous,
gorging, apoplectic cod. From his innocent youth
until he attains the bulk of a full-grown porpoise
he will swallow anything he can get his jaws
round—except the bunyip tradition ! Has been
known to nip a drinking magpie off a log and
swallow the bird whole. “A shark,” it is said,
“ will fly from a feather,” but Mr. Murray Cod
“sets” young ducks like a well-broken pointer,
and gobbles them like an ill-broken one. Up here
on the Macquarie there are various bunyip yarns ;
one to the effect that while some blacks were
bathing with their piccannies in the “Deep Hole”
at Ellengerah station, and while one of the gins
was tossing about her babe in the water, the infant
was suddenly snatched from her and taken under.
Certain it is, at all events, that the blacks have
never bathed in the hola from that day to this.

“WillS.”: Talk about pigs! My mate
carried a he-sucker from Zeehan to Mt. Black
(Tas.) and carefully brought him (the pig) up dog-
fashion. Dennis would answer to calls, &0., and
follow, when he grew up, better than most dogs ;
also he would waltz to the strains of “Erins
Green Shore ” till he became giddy. Would
follow us to work and lie on Dan’s bluey in the
smithy during the time we were in the tunnel.
He’d bark at all strangers on the approved watch-
dog system, and once he chased a couple of English
new-chums clean off the “show.” Dennis was
gifted with remarkable intelligence. During the
winter he was invaluable, for we utilised the
warmth of his body to soften the frozen gelignite.
We'd just roll in a bag and he’d lie on it. After
a while moroseness prompted his porkship to
“ sample ” the explosive, and once he got the taste
he became utterly demoralised. He gradually
lost his respectability, and his sole aim in life was
Nobel’s compounds. Fcr a while we encouraged
him in his acquired taste, but soon there was no
limit to his demands for “primers.” Then we
shut down on him, but it was like trying to cut off
the irrigation of the habitual boozer. Dennis
was a confirmed nitro-glycerinist, and, to satisfy
his depraved craving, he would “ surruptiously ”
visit the claims and stoiv away in his interior any
loose explosives he could reach. (Have known
canines ">> the same downward course after once
tasting dynamite). Dennis’s dissipations played
on his nervous system ; he lost his sleep and be-
came a mere shadow. IJltunately he struck up a
warm affection for Connolly’s ancient pack-horse,
who reciprocated ; but this friendship proved
fatal to both parties. One day, in the township, a
dog persisted in yelping at the heels of the flea-
bitten steed, who was attended as usual by Dennis.
Connolly’s moke kicked ostensibly at the dog, but
Dennis took the hoof in the vicinity of the ribs,
and then followed a muffled report, while the
\adjacent atmosphere was strewn with the frag-
ments of three animals. The concussion had ex-
yloded the nitro-glyeerine-laden oarcase of Dennis.
We buried all we could collect of the lamented
pdrker in a ineat-tin, and Connolly talks of suing
us] for the loss of his horse.

rocks and smashed it. Remember also a swag-
man, a few years ago, being caught by foot in a
trap near Lake Albacutya (Vic.), but, as he could
not possibly open the trap by himself, he perished
miserably.
* * * *
“Porthos”: I fail to see the refining
effects of near communion with Nature on “ a bold
peasantry, their country’s pride.” The men look
as though they have been chasing a sixpence to
the point of exhaustion, and the women are pre-
maturely wrinkled, and have the wan, pensive
look that the bush seems to fix on all that come
under its influence. The youths differ from town
hoodlums only in want of alertness, and a greater
monotony in their curses. Living in the country
gets you no nearer to nature. The countryman’s
work is as commercial and his thoughts as mer-
cenary as the townman’s. In fact, familiarity
with nature is usually antagonistic to that study
of and wonder at her works which have a distinctly
refining influence.
* * m *
Thornton, of Tower Hill (a well-known N.
Queenslander), tells how he went down to the
station lagoon the other day and shot a black duck
and an ibis. Examined the birds, and found
them covered with tick larvse. Went then and
inspected the cattle (supposed to be clean) feeding
beside the lagoon, and found them also tick-bear-
ing. If Australia wants to block the tick she’ll
have to run up a solid granite wall to the height
of about 15,000 feet above sea-level round every
infested district, and soak every bakblox swagger
in turpentine for a week.

THE TYPEWRITER IN WESTRALIA.

Two Socialists.—Address, “The Australia
Hotel."
It is enough to make G. Republican Dibbs stare,
and rub his glasses, and read again, and damn
Chicago under his breath and his big cigar. lor
G. Republican Dibbs is a fine fellow without any
brains in particular for the understanding of
economic tenets and theories ; and he belongs _to
the last old Tory generation, the generation which
is used to lump Socialists and Anarchists and dy-
oiamite-throwers together in the same enemies-of-
society lump, and to thank God that those
villainous new-fangled ideas don’t go down here
in the pure land of freedom where Suffield
remains in prison on evidence that wouldn t
convict a dog; and where a Minister of the
Crown seeks to gain a sea.t for his party by
telling of the possible expenditure of Govt, money
— i.e., by suggesting bribery and corruption. The
Bulletin therefore explains once more to G.
Republican Dibbs and the old-fogey generation
that a socialist is the very antipodes of an anar-
chist ; that the last thing he dreams of is a
dynamite bomb; that his proper _ address is
the Australia Hotel, because Socialism aims to
level things up to the Australia Hotel standard
of comfortable living ; and that his chief object in
life is to stop levelling things down under the
modern system which sweats seamstresses and re-
d ices the labourer to the state of a machine for
transforming flesh-and-blood into Fat Man’s bank-
-1 ialances.
G. Republican Dibbs can afford to smoke big
cigars because he gets <£l3oo a year from the com-
munity for his labor as manager of N.S.W*
Savings Bank. What every socialist wants to do is
to give every man in the community a chance to
fet £l3OO a year from the con munity in return for
is labor for the community, so that Jack Smith,
Tom Jones, and Bill Robinson can smoke big
cigars also if they want to, and the good living at
the Australia Hotel will be shared by every man-
jack and woman-jill in the country. And if that
is unattainable, then every socialist wants to get as
near that as the comniunity can—wants to even-up
the extremes of civilisation, prosaically speaking;
and, more poetically speaking, to make a better
world for better men. It is a dream, perhaps, but
what a glorious dream ! And that it isn’t alto-
gether a dream is shown by the fact that some of
it—even a good deal of it—is fast becoming reality.
And Sidney and Beatrice Webb, who work to-
gether in socialist books and socialist life, are
helping towards the realisation.
Sidney Webb is a plump little fellow in a tweed
suit, with pince-nez to help his blue eyes, and a
brown tuft of hair on the chin to match his
1 irown moustache. He doesn’t look so brainy as
his books—hardly anybody does. I cannot say if
Mrs. Webb looks as brainy as her books ; for Mr.
Webb regretted that at the moment “she wasn’t
visible,” and next day they left the Australia
Hotel for the land of “Socialism in Our Time ” (in
theory), and——well, I was sorry not to see Mrs.
Webb. For as Beatrice Potter she investigated
and wrote quite as much as her husband; and
the married firm takes rank as a chief
source of Socialist literature in England. That
is, /act-literature ; they collate statistics, and com-
pare ideas, and provide much material for Fabian
I ions to gnash their teeth over. ,
“When I’m at home,” said Mr. Webb, “I
write in the morning, and—as member of the Lon-
don County Council for Deptford—help to manage
the affairs of millions of people in the after-
noon. And when you come to think that all Aus-
tralasia hasn’t 4Ji- millions of people, you will see
there is some scope for the employment of intelli-
gence, if one has any. It is a striking thing now
that all over England young men who would
formerly have sought a career in Par-
liament are now busying themselves with
municipal affairs. The problems presented to
them are, if I may say so, in their way quite as
difficult* and quite as important to the well-being
of the people, as even those which Parliament has
to deal with. And the size of the constituencies
involved elevates municipal office to a dignity and
responsibility which attract the very best men.
Lord Rosebery, for example, has been at the same
time Foreign Minister and L.C.C. Chairman;
Lord Carrington leaves the governorship of
N.S. Wales to come and help govern London.
I’m glad to hear The Bulletin has advocated
amalgamation of the Sydney municipalities—it is
the obvious thing to do, and you would find it
would greatly raise the standard and value of
municipal life. We are devoting special attention
to Australasian municipal institutions, with a view
to incorporating the results in a book on British
Municipalism. Of course, our visit is mainly a
pleasure trip.
“You ask me to tell you how socialism is pro-
gressing in England. Well, the chief thing to
note about the progress is that there is progress all
along the line—the whole national mind seems to
be acquiring and employing socialistic ideas. It
is not so much in the passage of any single measure
of legislation, or in the success of any single idea,
that we see an advance; but in the general diffusion
of socialistic theories and the gradual application of
them by all classes of the community. England is
by no means standing still: we have a manhood
franchise for municipalities (and to some extent a
womanhood franchise too); and the Parliament
ary franchise is now practically based on manhood
suffrage. And, while you in N.S. Wales are still
without a complete enforcement of employers’
liability for accidents to workmen, we in England
have the Workmen’s Compensation Act which
came into force this year, and which protects the
workman in the case of every accident —even when
occasioned by his own negligence. And, mind
you, this Act was passed by a Conservative
Government. I don’t say it is just that an em-
ployer should be compelled to pay a workman
damages for an accident caused by the workman’s
fault: we say the State should pay, because it
isn’t to the State’s interest that a workman’s wife
and family should suffer when the breadwinner is
disabled. But a Conservative Government makes
the employer pay.
“ Then you have not yet free education in N.S.
Wales, unless parents incur the stigma of pauper-
ism. Practically every child in England can get
free education. We gave education to 6,000,000
of children in England last year at a cost of some-
thing like .£6,000,000. And, coming from great
things to smaller, to show how we look after the
recreation of the poor, I may say that in most of
the parks of London any team of lads can get a
cricket-pitch reserved any Saturday. And the
County Council paid £BOOO last year for public
music in the parks. These are some items result-
ing from the Progressive policy—and, as you
know, the Progressives got a smashing maiority
at last elections.”
And so, and so on, Mr. Webb talked, Know-
ing his subject thoroughly, finding his words
as he fingered his watch - chain, and leaving
a definite impression of sincerity armed with
many bluebooksful of “informations.” At 39
years of age, one of 700 or 800 Fabians who com-
bine thought with action, literary speculation with
practical effort, he is and is going to be one of the
most useful men in England. But I was sorry not
to see Mrs. Webb.

“J.L.F.”: It wasn’t “Patsy King”
that kept the Southern Cross Hotel in West Mel-
bourne and always had a big bundle of electors’
rights in his cupboard. The house was
in King-street, but the landlord’s name was
Ring. I knew him well. The house is still there
at the corner of King-street and Latrobe-street,
alongside the old Immigration depot. Captain
McMahon also got Ring’s votes —and they were
many.
Mr. Frank Senior, who has been chemist
to the N.S. Wales Governors for many years past,
has conferred a blessing upon the citizens of his
country by discovering a wonderful cure for head-
ache. The new remedy cures instantaneously, is
very palatable, and perfectly harmless to both
ladies and gentlemen. Moreover, it is perfectly
effectual, no matter what the cause of the head-
ache may be. This cure, the result of years of
experience, can be had in 3 sizes, by post 1/4, 2/6,
3/8., in N.S.W. only. To all the other colonies
1/8, 2/8, 3/8. Beware of imitations. Only address:
Senior, chemist, 246 George-street Sydney.*
Dear Bulletin. — Re your notion that only
certain kinds of persons cling to the archaic night-
shirt. Mark Twain, in “ More Tramps Abroad,”
describes his experiments with pyjamas and his
glad return to the night-shirt. Possibly to some
minds the pyjama seems immoral. Remember I
had a boyish idea that it was “ fast ” to have your
hair cut by a regular barber, and absolutely
wicked to eat oysters. I may have been right,
but now have doubts. Takes all sorts to make a
world ; what is one man’s night-shirt ib another
man’s pyjamas.—T.P. [Memo, by sub-editor :
Pour times, in the course of a more or less un-
ornamental existence, have roused a doctor in the
dead of night and brought him down in undress.
Three came down in night shirts. One—l wanted
him for a mine-accident in Queensland—came to
the door dressed in nothing at all but a large
mosquito-bite. Didn’t catch one medico in pyja-
mas. What does the profession think of the
relative merits of the two garments ?]
