Circus Life in Australia.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Two men sit opposite me in a small tent, hung
with harness, whips and bridles. The floor of the
tent is planked and carpeted. Three or four
travelling trunks and portmanteaux lie around,
bearing the brands of hotels in different parts of
the world —Cooktown, New York, Vienna, Rome,
Copenhagen, London. A table and half-a-dozen
chairs, some glasses, an open box of cigars, and a
couple of bottles of real good whisky complete the
furniture. Through the flaps of the tent, pinned
up to the top, I look into a larger tent, brightly
lit—a monster tent, its roof flapping lazily in the
wind like the wings of some great bird. The tent
is crowded with people, and the band is playing a
waltz. The two men are like and yet unlike—one
Don FitzGerald.
stout and robust, the other
thin and wiry; but both pre-
s< nt the aspect of eneVgetio
men in repose, in the pleasant
halting - place between two
periods of action. These are
the Fitz Gerald Brothers —
Dan and Tom—whose names
are Australian, as Barnum’s
is American. They are rest-
ing during the happy time
when Tom has counted the
receipts, and Dan has finished
his Bronchos and the pony
Commodore. They only re-
main to superintend the great-
est of all circus feats—the
taking down, packing and
transporting of a plant and
company as large as an army
corps ; and to negotiate a feat
infinitely more delicate but
not less important—the feat
of being interviewed for The
Bulletin.
m v w
A very small but very black boy, in tights, with
gauze-painted wings and butterfly horns, a bright-
faced intelligent boy, asks my choice, and taking a
little soda with it, I start to extract information.
Tom, the stout one, starts the conversation. It is
his rdle, as agent in advance, or proprietor-in-
advance, on all great occasions, such as entering a
big city, or being interviewed. He is not an
archaeologist, he says, and cannot pose as an
authority on the antiquities of the circus, though
the ancients did know a thing or two.
* • * •
“There’s that Greek vase in the British Museum
which my brother and I saw a couple of years
ago—quite three thousand years old. It belongs
to the Elgin marbles, and has sculptured upon it
an acrobat walking upon his hands. Would we
give him an engagement to-day ? Oh, yes! he
was a beautifully-made fellow, but he would be a
little behind in these days of one-hand balancing.
Here also is an Egyptian bas-relief, very likely
much older than the Greek vase. Probably this
vase was the ancient way of advertising, as we do
to-day with colored posters.”
* * * *
Dan ( loq ): “Colored posters, as we know them
now, are, I believe, the product of the cleverest
brain in the circus business to-day—the brain of
James Bailey, of Barnum and Bailey’s. Bailey
came out to Australia 20 odd years ago with
Cooper and Bailey’s circus. He evolved the
poster. One of our former agents, Harry Lyons,
"John Bums."
a Tasmanian, who
went round the world
with Blondin, used to
tell the story of Bailey
studying out his pos-
ters by the aid of
prints of animals,
beasts, birds, &c., cut-
ting out a picture
here and there and
arranging them as he
desired them in the
poster. A peculiarity
was that Jim Bailey
always selected the
animals in their most
bloodthirsty-moments, when they were tearing some-
thing, crunching their prey, preparing to spring,
or in combat with other wild beasts or birds. The
result was that Bailey’s posters were full of action
and compelled and attracted the attention of the
passer-by—which is the supreme object of a
poster.”
» • • *
“We were the guests of Mr. Bailey on our trip
to America” (Tom takes a hand), “and travelled
for a week with the gigantic organisation known
as Barnum’s, of which Bailey is now sole proprie-
tor. It moves on a special train, with dining, sleep-
ing, freight and menagerie cars. The people and
animals live on the trains. In season, the circus
plays twice a day—afternoon and evening. Besides
this, there is a costume procession through the
streets every forenoon. After the performance in
the evening, the tents come down as if by magic ;
everything is packed on the trains, the people go
to bed, and then the great procession moves on to
the next town. We are trying to introduce the
system to Australia. Oh, yes, we are just begin-
ning to feel big enough. Well, no, we’re not quite
as big as Barnum’s yet, but we’re not too bad for
a young country. As it grows, no doubt we’ll
grow too.”
* * • *
“I remember,” says Dan, in a reminiscent way,
and looking into the clouds of cigar smoke, “the
Cooper-Bailey show when it came to Australia. It
was our first big circus. Eh? Well, yes, it did
alter my ambition, and probably changed my
career. I was then a pupil-teacher in a school, at
Bathurst. I resolved to be a circus-clown instead
of a bushranger. Oh, yes; all the boys wanted to
be bushrangers. I was young then. Where was
I ? Ah ! In America they say that a circus is an
organised robbery, as Disraeli said that a Con-
servative Government is an organised hypocrisy.
Cooper-Bailey’s didn’t quite come up to that. But
they brought over a rowdy
gang of workmen, ready for
anything, who, at the least sign
of trouble, turned out with
pick-handles, and struck and
spared not. Their first esca-
pade was on Circular Quay, at
landing, where they came into
collision with the water-police,
and sturdy old senior-sergeant
Prince Little, now dead, let
them know a few nasty ‘ holts, *
in spite of hip-pockets and re-
volvers. One of the tent-men
was Jack Graham, the wrestler
(now in Melbourne), one of the
most courageous lion - tamers
we ever had. He had been privateering
or filibustering in Cuba, and picked up
with the circus to get out of America. I be-
lieve most of that gang had to get out of America.
One of the performers was glad to get out of Aus-
tralia. That was the man who played the big
steam Calliope piano. He shot a man on Mel-
P. T. Barnum.
bourne wharf from the deck of
the steamer just as she steamed
away from the wharf with the
circus on board. He swam
ashore, and escaped to America.
Eh! Oh, the usual cause.
Cherchez la femme t ”
• * *
We clinked glasses once more.
“Yes,’’said Tom, “I have heard
some good yarns about Bailey.
He used to go about with his
coat - pocket full of sovereigns, and jingle
them as he walked along. Probably this
was to remind him that he was a
millionaire. Dangerous? Of course. But he
always had four picked boxers and wrestlers
within arm’s-length of him. There is a story of
Bailey, for which, however, I won’t vouch. The
circus got to a big township up South, and the
story goes that the people would not pay the
prices, but stood in crowds outside. A few stones
were thrown that night at the tent, and a horse
was cut. A wire went down to the capital, and
about 20 first-class boxing-men were on the ground
next day. These were distributed about amongst
the crowd by Bailey, and instructed to capture the
first stone-thrower and bring him into the tent.
The performance began. Bailey went about jin-
gling his sovereigns, when presently a man was
brought in, his pockets full of stones. Bailey said,
sternly, ‘You darned scoundrel, you might have
hit a woman with one of them, ’ and, while the
man held the culprit, Bailey, a delicate little man,
kicked him till he could kick no longer. The man
was then allowed to go into the show. No. He
was not offered a seat, or, at any rate, he didn’t
accept one. We’ve never had any bad rows. The
American circuses carried it with a somewhat high
hand in Australia, and perhaps it led on occasions
to justifiable retaliation. ”
* * * «
At this point we were interrupted by the arrival
of a stout, dark-complexioned gentleman, who was
introduced as Billy Jones, the Grand Old Man of
Australian circus-business. He pleaded guilty to
nearly (50 summers, but can still carry a somewhat
Falstaffian figure over in a back somersault. He
took his with water—not soda. When he had
gone, Dan again became reminiscent. “ When I
was a very little boy in a very little bush township,
I saw a ‘dodger,’ or circus-bill, tacked on a gum-
tree. It represented a man standing on one leg on
a madly-bounding horse, and holding out the other
leg with his hand. While a small gang of boys
were discussing the possibility of this amazing
fea,t, a stout, dark, young man with curly black
hair rode by, leading two horses, and said, ‘Boys,
you must come to the circus to-night. There’s a
circus at Mrs. Taylor’s.’ That was Billy Jones.
Twenty years afterwards, when we were full-
blown circus-proprietors, Billy Jones joined us,
and has been with us ever since. He has an un-
paralleled fund of anecdote of Australian circus-
life, knows every bit of harness and property in
Commodore.
the show, and has an unequalled knowledge of
Australian roads. His knowledge of Australia is
like Sam Weller’s of London, ‘extensive and pecu-
liar. ’ Drop him from ‘the clouds anywhere in Aus-
tralia, he will probably say, ‘Let’s see, there’s a
turn-off here to the left somewhere to go to Bungle-
bong ’; or, ‘I was here fifteen years agp, and I
think you take the road to the right ’; or, possi-
bly, ‘You see that gum-tree there? Well, when I
was here before that gum-tree was only a sapling.’
He was with Rowe’s Circus when they left the
Ovens Goldfield with horses shod with gold shoes.
He travelled with Bully Hayes when _ that bucca-
neer took to show-life through love of a circus-
girl, and he saw the same gentleman nearly
drowned in the Bay of Islands, Maoriland, when
a boat upset and he had to swim ashore.”
* * * *
The FitzGeralds made their first big punch in
N.S.W. in 1889. From that time they have gone
on steadily improving in knowledge and experi-
ence. They have travelled round the world twice,
and have ransacked America and Europe in search
of novelties. They have seen nearly every circus,
menagerie, and music-hall in the U.S., Great
Britain, France, Germany, Italy, Austria, Nor-
way, and Sweden.
* * * *
Dan (foq.) : “On our first visit to America we
were the guests for a week of the three great
American circuses, Bailey’s, Sells Brothers, and
Ringling Brothers—all monstrous affairs. We
think the shifting by train and boat of our 120
horses, three or four tents, twenty cages of animals,
and 150 people a huge undertaking, but you should
see the big American shows ! The whole tiling
moves like a piece of clockwork. Oh, yes, we
consider our big tent and impedimenta take some
organisation to transport. But we have got it
down very fine, and now the thing moves auto-
matically. Of course we trust to the skill of our
people—such men, for example, as Yon der Meh-
den, who, besides being the finest cornet-player in
the world, is our general manager, also our most
responsible man. Then there is Frank M. Jones,
a Sydney boy, who, besides his pneumatic clown-
ing, makes our forage-contracts. Then there is
Morns, agent,
a most versatile
man, who may
be seen one day
posting bills on
gum - trees in
the backblocks,
and a few
months after-
wards convers-
ing amicably
in German and
drinking dry
gin with the
heir - apparent
to the throne of Austria - Hungary. Oh,
yes ; Prince Ferdinand drank dry gin in
my dressing-room when on his visit to Syd-
ney. It was evidently not his first circus, and he
appeared to know his way about dressing-rooms.
On my last trip to Europe I entered into business
relations with Herr Carl Hagenbeck, the great
animal trainer, who owns a Zoo at Hamburg, and
has agents scouring the forests of India and Africa
to recruit his menagerie, now the biggest in the
world. From him we purchased the lion-and-ele-
phant act last year, also the tiger and pony. W-
hope to have further relations
with him by-and-bye, and
thinks later on we shall as-
tonish Australians even more
than we have up to now. Well,
yes, our present co. is one of
the best ever brought to Aus-
tralia, in every sense, and has
been one of the most success-
ful. Last co. was most suc-
cessful also, except for the
death of Peart, the plucky
James Bailey.
diver. Madame Adelina Antonio was the
greatest aerial star, in our opinion, ever seen in
Australia, and, moreover, one of the most charm-
ing women.”
• • * •
In reply to a rather personal, not to say im-
pertinent question, Tom says —“Do we take any
interest in anything pertaining to the philosophy
of the circus ? Well, _ I didn’t know there was
much philosophy in a circus, except in the monkey
cages, where we had for a long time a chimpanzee,
named Mrs. Tang, of great age and supernatural
wisdom. You should have seen her getting a
patent stopper out of a lemonade bottle and drink-
ing the liquid. She died in most affecting cir-
cumstances near a place called Riverton, Maori -
land. We had camped out one Sunday, and all
hands were round her when she died—quite a
family group, as it were—all the ladies in tears,
men deeply affected. She was left out one cold
night in a wind storm, and got pneumonia. We
tried to get a doctor to prescribe for her, but they
Egyptian bas-relief.
all thought it
unprofessional,
so we could
only keep her
warm and await
results. Her
death was a most
human affair,
and I shall never
forget the pathe-
tic look in her
eyes. What lite-
rature there is of
the circus we
have it. Here are Van Hare’s ‘ Recollec-
tions, ’ which are most interesting. Look at
this book by Monsieur Hugues le Roux, illu-
strated by M. Jules G-arnier—he whose Rabelais
pictures created such a sensation in London in
1890, and who had the distinction of having four
of those pictures condemned by the English censor
to be destroyed. No, they were not destroyed.
The French Government intervened, and made a
diplomatic affair of it. These two—le Roux and
Gamier—have collaborated in this work, called
‘Acrobats and Mountebanks.’ They deal with
French circus life in all its phases—fairs, entre-
sorts, theatre booths, trainers, tamers, equestrians,
equilibrists, gymnasts, clowns—in fact, they ex-
haust the subject. Here is the only circus novel,
‘Les Freres Zemganno,’ by Edmond de Gon-
court, and there is ‘L’Art d’Equitation,’ by
Fillis, of Paris, uncle of Frank Fillis, who was
out here a few years ago.”
• • * •
“Yes,” broke in Dan, “if we had time to write
out our adventures, no doubt they would prove
good reading. About the menagerie? Oh, that
was added long after we made our first success.
We bought from the Sydney Zoo a cage contain-
ing a lioness and three cubs. One of those cubs is
the blind lion, ‘John Burns ’; another, the lioness,
Queenie, has been immortalised by the brush of
Miss Mary Stoddard, and the portrait by that
promising artist is now in the Sydney National
Gallery. We have had a number of cubs born in
captivity with us. The first litters, as a rule, did
not thrive, but the second ones did. Accidents !
No, we’ve been very fortunate with our beasts.
At least, we’ve never had any of the public injured
by them. Humphreys, our first tamer, was bitten
several times, but he seemed to like it. The
animals hated him. After a lapse of five years he
turned up lately in the menagerie tent in Maori-
land, and as soon as ever the blind lion, • ‘ Burns, ’
heard his voice, he went almost mad to get at him.
Jack Graham was scratched on the hand once, but
that was in play. He was always on the best of
terms with the lions.
* * * *
“We don’t often have to shift animals from one
cage to another, but there’s no danger in it, under
our system. We have reduced it to a science.
The Man-eater.
No, emphatically
—the Tantanoola
tiger did not escape
from our niena-
ferie. As Mrs.
’rig would say,
‘we don’t believe
there ain’t no sich
a tiger.’ Here’s
how the chang-
ing’s done. The
cages are placed
on waggons side
by side and lashed together. The doors
slide upwards. A man stands on top of
the cage, and when the signal is given
he lifts the door up. The animals almost
invariably scamper into the other cage, probably
in the belief that they are escaping. If they
don’t move ? Oh, we get Yon der Mehden to play
soft andantes on the cornet outside the new cage.
If that doesn’t entice them he stands behind the
old cage and blows a terrific blast. They always
go after** that. A friend of ours had a bad time
lately. We were shipping 3ome lions and tigers to
Hagenbeck in Hamburg, and we left instructions
with a friend to have a bigger cage built for a male
lion and change him into it for the voyage. He
decided to do the changing at night, when every-
thing would be nice and quiet and no one about.
The cages were in some stables in a back street in
the Glebe. When our friend arrived about eight
o’clock in the evening he fourijd the animals had set
up a dreadful roaring and ha/d attracted children,
as he assures us, to the number
of several millions. They ranged
upwards from infants m arms,
and had all brought their
mothers with them. He had to
employ several men to keep the
kids beyond scratching dis-
tance of the cages, and for an
hour and a half he stood bathed
in a cold sweat and with his
heart in his mouth watching the
trollies and cages being lashed
together. The fine, new cage
had a bed of nice, clean straw
in it. The lion, a splendid fel-
low from the Adelaide Zoo,
stood at the door of his cage during the prepara-
tions, and hungrily watched a plump baby carried
in its mother’s arms just near the bars of the new
cage. As soon as the doors slid up he simply
dived for that baby, and found himself in the new
cage, glaring horribly at the infant, which he evi-
dently made sure of. My friend has since refused
a thousand pounds to superintend the changing of
another lion in the vicinity of the Glebe. He
swears he will never forget to his dying day the
contrast between the furious rage of the lion in the
cage and the calm, fatuous face of the mother
pointing out the great beast to the smiling baby.
Oh, there was no danger, but still
• * * •
Tom resists all attempts to draw upon his stores
of circus erudition, refuses to discuss the lion-
taming exploits of Samson and Hercules, or the
Algerian adventures of Tartarin of Tarascon. His
eyes twinkle, however, at the recollection of a night
spent in Brisbane in company with a weii-known
Sydney merchant whose exploits in tiger-hunting
are world-renowned. They passed the time very
pleasantly exchanging stories —the merchant’s re-
lating to the noble beast in his native jungles,
while Tom’s were apropos of the same noble
animal in captivity and on tour.
* * * *
“ Moving a great show like ours ! Yes, we could
give points in transport to some army men. If
ever this country goes to war we’ll undertake the
transport of the army—
and, if necessary, let the
menagerie loose on the
enemy. Why not let the
clowns and the advance-
agents loose on them, you
say ? That would be hor-
rible cruelty, not sanc-
tioned by the rules of
the Geneva Convention.
We have to provision and
transport men, horses,
and animals, and put up
and take down the great
tents on an average three
times a week all the year
round. Yet, in the old
days, when we travelled
in waggons, we have come
into town at 7 p.m., and
have opened at B—a8 —a tent
up, seats erected, ring
dug, everyone fed, and
house packed in an hour.
At 11 o’clock the same
night every stick was on
the waggons again ready
to start at 6 a.m. the
Tom Fitz-Gerald.
following morning. Oh, yes, we have heard the
lines —
Fold our tents like the circus
And silently steal away. •
But not exactly that way. Latterly, as we havt
grown big, we travel by steamers (mostly specially
chartered) and trains. This gives us more free-
dom, and enables us to avoid bad weather and
catch the proper seasons. For instance, to begin
our Queensland tour, which is just concluded, we
jumped by steamer from Auckland (Maoriland) to
Cooktown (Queensland) —a tidy leap of nineteen
days.”

“ Episodes in circus history?” (it is Dan who is
speaking); “some are most pic-
turesque. When Old Man Wilson
came to Australia, his partner was
a delicate-looking man, with a
pale face and dark moustache, lie
was called Omar Kingsley. This
same Omar Kingsley was the
famous rider who had for years
masqueraded as a woman, under
the name of Ella Zoyara. He
had been courted and run after,
had offers of marriage by the dozen,
and was once abducted by a German
princeling, who had fallen in love with ‘him-her.’
All the time, he was a married man himself, and
his wife —Sally Stickney I think her name was— re-
sembled him, and often impersonated him on State
occasions. We have never gone in for ‘freaks,’
though in every town we are offered a two-headed
or six-legged calf. We don’t like freaks or dan-
gerous acts, and we’re not taking any more sensa-
tional divers. Here’s a perfectly horrible freak
story, told by Van Hare. Probably it’s a lie, but
never mind. A celebrated freak, a ‘ nondescript ’
or dog-faced lady, Julia Pastrana, married her
manager. He exhibited her through Europe. She
died m child-birth in St. Petersburg, and her
husband sold her body to a celebrated doctor for
£5OO. The doctor embalmed her so naturally that
the husband bought her back again for £BOO, and
afterwards exhibited her. I think that story per-
fectly horrible. Don’t you ? ”
* * * *
“If ever we are ruined,” said Tom, eonfiden-
tially, as we walked out
through the big tent, now
empty and lighted by only
one torch, “it will be
through the habit my
brother has of pensioning
off horses that grow old in
our service. His old-age
pension scheme already
causes us to pay for horses
left behind in all parts
of Australia and Maori-
land, where their old age
is soothed in nice grass
paddocks. When the
bills come in annually Dan
swears, but pays. He is
a horse-lover par excel-
lence. As to Commo-
dore, he is a Fitz Gerald
Brother ex officio. In France last year Dan had
some nice steak at his lodgings in Moulin Hubert,
and he relished it immensely. Afterwards they
told him it was from the ‘ boucherie chevaline, ’ or
horse butcher’s. His feelings may be better ima-
gined than described. Why, he would much
rather have eaten me/”

“ HermeB The battle of Omdurman and
the dramatic grimness thereof : Contemplate this
spectacle of the culmination of human heroism and
fanatical recklessness withering away under the
scientific deadliness of magazine rifles and machine
artillery. And the Khalifa’s wild flight hunted
neck and tail for 30 miles by British cavalry, then
by Arabs on dromedaries—dropping his favorite
wives one by one as the pursuit grew hotter—must
have turned to gall the mellow memories of his 10
years’ glory and made him envy the brother who
had died a brave death in the last rally round the
Black Standard.
Line upon line. A common remark on entering
a well-stocked ironmongery store is—“ What a number
of lines you keep.” This is true, but the art in keeping
a stock is just to keep the very line your customer wants
at the price he wants. In McLEAN BROS. & RIGQ
LTD. Melbourne and Sydney warehouses you will find
the very article you are looking for. Implements for the
farm, ironmongery for the house, guns for the sportsman,
fishing tackle for the fisherman at eye-opener prices. If
you can’t inspect write for quotations.*
Broken Hill, a stronghold of trades-
unionism once, is struggling desperately to get up
a trades hall; but unionism is so parched a thing
there now that, as the committee will not borrow
or run into debt, only a brick and a sheet of iron
can be got at a time. The real smashing blow
that unionism got was not the strikes but the
mines’ rule which compelled men to belong to a
union before they were given work. While the
rule existed propaganda work ceased altogether,
with the result that when the rule was abolished
it was found that two-thirds of those who were
members of the unions weren’t earnest unionists,
if unionists at all. Then the best organisers
either went to Parliament or found it more pro-
fitable to leave the district, and so the unions be-
came comparative wrecks. Now they have en-
gaged ex-M.P. Black to run a weekly propagandist
paper and do other organising.

A Wail From the North.
Dear Bulletin. —The latest plan of S.A. to save
the Northern Territory from ruin is to make over
a portion of it to Japan. I have been a resident
of the Territory for 20 years on and off, and have
witnessed various patriotic attempts to flood the
country with an opium-smoking, leprosy-stricken
rase : the present Government seems to have
got the disease in a more aggravated form. Its
continual howl is that white labor will not do in
the Territory; but white labor has never had an
opening in the Territory, except as regards the
railway. There are plenty of white men work-
ing on the line at present for 7s. 6d. a day, and
they do well enough and are quite contented, only
for the disgrace of having to work alongside
Chows. I know men who have been working
hard here for the last 25 years, all the time
struggling against the Chinese invasion—fighting,
struggling for a miserable existence on the poorer
outside fields, where they have to live on hard
tucker and bad water for half the year, sooner
than come in and intermix with the Govt, ’s yellow
pets on the inside fields, and also petitioning
Govt, to keep _ the Chows back from fields that
a nor’-west fossicker would rot be found dead on.
Government floods the country with aliens, and
crowds the European out, and then says white
labor won’t do in the territory. Well, they have
been nearly 30 years trying to colonise with
Chinkies, and the result is that the country is
poorer than it was 20 years ago, with the excep-
tion of a few miles of a railway that does not pay
working-expenses. If Govt, had had the slightest
inclination to make a start in the right direction, !
it should have arranged that Europeans only
should be employed making this line (at least half
of whom would have settled in the country, and
the rest have spent their money in it), in-
stead of allowing the contractors to employ !
cheap labor and make a pile out of the job,
not one penny of which benefited the Territory.
S.A. Government has effectually killed the goose
up here. Look at the thousands that have flocked
into Westralia within the last four years, yet some
parts of the Territory were quite as rich in gold and
richer in other minerals than Westralia; but the
Chows have been allowed the run of the goldfields,
not only alluvial, but the reefs, which they rooted
out down to water - level the whole length of
the golden belt. Then they filled in the country,
and no one now knows where the rich patches
were. The only mining hope for the future of the
Territory is strong companies with plenty of
capital and machinery to prospect the reefs below
water level. At present only two or three bona-
fide co’s have started, with good results so far ;
and they are getting more extensive machinery
with the intention of doing their utmost to bring
the dead to life. If Government will only look on
and not flood the country with more yellow
vermin, I am convinced that the companies will
come to prefer European labor.
As to the Territory climate, most southern people
have got the-idea that it is very unhealthy, but we
have little sickness here except fever in the mildest
form. Very few die of it, though most get an
occasional touch. My experience of the average
Australian is that he can inure himself to any
climate and can work there just as well as any
alien; but Govt. h,as never held out the slightest
inducement for settlers to come in. There are
hundreds of miles of blacksoil flats on the rivers
that will grow almost anything—rivers two and
three hundred miles long running all the year, alive
with fish, and the country around swarming with
game. If some of the other-side cockies, who are
struggling on small selections that will grow only
pumpkins, once got a footing over here on some of
the rivers, it would be a paradise for them. Goats
breed like rabbits here, and are equal to mutton
for the table. The place also is a natural
nursery for poultry of all sorts, and pigs do well.
Also, there’s plenty of outside country to run stock
on. To sum uji, in my opinion the only thing to save
the Territory is “Federation ” —or “ Separation.”
We have plenty of able men who have spent the
best part of their life here ; who know the re-
sources and requirements of the country, and
could run it to far better advantage than a South-
ern Government, whose only idea is that the Ter-
ritory is a receptacle for them to empty the refuse
of the world in.-

Caldwell moves in S.A. Assembly in favor
of the discontinuance of compulsory vaccination.
Premier Kingston declines to bring in a Bill on
the subject this season, but is believed to side with
the abolitionists.
What is the difference between the man
who buys the votes of 40 electors for a promise of
beer and a Minister of the Crown who buys the 40
votes of a township by promising the voters a
bridge over the creek ?
It is now difficult to discover which of the
two gentlemen is more anxiously endeavoring to
consolidate Lyne’s leadership of the N.S.W.
Opposition—Lyne, or G. H. Reid. Reid cer-
tainly has most to gain.
Alfred Deakin turns pious in his middle-
age, and the spiritualistic unbeliever—as was —
now wants to introduce religious teaching into
Vic. State schools. Deakin by name—is he to
wind up as deacon by nature ?

milk the State cow.

The Reid Government came out of the
censure debate with a majority of just four (in-
eluding pairs there were 61 for the Government
and 57 against it). Ex-lawyer Meagher, of Dean
celebrity, was one-fourth of the Reid majority,
Four isn’t much of a working majority in
a House where 119 out of 125 members are
accounted for, and the Wriggling Ministiy hangs
on by a hair.
Vic. House of 48 Fatmen cast out the
Woman’s Vote Bill largely because “the majority
of the women of the country have expressed no
desire to vote.” The same House of Lards has
again and again smashed One Man One , Vote de-
spite the fact that several successive Parliaments
been returned pledged to that measure.
Whether the country expresses or doesn t express
a desire its all the same.
Farmer Copley (renegade Liberal) in’Bs
told S.A. Leg. Council that without payment of
members he could not continue to sit in Parliament.
And when opposing the Bill granting “ House-
hold Suffrage” to man and wife for the Upper
Chamber he had the front to remark that the King-
stem Ministry is made up of professional politicians
and that he has no faith whatever in them when
they are dealing with national affairs.
Another claimant for the Federal capital-
ship : this time Port Augusta, which gets some-
what near (though still a good way off) The Bul-
letin’s idea of a site in the Mac Donnell Ranges in
Central Australia. Distances to capitals are: to
Adelaide 260 miles, Melbourne 742, Sydney 1020
(this via Broken Hill), Brisbane 1450 (this via
Birdsville), and Perth 1450. To the objection that
it’s on the sea, the local reply is that this particu-
lar sea is a gulf with a fairly narrow entrance.
There is a lot of humanity and horse-sense
about Dr. Cullen’s Bill, now before N.S.W. Parlia-
ment, by which no “female person’s” sewing-
machine or mangle shall be liable to distraint for
rent. But why not make the freedom from dis-
traint cover all tools of trade, as well as furniture
to a certain value? The poor music-teacher’s
jangling piano, or the hash-lady’s stock of tables
and beds and pots and pans, or a wash-tub or
embroidery-frame may also be a lone female’s sole
means of livelihood.
The principle of assessing on unimproved
values by municipalities has now been before
S. A. Parliament seven times. Since’BB the dif-
ferent measures have been thrown out in the Leg.
Council five times ; in ’92 by the casting vote of
the chairman. Six years ago the principle be-
came law, but Tory landholding M’s.P. placed
such restrictions as to voting that the Act is in-
operative. Assisted by the Kingston Ministry,
S.A. Democrats are now making a determined
attempt to amend the Act.
The Labor-party in Victoria begins to
assert its independence by voting more frequently
against the Turner Ministry, and the defeat of the
proposed Maffra Beet Sugar co. subsidy was one
result of this policy. And this is just where the
need of an elective Executive comes in. Under
the Party system the Labor section, in its efforts
to show that the progressive Turner isn’t quite
progressive enough, .may some day quite acci-
dentally upset him, and let that brassy Tory,
Dunky Gillies, into his billet.
The Byrnes Govt, suffered its first defeat
in Q. Assembly in committee on the Workman’s
Lien Bill. The little fiddle-faddle was as to
whether “workman” included “workwoman.”
Attorney Byrnes said “ Aes ; ” others said “ No.”
Lawyer Drake moved an amendment to make the
clause plain to the dullest understanding. Govt,
went down by 26 votes against 20. “A straw
shows,” &c. As a matter of law, by the Inter-
pretation of Statutes Act, except where otherwise
specifically provided against, the masculine always
includes the feminine.

landish in indecency.

within five months thereafter, said order will
lapse. It also provides that he may be ordered

Crick, M.P., seems to possess the power
of sending that erstwhile most good-humored poli-
tician, G. H. Reid, into fits of raging madness.
He was also almost invariably successful in per-
forming the same feat with Bruce Smith, who was
noted for his imperturbable manner and general
coolness under all other circumstances. As the man
who loses his temper is invariably in the wrong in
popular estimation, Crick scores every time.
Item from the great night of riot in Maori-
land House of Reps.—An M.P. : “Mr. Fisher
said that supposing my father had been sentenced
to seven days for stealing a glass of beer
Fisher (interrupting): “I said he was a most

Russell, leader of M.L. Opposition, gob a
great truth off his chest lately when he pointed
out that Maoriland had already paid more to Bull
in interest on its public debt than it had received
from him in loans. All the older provinces are in
the same situation. But, though politicians say
things like that, not one of them comes forward
with a definite scheme for raising the public-works
money out of taxation and thus dispensing with
the loan curse hereafter.
That splendid British man-o'-war, the
Rapid, lately wandering in Australian waters,
crawled back to England in a trifle over four
months (a decent sailing vessel can do the same
voyage in three), and was condemned as unsafe for
further struggles with the mighty deep. It is just
14 years since the Rapid was launched, so it would
seem that she must have been pretty cronk to
begin with. A good many of Bull’s fighting ships
have dark suspicions attached to them.
Yosper, anyhow, is doing good educational
work in the West; and that is about the most
serviceable sort that can be done, so long as
Premier Forrest persists in his refusal to remedy
the swamping of goldfields miners by far Northern
cattle. Vosper’s latest proposal (of course, he
hasn’t the ghost of a chance to carry anything) is
that the West Australian Bank ought to have its
accounts audited by an officer of the Audit Dept.,
inasmuch as it is entrusted with large sums of
Govt, money. Vosper comes from Queensland,
so ought to know a thing or two of interest in
that connection.
Barton should be returned for the Hastings
and Macleay because able, independent-minded
men, not mere party-hacks, are needed in Parlia-
• ment to frame the Constitution of our nation. In
the making of that Constitution it is vitally neces-
sary to employ the most intellectual exponents of
every shade of opinion. Barton is a man who has
taken a leading place in every sphere wherein he
has worked. At the University, in Parliament
as a member and as Speaker, and at the Bar.
Those who would have the Australian Constitu-
tion made by a party instead of by all parties are
traitors to our country.
Henry Wrixon, M.L.C., did his share in
the rejection of the Woman’s Suffrage Bill by
Victoria’s House of 48 Rich Landlords on the
ground that for women to have a vote would cause
division in the household, and would be against
“the refining influences which at present operated
on women.” Of course, if the mere possession of
a vote is against refinement, it is reasonable to
infer that Wrixon, as the owner of several votes
and a politician to boot, must be a rather coarse
and brutalised individual by this time. And, by
the way, what are the “ refining influences which
at present operate on women ? ” It is a good old
cant phrase, but what does it mean?
When Higgins, M.L.A., suggested in Yic.
Assembly that the province should pay for its
public works out of revenue, instead of floating a
gaudy £2, 000,000 loan, Minister Foster promptly
rose to point out that part of the loan was for
waterworks in Higgins’s own constituency of Gee-
long. The presumable object of that irrelevant
argument was to bribe Higgins to silence, or else
to draw the attention of Higgins’s constituents to
the fact that their member was wickedly en-
dangering their little job for the sake of mere
principle. An Assembly which wasn’t utterly
corrupted by loans and road-and-bridge swindles
would have aimed the biggest available book at
Foster.
Several times in the course of the late
N.S.W..censure debate and many hundred times
before then, the party which wants to Override the
majority of -June 3 repeated the old accusation
that Barton declared the Federal Bill the best Bill
conceivable, and now showed himself a turncoat
by attempting to amend it. The shortest explana-
tion on which point is that the statement is a dis-
graceful lie and the circulators thereof are mostly
liars. What Barton said over and over again was
that the Federal Bill was the best that could be
got from that Convention ; also, that he believed it
was the very best possible Bill considering all the
divergent interests involved in the making of it.
Any man can be made to say anything if you saw
his sentence off in the right place.

Some years ago, two trains used to back-
up to each other at Haleombe (M.L.) —one going
north to Wanganui, other south to Foxton; and
one day a well-known Maori named “Wi”
coupled, unobserved, the rear carriages of the two
trains, which were backed up close. Presently
the W. guard whistled and Wi danced around
singing out, “I hackee the Wanganui ! No! I
backee the Foxton ! ” till the former was fairly
dragging the latter out of the station. Then,
when Wi saw the surprised passengers jumping
out of the Foxton train, he shouted “Wanganui
makee the win. Wanganui makee the win. I
backee the Wanganui.” Nobody was hurt and
the trains were stopped within 100 yards ; but Mr.
Wi’s little pleasantry cost him six months gaol.

On Japanese railways a watchman is em-
ployed at the entrance to each tunnel. When the
train enters, the man lets down a closelv-woven
curtain that closes that particular opening. The
result of this simple contrivance is that the smoke
all follows the train, and, when the locomotive
has left the tunnel, hardly a particle of smoke re-
mains in the excavation. Said to be a splendid
idea for keeping the air of tunnels clear and sweet.
As Barton’s fees in the M‘Sharry case are
paid half by plaintiff and half by defendant, the re-
current tear-shedding of N.S.W. Government,the
defendant, over its share of the 25 guineas a day,
while plaintiff stands uncomplainingly to his
agreement, is explicable only on grounds of
political partyism. Especially as the Government
only started complaining when it found Barton
coming back to politics. And the Government, by
indulging to the extent it has indulged this spirit
of partyism, is, if Barton’s decision be adverse to
M'Sharfy, supplying that plaintiff with a sub-
stantial ground for appealing to the Privy Council.
The Government is, practically, doing what it can
to bounce the arbitrator into an early verdiot
which to the losing side must naturally seem an
immature one.

World s Championship Results
CABLEGRAM.
To the Dunlop Tyre Co*
Melbourne.
VIENNA,
Saturday, 10/9/98.
The 100 Kilometres (62£ miles) World’s
Professional Championship won by R.
Palmer, of England, on
Dun iflp Tyres
THE above event is the second of the
World’s paced long-distance champion-
ships set down for decision at the World’s
Championship Meeting now being held
at Vienna. R. Palmer, the winner of the
above event, is England’s finest middle
distance pace follower, and has amongst
his best performances nearly all the
World’s records up to 100 miles, the cen-
tury being ridden in the marvellous time
of 3hrs. 25mins. 21 4-5 sec. Palmer would
be paced by the famous Dunlop pacing
teams, which last year assisted J. W.
Stocks (on Dunlops) in carrying off the
same event. The two remaining events to
be decided are the One Mile Amateur and
One Mile Professional World’s Champion-
ships. These sprint races have been
carried off during the last two or three
years by Continental sprinters ; but Eng-
land has a worthy representative this year
in F. Chinn, who has a big chance of cap-
turing the professional “blue riband.”
CABLEGRAM.
To the Dunlop Tyre Co.,
Melbourne.
LONDON,
Thursday, 8/9/98.
The 100 Kilometres (62g miles) World’s
Amateur Championship won by A. J.
Cherry, of England, on
Duniqp Tyres
THE above event is one of the four World’s
Championships set down for running
under the International Association rules,
the venue this year being at Vienna. 11
was A. J. Body’s ambition to ride in the
professional championships, to be run in
Vienna this week ; but, as intimated liv-
eable, the International Association re-
fused Body’s entry on the ground that the
Australian Leagues were not affiliated
with the International Association. This
is to be regretted, as on the form shown by
A. J. Body in the Cuca Cup, which he
won, he would have had a big chance in
the events to be decided at the Austrian
capital this week. The 100 kilometre race
is a paced one, and the winner, A Cherry,
is a well-known English amateur, having
won last year’s 50 miles Amateur English
Championship. The World’s Champion-
ships were run at Glasgow, Scotland, last
year, and this particular event was won bv
E. Gould (on Dunlops), another English
crack. Last year England retained two
out of the four World’s championships,
Denmark and Germany accounting for the
other two events.

486 KENT STREET, SYDNEY;
and at Melbourne, Adelaide, Perth, and Christchurch, N.Z.
Mr. “Jim ” Parker, late squatter and
formerly well-known on the far N.Q. mining-
fields, who has for some time past been endeavor-
ing to push a fruit-preserving invention of his in
Sydney, is missing from his usual haunts, and
friends are anxious concerning him. Any infor-
mation will be gladly received by Messrs. Warden
Harry Graves & Son, 5 Spring-st., Sydney.
Touting has already been started at
N.S.W. Parliament House by members anxious
to fill the vacant seats on the Public Works Com-
mittee. The most necessary qualification is to be
able to show that you are in an advanced stage of
“hard up,” and the member who has the necessary
meanness of spirit to go round and prove to ad
hands that he is next to bankruptcy is pretty sure
of a seat. The amount of lobbying that is done in
this connection is absolutely disgraceful.
Premier Reid’s latest action is further
proof of the intensity of the Wriggler’s Federal
zeal. He strains every effort, and also the truth,
to defeat the Federal leader, and put a political
nonentity like Smith, author of the Bounty Bogey,
back into Parliament. The electors of the Hast-
ings are not likely to be gulled by the protesta-
tions of one whose every Federal action has been
a contradiction or a lie. As The Bulletin has
shown, Reid now denounces every clause he voted
for, and advocates every clause he voted against
in Convention. Barton gave his whole ability to
framing a measure in which the balance of power,
which is justice, would be equitably held between
the States; lie, looking ahead, endeavored to con-
solidate and forward the interests of United Aus-
tralia, recognising that, union once accomplished,
the present artificial barriers will fade from the
minds of men.
“Poor Poussard ! ” says one. who knew
him, “ Sydney loses an artist in losing him. His
playing lost force in his later years ; that was in-
evitable ; but in his prime his violin held witchery.
And even three years ago I heard him play in a
trio of, Raff’s —that one with the magnificent
adagio.” Madame Kellertnann played also, and
played brilliantly; Herr Strauss was good ;
but Poussard—ah, he eclipsed them both, he was
grand. Yes, there was in that man a passionate
fibre that thrilled at the touch of fine music, and
at his best he had the skill to transmute
his emotion into sound with those delicate
fingers, and thrill you also. He did not
fail in technique; but it was not in techr
nique that his power lay—it lay in his grace,
his fire, his feeling, in that inexpressible some-
thing which is the artist’s heritage, which
divides him for ever from the mere skilled crafts-
man. True, he did not stand in the very first
rank ; we have had Wilhelmj, we have had
Remenyi, we have had others to surpass him ; but
by moments—yes, by moments —he made you feel
he had a spark of genius. And, as a man,
he had the good heart—he never spoke ill
of any. And bon enfant I—to1 —to hear him laugh !
—he laughed with his whole being, laughed
all over—it was a veritable explosion. And
t rench withal —oh, a real Frenchman ! He
had the national weaknesses—he would exag-
gerate—if you asked him how many pupils he
had, he would say, “Oh, three hundred.” But
he did not exaggerate his age—my faith !
About 56,” he would say. Now, we who know
him think he must have been 71. The good
fellow ! He never smoked a cigar—always
cigarettes ; but he never refused a cigar. Always
it went to his pocket. Yet, mind you, there was
nothing mean about Poussard, nothing stingy. It
was just a little trait, you understand ; he did not
like to let the cigar pass him, so I think. And he
was going to Maoriland next month, to Europe
next year ; he was full of plans. It was very
sudden, his death. Why, his little son (himself a
promising violinist) was to have had a birthday-
party on the very day when that. worn brain
snapped. Poor Poussard ! Ah, there are regrets! ”
Sydney D.T. advt. :

racks, Paddington.
By the way, Gen. French completes his three
years’ appointment as N.S.W. commandant in
April next.
“ Tang Anyut ” : The reward of virtue.
Old friend of mine—awful swearer and swiper—
once endeavored, in playful mood, to pitch a
Dutchman over a hotel verandah. Dutchy was
strong, but verandah-post wasn’t —result, broken
head, arm, collar-bone and ribs. While in hospi-
tal recovering, received news of death of maiden
aunt. Forsook evil ways, went home to look after
things, fell down stairs in his new house and was
killed !
S.A.’s well-kept mortuary statistics are
alternately cheerful and depressing. For in-
stance, 63 people were drowned in ’B9; but
(? owing to increase in the number of swimmers)
the figures have- steadily fallen to 35 last year.
Cancer’s victims have, on the other hand, gone
from 116 in ’BB to 185 in ’97 (? diseased meat); and,
for some not very clear reasons, heart-disease
victims are steadily becoming more numerous.
The Rev. Gribble, a well-known Northern
clergyman who devotes himself to bettering the
condition of the blacks, is fond of performing the
marriage-ceremony among the aboriginals. Accord-
ing to the Cairns Post, he was catechising the
niggers the other day before Dr. Roth, and asked
a dusky pupil, apropos of the “means of salva-
tion,” “What are these good works that are
pleasing in the sight of the Lord?” “Please,
sir,” said a. festive buck, “getting married.”
Du Paty de Clam’s dismissal from the
French army, following hard upon Col. Henry’s
degradation and suicide, marks the fatal rift in
the monstrous Dreyfus conspiracy of concealment;
and, as de Clam bossed the court martial that con-
demned Dreyfus, and afterwards played “ veiled
lady ” in the Esterhazy hush-up, it is evident that
the final burst-up is at hand—thanks, funda-
mentally, to the noble perseverance of the con-
demned man’s wife. This is the sort of thing that
should have a statue.
“ General ” Booth, of the “ Army,” made
his 33rd annual report on July 18. Claimed for
the organisation that it now operates in 47 coun-
tries, has 12,033 staff, and 48,162 local officers,
besides 2986 cadets and other employes ; that its
weekly publications have a circulation of 880,000,
and its monthly magazines 131,074; and that
26,000 “submerged ” are daily “cared for.” The
“ Army’s ” “ land colonies ” now number 15 ; bub
“prison-gate homes,” of which there are alto-
gether only the same number, seem to get more
attention in Australia than elsewhere.
Little was said by the papers about the
disgraceful scene in the lobby of N.S.W. Assembly
prior to the recent want-of-confidence division.
It was the effort of the jackals of both sides to
make amenable members drunk, in the hope that
they would be unable to take part in the division
—though, by the way, the member who is so
drunk as to be unable to vote must indeed bo
memorably “tanked.” The hero of the evening
was Crick, whose knowledge of Parliamentary
forms and readiness of wit are proverbial. Just
as the division-bell had ceased ringing he appeared
at the bar of the House with a laggard friend,
whom he had been skirmishing round to find, and
the bar went down before the laggard member
could get through. Whereupon Crick drew Mr.
Speaker’s attention to the fact that, as the resolu-
tions had not been read, a vote could not yet be
taken (it was a pure guess on Crick’s part that the
reso’utions had not been read, but his assumption
that the formality had been neglected proved
correct). Mr. Speaker ordered the resolutions to
be read, whereupon the bar was raised and Crick’s
stray sheep got through and joined in the division.

This mode of treatment is absolutely free from
Mercury, and other deleterious, poisonous, or en-

This New Method of

on the contrary, promotes digestion, clears the

Many sufferers fear lest others should know they

SPORTING NOTIONS.
If, as we hear so often, the chief charm of
horse-racing- lies in its uncertainty, patrons of the sport
must have been well pleased on the last day of Randwick
meeting. Blair, at 12 to 1, defeated the short-priced
Stand-Off for the High Weight Handicap, and Johansen
scored his second win of the meeting in the Members'
Handicap, the favorite Tui finishing nearer last than first.
Levanter started a prime favorite for the Second Steeple-
chase, and, carrying his 13st. like a feather-weight, won
as he liked from Pomeroy. Parnell fell and Underwood
got a nasty gash in the head, but he remounted and
finished in third place. Another 12 to 1 mare, Sweet
Marie, won Waverley Handicap, and the sterling little
ex-galloway Fitzroy appropriated the Final Handicap.
Ambente, Merloolas, and Nevada were the only starters
in Randwick Plate ; Merloolas, on the strength of his
two previous wins from Amberite, starting an even-money
favorite. Merloolas cut out the running, but when fairly
on the road down the straight Amberite passed the
favorite and won, after a great set-to, by a head.
* * • *
A.J.C. Derby resolved itself into a sprint, for
during the early part of the race the horses barely gal-
loped fast enough to keep themselves warm. Some of
the w.f.a. races were also run at a middling pace, and it
is high time that a time-limit should be imposed to pre-
vent farces.
* • • •
Emily Soldene, in her description of A.J.C.
Derby, hit off to the life the average lady-punter. “ What
is it ? ” said I to a woman in shabby black, with a shabby
black tippet trimmed with shabby black fur. “ Oh, don’t
”. s nei * hb °r> grabbing rny arm. “ What is
it? said I again. “ Oh, m3’ God ! ” screamed the woman
111 s *} a hby black, “it’s Picture.” She waved her arms
and her umbrella frantically—“ Come on, you beauty I
Come on—Picture ! —Picture ! My darling I—my beauty 1
I knew it!
* * • •
The weights for the handicaps run at Randwick
tost Saturday were not, as usual, published the day
before ; consequently owners and trainers were unable to
decide early as to running their horses, and endless con-
fusion, much heartburning, and fearful language ensued.
ror the sake of stalling off a few of the supposed in-
fringers of the club’s race-book copyright, the A.J.C. not
only broke faith with the public, butalso broke its own rule
which says that the weigtits shall be published the day
before the race. If the club issued, in addition to the
race-book, a cheap card setting forth names, numbers,
weights, and colors, everyone would buy it, and there
would be no need for such high-handed action as that of
last week.
* • • •
Amberite’s performances during the recent
meeting were of a very in-and-out description. After
being badly beaten by Merloolas on the first day, he ran
a great horse in the Metrop. (won by a stable companion)
carrying top weight and finishing a close fourth. He
then “ went off,” and finished behind The Grafter, Mer-
loola* and Cocos, but two days later again showed
wonderful improvement, and beat said Merloolas. Am-
berite would now appear to be a most unreliable animal.
♦ * * m
A winner at the recent Randwick meeting who
will he heard of again is Blair, a big, clumsy-looking geld-
ing of the cab-horse type, with little or no breeding, but
a good-tempered, honest trier, and a beautiful galloper.
His wins were at six and ten furlongs, and were both
easy.
* * • *
Some owners took advantage of the strong
muster of bookies in Sydney last week to thus early
support their Derby and Cup candidates. For Caulfield
Cup Reka, Massinissa and Hymettus received very solid
backing, whilst Reka and Merloolas were also heavily
supported forMelb. Cup. Gauleon and Woodlark coupled
were backed to the tune of 7000—700, a wager of which, at
present, the layer seems to have a bit the best.
«...
Last week’s trot at Brighton, Sydney, was the
most exciting event on the programme. Siren was
f' v ' n n Kola a couple of secs, start, and from the word
Go the pair commenced to battle it out. Entering
the straight for the last time, Siren, Kola, and little Satan
were all together, but Kola breaking at the critical
moment., Siren just won from Satan.
*-• * •
The demand for the Tote in Yic. and N.S.W.
has had the temporary effect of enlarging s.p. prices (on
paper) against horses that don’t win, for the horse-
reporters to a man are anxious to make out the best pos-
sib'P case for Ikey Mo. But they can't make a good job
of it, anyhow. The Sydney Handicap winner, Sweet
Mane, was returned by the s.p. reporters as a 20 to 1
chance in a field of 12 onty. That looks attract ive. Ikey
Mos barrackers will point to the Sweet Marie case as a
proof that the gent with the prominent feature to his
face lays odds equal to the tote’s. Now, presuming that
he did bet as reported—viz., from sto 2 against Fucile
down to “15 to 1 against Charge, and 20 to 1 against
others -and presuming that he laid the odds to lose
ClOO against everything in the race, he would have £149
10s. in his bag wherewith to repay £lO5 t o the backer
who took “20 fivers” about Sweet Marie. £44 10s. would
stick to I key’s fingers-say, £3O more than the tote
would deduct for commission. Meanwhile punters can
amuse themselves by studying the s.p. returns, and see-
ing whether they are true statements of the case as
regards the horses that do win.

Taking the Argus figures as a true statement of Ikey
Mo’s business on Tecoma’s race (the chances are the
Argus reporter didn’t, understate the odds) we find Ikey
could have betted to win from £7 10s. to £l4 10s. on a
turnover of £47 10s.—say a profit varying from 15 to 30
per cent. Well, the tote would charge not more than 10
percent, on any winner. The newspaper that barracks
for Ikey Mo on commercial grounds gets itself into much
the same argumentative mess as the parson who objects
to the tote on behalf of public morality.
Dear Bulletin.— As an advt. for the A.J.C.
Spring' carnival I notice about Sydney a poster, which
depicts a ruck of conventionally-drawn horses, stretched
In impossible galloping postures, while the jockeys who
bestride them are libels on Australian horsemanship.
Aow, why should not the A.J.C.—like the shipping co.'s—
commission competent men to design something artistio
and local? Why not give, say Mahony, the commission
to design a poster that, besides being true to life, would
be an ornament to the city and a credit to the club. An-
other business-poster depicts a two-horse “finish,” and
observes underneath that “ So-and-so wins, hands down/ 1
Yet both jocks are represented as hard at work with their
prads. Fancy a man, with both heels and hands at work,
winning “ hands down ” 1 How many understand the
phrase ?—Jack Shay.

The annual report of the V.A.T.C., Caulfield,
shows that, with a gross revenue of £31,000, the credit
balance is £8935.
* * * •
A pork-suppered publican at Coolah scored the
first Melb. Cup dream of the season, last week. As the
horses were racing down the straight he saw a brown
horse come bounding like a greyhound through a
lot of sheep dogs, but just then the crowd “took to
him for a guy-a-whack bookmaker, and in the scrimmage
he lost sight of the horses. When he awoke, his wife was
punching and shaking him, having become alarmed by
his stertorous breathing. He says he wouldn’t have
minded the punching if she had oniy given him a chance
to recognise that brown horse.
* • • •
The bloom ia off the cycling industry. N.S.
WalM “amateur” cyclists cycled mostly to empty
benches at Sydney Cricket Ground on Saturday. How-
ever, some good racing was seen. Goodson (Q.) won the
Ten Mile Championship from Longmuir, and New and
Park (Vie.) scooped the Lap Scratch race. “Monny”
Kerr (brother to ex-champion Wally) just scraped home
in the Five Mile Handicap. In this race half-a-dozen fell
and young “Jim’ ’Tooher was so seriously hurt—skull-
fracture—that he died soon after.
• * • •
The death of poor Tooher produced very little
shock in the Sydney community. Such a fatal accident
had been expected ever since the first field of inexperi-
enced riders wobbled round the hard, high banking. No
man who has seen a big field of horses gallop at a hurdle
having room only for six abreast has been surprised at the
accident which follows. And the death of Tooher pro-
duced no more effect than is produced by the death of a
patient who for a long time has notoriously been suffering
from an incurable and severe malady. The danger of a
fatal fall with a crowded cycling field is so imminent that
a lion-hearted rider like Martin plavs upon the fear that
is m every rider in order to win the race ; makes it
one of his chief instruments of success. Fear of falling
prevents the majority of cyclists from winning a race.
Martin often used openly to take his life in his hands on
the track ; and perhaps always acted on the assumption
that he was the only man who would dare take his life in
his hands. The fear of falling is so severe that if a cyclist
does get a really bad smash in a bunch, he never regains
the nerve to thread through another bunch at 25 miles
an hour. Given crowded fields, there will be many more
fatal accidents on the cricket-ground track.
* * * *
Alf. Middleton, the Dunolly man with calves
on his heart, won the Vic. 100-miles-road championship
on Saturday, but by only a couple of lengths from “ Little
Bil martin ’’ Forbes ; Broadbent, Iredale, and Calder fol-
lowing within syds.—a really phenomenal finish to a race
of that distance. Considering that 50 miles had to be
ridden in the teeth of a stiff northerly gale, the closeness
of the final sprint seetns all the more extraordinary.
Carpenter, last year’s winner, could only get into sixth
place, and Corbett, who won the recent 50-mile spin,
dropped out through accident.
• * * *
M‘Donald, the Pt. Darwin telegraph-operator
who has made a cycling dash down to Adelaide, has
easily lowered all “records ’’-Murif’s and Coleman and
Mather’s. Everything, though, was in his favor-
weather, tracks (hardened by rains), the assistance of
transcontinental linemen, accurate description of the
tracks, the knowledge that the trip had been accom-
plished, and the fact that expensive preparations are
being made en route for the line-duplication. Besides
there was a girl waiting at the other side. Murif’s ride
still stands as the really plucky thing; for, spite of all
that has been said,, that eccentric set out absolutely “ on
his own,” and with an amazing ignorance about the
route.

The other afternoon a bike-yahoo rode down
Oxford-street (Syd.) with both hands stuffed into bis
pockets and a big butcher’s-block pipe sticking out of
his face. After about two-score of miraculous escapes
he succeeded in riding over an ancient female and empty-
ing the contents of his pipe into the eye of a man who
tried to save her. In trifles like this N.S.W. might with
advantage copy Italy, where the law jumps with both
feet on the cyclomaniac who lets go the handles, takes his
feet off the pedals, or gyrates about the streets without
bell, brake or candle;

ney enquiry re prices to members of the Cycling Associa-

* • * *
The question of the liability of an innkeeper for
the safety of his guest’s bicycle entrusted to his custody
formed the subject of an interesting case in an English
County Court lately. Plaintiff rode on Easter Monday
from St. Albans to Luton, where he entered the yard of
an hotel. One of the innkeeper's men, in answer to a
question as to where the machine was to be put, pointed
to a shed in the yard. The machine was put there, and
plaintiff went inside for lunch. On returning, he found
the bike had disappeared. The innkeeper, when sued,
E leaded in effect that plaintiff ought to have padlocked
is machine, that it was bank holiday and there were 50
other bicycles there, and that he couldn’t be expected to
see that each man took his own, that the common law on
the subject never contemplated such unprofitable things
as cycles as being under its protection, and that plaintiff
must prove that the innkeeper was guilty of negligence.
But the Court bl ushed all these pleas away, and, apply-
ing the old doctrine of the common law'that an inn-
keeper insures the safety of his guest’s goods properly
left in his charge against all loss, unless occasioned by the
act of God or of the Queen’s enemies or the guest’s own
negligence, made an order in favor of the cyclist. It was
especially held in this case that it was not negligence to
omit to padlock the cycle.
• * * *
Fitzroy (Vic.) footballers astonished all the
knowing ones on Saturday by defeating Collingwood by
22 toll, after a game which, though played in a gale, is
regarded as the best of the season. The Vic. premiership
contest seems interminable. If, was expected to end three
weeks ago, but Fitzro.ys upsi t all expectations by beating
Essendon, and placing themselves level with their oppon-
ents and Collingwood. Last Saturday they had to decide
whether the “Woodsies” or themselves' should meet
Essendon for the final, and all tips were knocked on the
head by the result. Next Saturday, when Fitzroy and
Essendon meet, the chances are they will make a draw of
it, for finality has been so often deferred this season that
it seems unattainable.

Of all Vic. clubs St. Kilda possesses a record
unique in the annals of local football, the doughty
“ Saints having won not a single match for two years !
In ’97 season they managed to score a draw or two, but
this time they have lost every match. Yet there is not a
club on the list that has not at some time had to go under
to the seasiders.

Opponents of electorate cricket in Adelaide,
who were very strong prior to its initiation last year, are
being oonfonnded by the progress of two clubs, Sturt and
Port Adelaide, which have made big successes of local
grounds. The poor displays of S.A. cricketers as regards
fielding and catching have often been attributed to the
fact that matches have been played in restricted spaces
on the Adelaide Oval, a drawback now likely to be re-
moved.

“ The essential difference between English and
Australian bowling,” writes The Bulletin’s London cor-
respondent, “ was plainly exposed iri the Middlesex-
Surrey match, when the latter lost their hope of the
championship. The former bowl for their averages, the
latter for their team, and, in fact, English bowlers are
merely stonewallers. To win a three-days’ match the
batsmen have to make runs, and that pretty rapidly ;
therefore, J. T. Hearne and his like keep a splendid pitch
and systematic break that do not allow of many liber-
ties. But when it comes to shifting ‘stickers,’ who do
not want to score—well, give me a man, not a machine.
In the last innings Surrey had 287 runs to get, and lots of
time to do it in. The cracks (bar Abel) collapsed, and
then the draw was played for. Here Hearne was useless,
but Albert Trott fed, coaxed, and puzzled them so cleverly
that a few minutes before time the game was won. Had
it not been for A.E., Middlesex would have drawn, for
two Hearnes would not have shifted the batsmen. Yet
the Australian got 6 for 87, and the Englishman 4 for 38,
though each bowled 40 overs. The latter had 24 maidens,
the former ten less, yet can it be questioned which was of
the more service to his .side?”

“F.” : At N.S.W. Publio Schools Sports last
week saw several “ three-card ” men with the usual large
umbrella and collection of very-evident. ‘‘ buttoners ”
doing great biz by enticing the youngsters to bet pennies.
The adult idiot who fancies he can pick the card mostly
earns all he gets ; but when big, able-bodied men com-
bine to take a child down for a penny, they deserve
squashing with the large, heavy, flat foot of the law.
* * * *
Golden Gate Athletio Club, Sydney, gave some
600 enthusiasts a good half-dozen rounds on Saturday
night, when Hope and Essler supplied the battle of the
evening. The set-to was even until towards the end of
the sixth round, when Essler got all the best of it, but the
referee stepped in and declared it a draw. These Satur-
day-night shows, as a rule, are well-contested, and devoid
of brutality, as the referee is a man who knows when to
knock off. There was more blood spilt when Underwood
got his fall off Parnell, on Saturday, at Randwick, than
has been shed at the Golden Gate since the inception of
these contests.

“W.”: An unpublished incident anent a
recently-deceased high N.S.W. ex-civil servant. In his
young days he was no end of a bruiser, and I was one of
a party whioh accompanied him to Garden Island to
settle a little difference. He had a mop of curly bright
hair, but when his opponent appeared with hair cropped
as if just out of gaol, it was decided that our man must
have a crop also. A knife that would cut could not be
raised, so we knocked out of the bottom of a bottle the
piece which boys call a swallow-cutter, and with that we
hacked his hair off. He won like a tradesman.

A determined attack has been made in the
Westralian goldfields towns on Sunday sports. One fight
took place in the Kalgoorlie Council, on a motion to con-
tinue the granting of the use of the public grounds for
that day. T’othersiders quoted, in opposition, Broken
Hill as a mining town, working under similar conditions,
which had its sports meeting's and football-matches on
Saturdays. But the argument did not avail; and the
Sawbath is still to be enjoyed. But, as for Broken Hill,
though it shuts the town grounds on Sundays, it opens
those reached by train—and things happen then that
wouldn’t happen at home.

his return.”
Queensland vital statistics for last year
show that the population has increased faster
than that of any other continental division—Wes-
tralia excepted. The Q. rate of increase was
2‘65 per cent, as against Victoria OTI p.c., Maori-
land 2’09 p.c., South Australia o‘7B p.c. Total
increase for last statistical year was 12,251 persons,
of which number 9004 arrived in the first half of
present year. Males (naturally for so young a
country) predominate—l 27 to every 100 females.
In 10 years Q. has attracted 27,000 persons from
the other Australias. The North possesses
95,837 inhabitants, the Centre 57,610, the South
331,253 ; sum total, 484,700. (Separationists will
note with interest that the South still boasts no
less than 68 per cent, of the total population.)
Marriage-rate, six per thousand. Average Q.
family greater than those of Germany, Russia,
France, England, Scotland, Belgium, Sweden, or
Denmark. (Ireland, however, is slightly ahead.)
Birth-rate, the highest on the continent, 29.92 per
thousand. Death-rate seems to get lower every
year, being now down to 11 ‘33. (Alien death-rate,
however, is still as portentous as ever). The
ladies of Bananaland are cosmopolitan in their
tastes. During the last year 738 of them married
natives, 346 married Englishmen, 80 wedded
Scotchmen, 105 settled down with Irishmen,
while jfire married Chinamen, and three Polyne-
sians. The women, however, who live unmarried
with various smellful aliens may be reckoned by
the score.
“ Von Kotze ” on J. A. Philp and the
Chinese question :
It is bad to suffer from pet-theory on the brain. S. A.
Philp asserts that he never advocated Chinese immigra-
tion at all. Well, if he didn’t do so directly, the inference
was unescapeable. He says, “ that the Chinese market
gardeners are not equal to the supply of vegetables is
evidenced by the increased price of vegetables and the
always rising wages of the market gardener.” Which is
acbully a capital argument for my contention. Exclude
Chinese, and the wages rise ! More than even I ever
dared to maintain. Again, “it is the Chinaman who
garners the wealth of the deserted gold and tin fields,
and faces, without a grumble, the Chinaman’s work of
scrub-cutting, etc.” Yes ; and keeps the white fossieker
off the ground. The Palmer, for instance, would be
carrying a white population to-day, and the commercial
destiny of the Far North would have been very different,
but for those slant-eyed gleaners. J.A.P. further denies
that he favors racial intermarriages, which in the face of
his plea for immigration-restrictions modification" is very
much on a par with Reid’s Federal professions. More
Chow market-gardener means more yellow hybrid.
Lastly, the liking or dislike for Chinese is not a matter of
taste, but of healthy racial instinct ; and a cosmopolitan
philanthropy that takes to its bosom the better class of
Asiatic in preference to the slum-bred white produce of
cheap labor and monopolism, is a very ladylike, and there-
fore very illogical, sentiment indeed. On the whole, Mr.
Philp makes the case against the Chinaman so much
blacker, or yellower, than I could, that the Chow might
well, in this case, ask to be “ saved from his friends.”


The French military authorities have been
at last forced by the remorseless logio of
events from the possibility of maintaining that
Dreyfus wrote the famous “bordereau” upon
which principally he was condemned. Looks,
however, as if they were still holding out against
revision of the sentence in the expectation that
Dreyfus, by becoming the “Man who’s Dead,”
will himself put an end to the sordid “ situation.”
That well-known Bulletin writer, Ed-
ward Dyson, is publishing through Geo. Robert-
son and Co. a new book of Australian tales,
entitled “Below and On-Top,” dealing mainly
with mining, township and bush life. The volume,
published at 3s. 6d. cloth, 2s. 6d. paper, will be
illustrated by Phil May, Alf. Vincent and Amb.
Dyson.

Jawbone Neild, when interviewed by
Sydney Daily Telegraph for an explanation of
his absence from the censure vote division, was
curt and snappish to a degree. The Majah
much resents his non-inclusion in the Ministry.
He significantly remarked that this was the first
occasion he had been summoned to a division by
the Reid Government.
Under Maoriland Land Settlements Act
of 1894, to March of recent financial year, 28
estates of 86,919 acres had been purchased for
£377,553 65., to which must be added i 11,761,
expense of roads, surveys, administration, &c. ;
19 had been subdivided, and greater part selected ;
133 houses erected, and <£13,022 worth of improve-
ments effected. The rental (4'76 per cent, on cost)
leaves a surplus after providing interest on
purchase money. Of 397 selectors 12 only have
forfeited their rights for breach of covenant.
Balance of land when selected will further aug-
ment the profits and demonstrate how to do with-
out the landlord.

The English Amalgamated Engineers’
Society, which was about the most conservative
of ’totherside unions, has been a good deal shaken
up by its recent strike-defeat; now its trade
organ prints many pages in advocacy of political
action. Maori land, with its anti-strike legisla-
tion, is naturally the model just at present.
£ At Albury the other day a man run in
on the vag.” told the bench that he had travelled
to that town under the belief that it was abso-
lutely certain to become the Federal city. “He
was remamded to the gaol hospital for medical
examination.” Surely Moss Vale had a finger in
this !
The South Australian Full Court lately
took a day off, along with the Crown Solicitor
and the most learned member of the local Bar, to
consider the molar teeth of a black girl with a
baby. It all began with the fascination of Chong
Yow, an unpretentious yellow person with a
Northern Territory mia-mia. A stray trooper
suspected Yow of smoking uncustomed opium,
and “raided ” him. Now, all this trouble having
been taken over a mere Chow, he had to be
guilty of something ; and the black Kit who
shared the Celestial mansion (and had apparently
shared either that or some other mansion for
several months) was a convenient peg for a prose-
cution. To the astonishment of Yow (who denied
responsibility for the lady’s interesting condition),
he was charged with carnally knowing, and a
few other things, the allegation being that
Kathleen (The Bulletin hereby performs
the ceremony of baptism, which appears to
have been overlooked) was something be-
tween 13 and 16—most likely 13J. Kathleen’s
mamma had, however, omitted to register her
daughter’s birth, and the family Bible had bpen
mislaid ;as a matter of fact, Kathleen’s mamma
had been mislaid, too. A doctor was called in,
and, having looked into Kathleen’s mouth, pro-
nounced her 13 or about—on the evidence of
the molar teeth. The fine legal point, however,
was that Kathleen had a mamma, and that that
lady would have given the best evidence of Kath-
leen’s age; yet no attempt had apparently been
made at Palmerston, where Yow had been duly
found guilty, to produce mamma. Wherefore the
“best evidence ” wasn’t on hand, and the evidence
that was on hand wasn’t any good, unless the
doctor had great experience in connection with
female blacks’molar teeth, which he hadn’t. And
so Yow, who has by now probably served a couple
of years or so (happily it isn’t likely that he has
the least idea what it’s all about; except, probably,
that it has some connection with the disowned
infant) is declared to have been wrongly convicted,
and the conviction is duly set aside.

Another use has been found for the par-
son. Archdeacon M‘Cullagh, the generally-
trusted Anglican dignitary at Bendigo, has, for a
week or two, been sitting in his vestry hearing
evidence and arguments in M'Coll v. Hoskins—an
action in which the Yic. ex-Minister of Mines
wants a thousand or two from a local alderman
for throwing doubts upon the purity of some of
the plaintiff’s Ministerial actions. The matter
was taken out of the regular courts in its earliest
stages.
The British Government’s pension-list for
’97-’9B shows a decided tendency to do a little
something for brainworkers and their poor rela-
tions. A daughter of Mrs. Olipbant gets £75 a
year, the widow of Prof. Palmer gets .£lOO a year
in recognition of her late husband’s classical
scholarship and lack of capital, and a dozen or
more other people are provided with bread and
cheese for the term of their natural lives on
account of their own or a parent’s distinguished
labor in an unremunerative field.
