In a recent English divorce-case the appel-
lant stated that the respondent was pleasantly
given to going to bed with his boots on, and
scraping them on her legs. The respondent did
not deny this, but said it was only his little joke.
Goes to show what an elusive element Humor is,
no two persons having exaotly the same sense
of it.
“S.” : (Bulletin, 27/8/’9B) : Zenith of a
Hyperbolic Curve. Only a point can have a zenith ;
and that in the perpendicular to the tangent at
that point. But a hyperbola has an asymptote,
being a line which the curve approaches but never
reaches, except in infinity. I have taken the
name of Asymptote unto myself : in the infinite I
shall reach perfection, that is, hyperbolic per-
fection.
Amateur-scientist O’Connor, who took four
specimensof theceratodus to England, recently sold
two to London Zoological Society for .£9O, and
was offered .£lOO if he could land the remaining
two in the gardens of Paris. O’Connor writes
that, by a strange coincidence, a man arrived
about the same time from South America with a
living specimen of the lepidosiren, another of the
group of three existing fishes to which the eera-
todus belongs. .. . Worth noticing, as a specimen
of lamentable ignorance, that a certain much-
advertised dictionary of Australian words and
phrases actually mixes up the ceratodus and the
iarrimundi —two fish about as much alike as chalk
and cheese.
“ Scotty W.” : Re animal-taming, a Parsee
gentleman once told me, “You may tame a
Hindoo, not a Mussulman ; the former has grati-
tude, the latter none. The Hindoo is a lion, the
Mussulman a tiger. You cannot tame a tiger or
a Mussulman ; they will bite the hand that feeds
them ; whilst the Hindoo will lick the hand that
thrashes him.” [“ Scotty’s ” Parsee friend made
wild confusion between “ taming’s ” two senses of
“ making friendly ” and simply “ cowing.” You
can’t so easily cow the Mussulman as the Hindoo,
but you can make a much more trustworthy friend
of him. The average Mussulman is as much of a
man as you are, and he knows it; the average
Hindoo is not as much of a man—>and he knows it.
The Hindoo a lion !—oh !]
An article by G. W. Smalley in Har-
per’s New Monthly treats of Gladstone’s dis-
like for the “ Hero of Khartoum ” as an inexplic-
able thing. When Gordon was about to depart
on his last mission to Egypt the G.O.M. refused
him a personal interview, and essayist Smalley
can only suggest a mean political reason for the
refusal. The real reason, probably, was Glad-
stone’s moral loathing for the gory mystic who
took sacramental bread and wine at twelve London
churches a few hours before he left for Khartoum.
It has been said of Gordon that nobody who had
an unquestionable knowledge of his Christian life
among un-Christian peoples was ever known to
gush about his virtues. The actual man Gordon
was known to very few “brother officers,” and
those discreet old tacticians held their tongues
and smiled from the corners of their eyes when
the hero’s praises were being sung in sassiety.
Gladstone, presumably, had “ the whisper ” con-
cerning pietist Gordon.
The Esperance (W.A.) people maice out a
strong indictment against Premier Forrest in a
pamphlet circulated in the interests of the Espe-
rance-Norseman line. In ’96 Forrest said the
Govt, was willing to build the line whenever it
was shown that the field justified it, that the
Govt, did not favor extending the Coolgardie line
in that direction, and, when it did propose to
build the line, it would start from Esperance.
Later he said, though there were some” persons
who thought a line from Esperance would be
antagonistic to the interests of Fremantle, no such
narrow views were entertained by him. At Norse-
man recently he admitted that Esperance was the
natural port of the field. But in the face of all
these assurances, he now proposes to give that
field a line from Coolgardie, which means 480
miles transit to Fremantle instead of 120 miles to
Esperance, and says: “Esperance will have to
remain isolated. I have no doubt this will not
please the people of Esperance, but I cannot help
it.” It is not the trade of the Norseman alone
that the small-minded and big-bodied W.A. Govt,
is afraid of, so much as the fear of the trade that
would go via Esperance to the Eastern provinces
from the bigger fields higher up, instead of taking
the long and costly route to Perth, in order to
keep the boom going there.
Dr. Aveling has died within four months
after the suicide of his wife, the daughter of Karl
Marx. He seems to have fallen out of the odor
of Socialistic sanctity during the last few months.
Not a single Socialist or trades unionist represen-
tative was present at his cremation, which was
rather hard on the author of the “Student’s
Marx ” and a good worker for the movement, and
the following, from Reynold’s Newspaper, hardly
explains it: —
If Dr. Aveling had died a few years ago his obsequies
would have been attended by a notable gathering of
Socialists and trade union leaders. Yesterday, not a
single representative man, as far gs we are aware, put in
an appearance at the Woking cremation. The reason is
not far to seek. Gifted with considerable ability-
ability that had secured him a doctorship at London Uni-
versity, a fellowship at University College, a professorship
of comparative anatomy at the London Hospital, &c.,
qualifications of the very highest degree, Dr. Aveling had,
nevertheless, alienated almost everyone of his acquaint-
ances by a long course of low intrigues. That the suicide of
Karl Marx’s brilliant daughter was due to his heartless treat-
ment is abundantly proved by the mournful letters that
she has left behind her. Strange to say, in spite of an
exterior that almost recalled Quasimodo, he always exer-
cised a remarkable fascination for women, and the effect
of his lecturing tours in the provinces was frequently dis-
counted by tales of victims that he left behind him.'That
these offences were only known to a few persons, who
were unable to speak in fear of injuring the innocent, is
the explanation of the position he occupied in the politi-
cal world. In order to shield the unfortunate lady who
had united her fortunes with his own, silence was neces-
sary. But Dr. Aveling's death has almost coincided
with an exposure that would have driven him from the
movement for ever. To find Dr. Aveling’s counterpart in
history, we have to turn to the career Of Wainwright, the
essayist, who appears to have been in the same way a
strange compound of sin and genius.
Concerning this remarkable man, a scribe who
knew him writes to The Bulletin :
Aveling had a brilliant career at Cambridge, was a
D. Sc. of London University, a colleague of Bradlaugh as
Atheist lecturer, wrote several excellent scientific text-
books, was the author of several plays (written under the
name of Alec. Nelson), was a powerful orator, a fine
critic of literature, and—a heavy drinker. He was a
little man, with geat wide brilliant brown eyes that stood
out like bosses from his forehead ; had a powerful jaw,
a lofty forehead, the true, musical voice of an orator,
and a simply prodigious memory. But his character had
a kink in it. Old William Morris described him vividly
in an epigram unfortunately unprintable. His “ Students’
Darwin ” and his essays on the plays of Shakespeare were
both masterly works. Bradlaugh and Mrs. Besant broke
off from him on account of some curious doings in connec-
tion with his science classes; His best play was “ The
Jackal,” which had a fair run, and was doubtless a tale
of personal experience, the plot centering on a young
fellow whose brains were exploited by a wealthy cad,
who enjoyed the reputation of the young man’s work
done in his name.

petition will appear in next issue of Sunday
Times and The Bulletin.

Machine services for churches.—One of the features in
a recent electrical exhibition was a church lighted en-
tirely by tubes of soft, white, phosphorescence in the
roof. To make the show even more true to the condi-
tions it simulated, there were pews with hymn-books and
a real organ, upon which a prelude and an offertory were
played at each session. This being greatly appreciated,
it was suggested that the ecclesiastical idea should
be further carried out by introducing a real ser-
vice. As the show was practically continuous, there
were objections to engaging a clergyman, but a phono-
graph was mounted on a chair at the altar, with its brass
funnel stretching over the lectern, and from this instru-
ment the Lord’s Prayer, the 23rd Psalm and the Beati-
tudes were given out with sonorous effect. It may be
that in the near future a whole church service, sermon,
music and all, will be run by machinery.
Look at the humor of it. The show has to be<
made “true to the conditions simulated,” a
simulacrum of a sham; the “ecclesiastical idea”
is good, very good, “in a real service.” And,
hold me, “ the phonograph mounted on a chair”
with a “ brass ’’ funnel ! Could anything hit the
nail neater than that “ instrument ” with its
“sonorous” or is it “ snorous ” effect? And
there will be no difference, except in speed,
whether the Archbishop of Canterbury or the
curate of Pairandmaitem turns the handle. This,
however, opens up a serious question : How about
machine-made marriages ? Our law courts could
fine drunks by a-shilling-in-the-slot fashion.—
Scotty the Wrinkler.
Gerald Quealey, Bendigo’s dashing amateur
pug-reporcer, was once “ doing ”an inquest for
Bendigo Independent and, not sufficient men
being on hand to form a jury, Quealey was
pressed into service by the orderly. The case
was one of death from natural causes and
the evidence was simple and straight ; but at its
conclusion, the reporter, who had been elected
foreman, insisted on a retirement. After a lapse
of half-an-hour, he reappeared, and, having
announced the verdict, turned to the coroner:
“ Sir, I cannot allow this occasion to pass without
expressing my deep appreciation of the high in-
telligence and acumen displayed by my colleagues
on the jury.” The coroner stared hard. “ I hope
I am not doing anything irregular,” asked
Quealey. “No ! no ! but it’s not usual.” “ Perhaps
not,” continued the Independent man, unabashed,
“but I feel it my duty to express my recognition
of the assistance which my colleagues have given
towards the finding of this verdict. I may say
that the high order of intelligence displayed by
the other gentlemen of the jury was responsible
for the expedition with which we arrived at a
conclusion.” As coroner, orderly, and witnesses
stared open-eyed at Quealey, wondering whether
to laugh or not, the foreman drew another breath
and went on : “ And to you, Mr. Coroner, for your
able summing-up another word of thanks is due ;
for I must say had it not been for the lucid, con-
cise manner in .which the different points of the
evidence were given by you, sir, we would not
have been so well able to grasp the full strength of
the case.” And, bowing politely and with a
solemn face to all hands, Quealey left them in a
state of collapse. Outside court he confided in a
companion. “I couldn’t resist that borak,” he
said, “they were the thickest-headed lot of owls I
ever met.”
The name of a certain hugely-rich Scotch
squatter is a household word down South as a
synonym for canniness. There was a lot of good
in the eccentric old Mammonite, but one had to
bore for it. A lately-deceased pastoralist, F ,
was fond of recounting how, when there were
several lady visitors at his station and they were
just sitting down to dinner one sultry evening,
Mac was announced as wanting to be put-up
for the night. With many doubts as to his pro-
bable behavior, F had him shown in. Croesus
was clothed in a pair of tweeds, blue cotton shirt,
open at the throat, and calico coat, all strangers to
the washtub. The waitress laid the usual table-
accessories for him, and no sooner was this done
than he shook out the serviette, wiped his face,
the back of his neck, and as far down his breast-
bone as he could reach ; then he laid down the
serviette with a huge sigh of satisfaction, as of
one refreshed from a bath, and exclaimed, “ Eh
but thot’s gran’.” For breakfast the first time
writer put-up at Mac.’s homestead there were
three eggs cooked—an unusual extravagance, as
the old man sold his eggs, &c., in the adjoining
township. The scribe had a chop and an egg, and
the squatter an egg—which, though soft boiled,
he managed to eat with a knife. The host, fear-
ing that the remaining one might regale the
kitchen divinity, said, “ Eat this ither egg, ma
boy.” “No, thanks ; I’ve done very well.” Then
he held it out in his palsied hand, with the further
invitation : “ Pit it in yer pocket, lad, an’ eat it
alang the road.” The gilded ancient once came to
Sydney to buy a mob of cattle which were travel-
ling in from out-back. They were delayed through
floods, and, to save the expense of a six-weeks
sojourn in town, he took a billet as gardener at
15s. per week, only leaving that employment a
day or two before the cattle-sale. A short while
before he died he was in Melbourne, and, meeting
two brother-squatters, somehow or other invited
them to lunch —at Menzies’, if you please. When
they’d stoked, Mac. enquired, “ Hoo much do I
owe?” “ Sixteen-and-six, sir.” “ What’s that
you say, me marn? Saxteen-an’-sax ? ” After
much fumbling and counting, he anted-up that
sum, and said : “ Tak this to Muster Menzies,
and tell him it’s the first time I was ever in his
hoose, an’ it’ll be the laist. Ah don’t believe in
extoortionate ohairges.”
The struggle for a crust. A Sydney win-
dow-sign reads: “ Dressmaker and medical clair-
voyant.” Catch ’em on both the weak points !
The revelations re massacres of wounded
Arabs have evidently impelled General lvitchener
to order all newspaper correspondents to retire
from the Anglo-Egyptian army in the Soudan.
.But what nonsense is all this pretence about the
slaughter being the unauthorised work of the
savages assisting the white soldiers ! During the
fighting around Abuklea and Suakim in ’B4, Mel-
ton Prior sent pictures to the Graphic showing,
not the black allies but the real live Christian
lommy Atkinses spearing their disabled enemies
after the actual fighting had ceased. Some of
those pictures ought to be resuscitated in view of
the pharisaical endeavor to shift the charge of
inhumanity on to the shoulders of the nigger
assistants.

About months ago Sunday Brunker
was reported as telling a public meeting in his
electorate that a 27 per cent. Federal tariff meant
<£27 taken out of every £lOO of a man’s income.
And last_ week the dull old man was at length
prodded into making the usual explanation—that
he had been misreported :
I said if you have to pay your taxation through the
Customs House it dribbles away, and you know nothing
at all about it till you find your funds diminishing ; but
if any of you have a mortgage on your property, if you
borrow £lOO, and you are fools enough to pay 27 per
cent, as some people do—in fact some of them go so far
as to pay 60 per cent.—then that means £27 a year to
your mortgagee, and you have to dump it down every
half-year or every year.— Hansard report.
And by a weird coincidence a whole lot of news-
papers which were represented at Brunker’s meet-
ing reported him as saying the thing he says he
didn’t say, and not one, so far as The Bulletin
knows, reported him as saying the thing he says
he did say. And Brunker himself never explained
to any one of all these papers what a remarkable
mistake they had all made. The only probable
feature about the present yarn is that the thing
Brunker said he said is so utterly dull and useless
that it looks exactly like the thing Brunker would
say. Also, that wise old man might safely add
that if you pay Is. 7d. per lb. for butter, then
every time you buy lib. of butter you have to
dump down Is. 7d., and as you can’t pay the Is.
7d. and have it —why, then, you don’t have
the Is. 7d. any more because it is gone.

To the Wool Growers of New South Wales-
have pleasure in informing' you that, the dispute in
’ T the Wool Trade—which has for some time past
been under consideration by the Wool Buyers' Associa-
tion and the Co-Operative Wool Co.—has been satis-
factorily settled.
A conference at the instigation of the wool buyers was
held to-day between the Wool Selling Brokers’ Association
and the Co-Operative Company.
At this meeting the whole question of the selling
charges on wool in Sydney was fully discussed, and it
was mutually agreed that the following reduced scale of
charges be adopted, viz.:
Warehousing, Insurance in Store, etc. I/O per bale
Commission 3 per cent.
Cartage, Storage, Insurance, Re-
packing, etc., to the buyer. 1 j(i per bale
It was unanimously agreed that these charges are based
upon fair and equitable lines, taking into account the
absolute necessity of placing wools before buyers in a
manner to secure the highest poi-s’ble results for growers.
lIY. TRENCHARD,
Acting-Chairman Wool Selling Brokers’ Ass’n.
FRANCIS BACON,
Chairman Co-Operative Wool & Produce Co.
September 19, 1898.

“D.”: John M‘Arthur, first owner of
Camden Park, Camden, N.S.W., was granted as
much land as he could ride around in a day for
first importing Merinos. Said that he took as
much as he could see as well; at any rate, the
estate now comprises between 50,000 and 60,000
acres ; 60 families living on it. Family bnrying-
ground on estate ; big house built by convicts.
Some of the employes have never been to Sydney,
their fathers having lived and died on the place.
I was reared there myself—one of a family of ten,
father receiving .£1 a week, house, milch cow,
acre of ground and two rations. Colonel Onslow,
son of present owner (a lady) took N.S.W.
Mounted Rifles ’Ome at Jubilee time.
During the recent N.S.W. electoral cam-
paign two candidates with similar names —one
being Fitzpatrick and the other Fitzgerald—but
very divergent views, pervaded various remote and
mountainous districts in pursuit of the coy elector.
One of them came late in the evening in his buggy
to a small settlement which was utterly hostile to
him and his views—a fact the more remarkable
inasmuch as the electors had never beheld him.
His surprise was great, therefore, when he struck
the first house, at being received with much en-
thusiasm, and set down to plentiful bush fare,
while the “ boss ” scoured the country in the dark
for the free and independent. A good meeting
was assembled, and when he had finished tea, bitz
started to air his views in the dining-room. But
as he went on, the audience grew more and more
astonished and uneasy, till at last the host in-
terrupted, with horrified misgiving. “ Excuse me,
sir, but are you the Protectionist candidate?’
“No, sir, Pm a staunch Freetrader” The meeting
broke up in great disorder, and the wrong Fitz
polled exactly one vote there on election day
against the other’s sixteen.

THEY CALL IT A MIRACLE!
Doctors give him up!
Hospitals discharge him INCURABE!
A DARK AND GLOOMY PROSPECT!
A RAY OF SUNSHINE and a PERFECT RESTORATION.
(BY OUR SPECIAL REPORTER.)
What is locally
Considered a. Miracle
has lately been performed at Silver Water,
sometimes called “ Ibbettown,” so many
Ibbetts live there, a prettily-situated village
near Auburn, and about 10 miles from
Sydney, on the main suburban line.
News of the perfect restoration of health
to Mr. William lbbett having got abroid,
we dispatched one of our reporters to the
district to institute a searching investigation
into the case, and find out
The Absolute Truth
from Mr. lbbett and the neighbours, who
appear to have made I lie case one of general
interest in the neighbourhood for some time
past, for it has been a daily subject of
conversation amongst the many people
acquainted with the particulars.
The r< porter could not make the people
he questioned understand which member of
the lbbett family he wanted to see till he
Mentioned the Illness
of the man in question, at which the re-
quired house was at once pointed out as that
of the man the doctors
Could not cure, and Hospitals
turned out as Incurable,
but Clemen's Tonic had
Thoroughly Restored.
The reporter was fortunate enough tu
obtain the iu'erview he sought, when h
asked : —“Willyou do me the kindness, Mr
lbbett, to tell me how your complaint first
started, how it progressed, and how you
finally recovered ? ”
“I will,” answered Mr. lbbett. “If
commenced with seveie pains in the head,
and afterwards pains down the neck and
spine came on. d hen all at once I lost my
appetite. I could’nt look at my food, the
very sight of it turned me sick. Mj
digestion went all wrong. What little
I did eat (and I forced myself to eat some-
thing now and then to keep body and soul
together) gave me great pain. I only tool
soft food, but even this would not digest,
but
Turned Sour and Fermented
in my stomach, causing me to suffer terribh
from wind and flatulence. Besides, I could
get no sleep. I did not get, as a rule, more
than an hour’s sleep a night, and that in
disturbt d, uneasy snatches," each of short
duration, which did me no good at all. I
had to
Give up my Billet
at the Auburn Meat Works, and I used to
sit all day long in the chair feeling nerveless
and languid, with no energy or desire to
move. The pains in my back got worse
daily, especially along the spine. I got
most distressingly weak, and strangely
nervous. I have been accustomed to be in
the works with the whirl and crash of
machinery going on all round me, and I
never suffered the least inconvenience, but
now every sound went
Through me like a. Knife,
and would startle and give me such a shock
that I should almost fall to the ground, and
T trembled like a leaf.”
“ Why did’nt you see a doctor ?”
“ I did, and what use was it ? I had
several, and obeyed their orders, and took
their physic. To what end ? I only got
worse and worse. Finally they gave me up.
They said they could do nothing more for
me, and they had done nothing then ; and
one of them told me that he had given me
medicines strong enough to kill a horse.
This, I believe, was a fact, but it evidently
was not the treatment my disease required,
for it entirely failed to relieve me.
“ You must have got pretty low-spirited.”
“Of course. I became very much de-
pressed. Everything ahead
Looked Dark and Gloomy
to me. My friends tried in vain to cheer
me up, but nothing could give me hope, as
every day my disease increased. I got very
thin. I was like a skeleton. I fell away
in a very short time from 10 stone 4 lbs.
to 8 stone 5 lbs., and my face was pale and
colourless.
“ Did you suffer in any other way thal
you’ve not yet mentioned ?”
“ Yes ; my kidneys were affected, and a
dull, dragging pain in the small of the back
was continually distressing me (the doctor
said this was a sign of kidney disease), and
my water was very high coloured, but I
don’t care to go into further particulars as
to my kidney symptoms ; bui I can only
assure you they caused me great distress.
As a last resort, on my wife’s suggestion, I
was taken to the Hospital, Sydney. I
thought that if it was possible to cure me
at all I should have the best chance there.
The doctors gave me a very careful examin-
ation. They sounded me all over and took
my temperature, but they could make
nothing of my case. I believe that they
treated me there
For Kidney Disease.
But none of the treatment I received at the
Hospital relieved me, and the pain in ray
back whilst at the Hospital was agonising.
After being there some weeks I was at last
Discharged as Incurable
The doctors said they could do nothing for
me. My wife and friends were very much
grieved that I had to return home without
relief. As for myself, I fully thought I was
going home to die. I gave up ail hope of
ever getting on my feet again.”
“ What induced you to resort to Clements
Tonic ?”
It wms recommended to me by my cousin.
She s id she had heard of someone in whom,
so far as some of the symptoms went, had
been atllicted as I was and who had been
Entirely Restored to Health
by that remedy. She was so positive that
I got my wife to obtain some Clements Tonic
for me. I had, however, very little hope of
its being of any service, hut you know how
a drowning man
Catches at a Straw.
My wife bought a bottle and I commence 1
to take it regularly.”
“ How did it act in your case ?”
! ‘First, by relieving the awful pains in my
hack Then, as I continued to lake it, I felt
my nervous system gradually get a better
tone. I was not so fretful and irritable, and
I began to sleep much better. I was’soon
glad to find my digestion greatly improve.
Clements Tonic prevented my food going
sour and disagreeing with me, and I was
able to once mere eat solids freely and
without suffering afterwards, as I had been
accustomed to.”
“ And what about your kidney trouble
“ I found a most
Distinct Beneficial Etteo*.
upon my kidneys. It didn’t take long for
Clements Tonic to make those organs work
properly again, and to rid me of all disagree-
able symptoms which had formerly troubled
me.”
“ You, of course, continued Clements
Tonic?”
“Yes, you may be sure I didn’t miss a dose.
I Tom the time of starting I put all other
medicines aside Day by day I got
Stronger and Stronger.
i. was able to look hopefully to the future,
and my depression had vanished as though
it never had been. No longer was I dull
and heavy in the day time, but was bright
and lively Once I started to get better the
ate of my recovery was astonishing. I
Came on hand over hand,
so to speak. Clements Tonic gives you a
great appetite and no mistake, and I ate
splendidly and put on flesh again rapidly.”
“ Your relations and neighbours must
have been surprised at the way in which you
were restored to health ?”
“ I should just say they were—you just
ask them about me and they will tell you
all about it.
Ail my Troubles Vanished
under the influence of Clements Tonic, and
I was able to go to work again, though till
I was completely strong I always took a
bottle with me when I went to work, I
found it so invigorating.”
“ And your health now !”
“ I am now quite cured, and as strong
and well as ever in my life, for which I
thank Clements Tonic. If anyone wants to
know more about my case, they have only
to call on me and I shall be only too pleased
to tell them how Clements Tonic brought
me back to health and strength when the
doctors gave me up and
The Hospital turned me out
as an incurable, and I thought my days were
numbered.”
“ May we publish all you’ve said to me ?”
“ Yes ; you are quite welcome to do so.”

They Wait on the Wharf in

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Seems to me that honest, hard-working’ men seem to
accumulate the heaviesl'swags of trouble in this world."
—Steelman.
We were coming back from West Australia,
steerage (said Mitchell’s mate, known sometimes
as “{Sympathy Joe) —Mitchell, the Oracle, and
I. I had gone over saloon, with a few pounds in
my pocket. Mitchell said this was a great mis-
take-I should have gone over steerage with no-
thing but the clothes I stood upright in, and come
back saloon with a pile. He said that it was a
very common mistake that men made, but, as far
as his experience went, there always seemed to be
a deep-rooted popular prejudice in favor of going
away from home with a few pounds in ones
pocket and coming back stumped ; at least
amongst, rovers add vagabonds like ourselves—it
wasn't so generally popular or admired at home,
or in the places we came back to, as it was in the
places we went to. Anyway, it went; there wasn’t
the slightest doubt that our nearest and dearest
friends were, as a rule, in favor of our taking
away as little as we could possibly manage with,
and coin ing back with a pile, whether we came back
saloon or no ; and thac. ought to settle the matter
as far as any chap that had the slightest considera-
tion for his friends or family was concerned.
There was a good deal of misery, underneath,
coming home in that steerage. One man had had
his hand crushed and amputated out Coolgardie
way, and the stump had mortified, and he was
being sent to Melbourne by his mates. Some had
lost their money, some a couple of years of their
life, some their souls; but none seemed to have
lost the heart to call up the quiet grin that
southern rovers, vagabonds, travellers for “ graft ”
or fortune, and professional wanderers wear in
front of it all. Except one man—an elderly
eastern digger—he had lost his wife in Sydney
while he was away.
They sent him a wire to the Boulder Soak, or
somewhere out back of White Feather, to say
that his wife was seriously ill; but the wire went
wrong, somehow, after the manner of telegrams
not connected with mining, on the lines of “the
Western.” They sent him a wire to say that his
wife was dead, and that reached him all-right—
only a week late.
I can imagine it. He got the message at dinner-
time, or when they came back to the camp. His
mate wanted him to sit in the shade, or lie in the
tent, while he got the billy boiled. “You must
brace up and pull yourself together, Tom, for the
sake of the youngsters.” And Tom, for long in-
tervals walking up and down, up and down, by
the camp—under the brassy sky or the gloaming
—under the brilliant star-clusters that hung over
the desert plain, but never raising his eyes to them ;
kicking a tuft of grass or a hole in the sand now
and then, and seeming to watch the progress of the
track he is tramping out. The wife of twenty
years was with him—though two-thousand miles
away—till that message came—she died to-day—
just now—an hour agone.
Or Tom sitting with his mates round the billy,
and they talking in quiet, subdued tones about the
track, the departure of coaches, trains and boats—
arranging for Tom’s journey East, and the working
of the claim in his absence. Or Tom lying on his
back in his bunk, with his hands under his head
and his eyes fixed on the calico above—thinking,
thinking, thinking. Thinking, with a touch of
his boyhood’s faith perhaps, or wondering what he
had done in his long, hard-working married life,
that God should do this thing to him now, of all
times.
“You’d best take what money we have in the
camp, Tom ; you’ll want it all ag’in the time you
get back from Sydney, and we can fix it up arter-
wards . . . There’s a couple o’clean shirts o’mine
—you’d best take ’em—you’ll want ’em on the
voyage . . . You might as well take them there
new pants o’ mine, they'll only dry-rot out here—
and the coat, too, if you like —it’s too small for me,
anyway. You won’t have any time in Perth, and
you’ll want some decent togs to land with in
Sydney.”
* • * *
“I wouldn’t ’a’ cared so much if I’d ’a’ seen the
last of her,” he said, in a quiet, patient voice, to
us one night by the rail. “ I would ’a’liked to
have seen the last of her..’’
“Have you been long in the West?’’
“ Over two years. I made up to take a run
across last Xmas, and have a look at ’em. But I
couldn’t very well get away when ‘exemption-
time ’ come. I didn’t like to leave the claim.”
“ Do any good over there ? ”
“ Well, things brightened up a bit the last
month or two. 1 had a hard pull at first ; landed
without a penny, and had to send home every
shilling I could rake up to get tilings straightened
up a bit at home. Then the eldest boy fell ill, and
then the baby. I’d reckoned on bringing ’em over
to Perth or Coolgardie when the cool weather
came, and having them somewheres near me,
where I could go and have a look at ’em now and
then, and look after them.”
“ Going back to the West again ? ”
“ Oh, yes. I must go for the sake of the
youngsters. But I don’t seem to have much
heart in it.” He smoked awhile. “Over2oyears
we struggled along together—the missus and me—
and it seems hard that I couldn’t see the last of
her. It’s rough on a man.”
“ The world is damned rough on a man some-
times,” said Mitchell, “most especially when he
least deserves it.”
The digger crossed his arms on the rail like an
old “ cocky ” at the fence in the cool of the even-
ing, yarning with an old crony.
“ Mor’n 20 year she stuck to me and struggled
along by my side. She never give in. I’ll swear
she was on her feet till the last, with her sleeves
tucked up—bustlin’ round. . . And just when
things was brightening and I saw a chance of
giving her a bit of a rest and comfort for the end
of her life. . . I thought of it all only t’other
week when things was clearing up ahead ; and,
the last ‘order’ I sent over I set to work and
wrote her a long letter putting all the good news
and encouragement I could think of into it. I
thought how that letter would brighten up things
at home, and how she’d read it round. I thought
of lots of things that a man never gets time to
think of while his nose is kept to the grindstone.
And she was dead and in her grave and I never
knowed it.”
Mitchell dug his elbow into my ribs and made
signs for the matches to light his pipe.
“ An’ yer never knowed,” reflected the Oracle.
“But I always had an idea when there was
trouble at home,” the digger went on presently, in
a quiet, patient tone. “I always knowed; I
always had a kind of feeling that way —I felt it—
no matter how far I was away. When the young-
sters was sick I knowed it, and I expected the
letter that come. About a fortnight ago I had a
feeling that way when the wife was ill. The very
stars, out there on the desert by the Boulder Soak,
seemed to say : 4 There’s trouble at home. Go
home. There’s trouble at home.’ But I never
dreamed what that trouble was. One night I did
make up my mind to start in the morning, but
when the morning come I hadn’t an excuse, and
was ashamed to tell my mates the truth. They
might have thought I was going ratty, like a good
many go out there.” Then he broke otf with a
fort of laugh, as if it just struck him that we might
think he was a bit off his head, or that his talk was
getting uncomfortable for us. 14 Curious, ain’t
it ? ” he said.
“ Reminds me of a case I knowed,” commenced
the Oracle, after a pause.
I could have pitched him overboard, but that
was a mistake. He and the old digger sat on the
for’ard hatch half the night yarning, mostly about
queer starts, and rum go’s, and tfurious cases
that the Oracle had knowed, and I think the
Oracle did him a lot of good somehow, for he
seemed more cheerful in the morning.
We were overcrowded in the steerage, but Mit-
chell managed to give up his berth to the old
digger without letting him know it. Most of the
chaps seemed anxious, to make a place at the first
table and pass the first'helpings of the dishes to
the “old covo that had lost his missus.”
They all seemed to forget him as we entered the
Heads ; they had their own troubles to attend to.
They were in the shadow of the shame of coming
back hard-up, and the grins began to grow faint
and sickly. But I didn't forget him. I wished
sometimes that I didn’t take so much notice of
things. ■
There was no mistaking them —the little group
that stood apart near the end of the vvharf,
dressed in cheap black. There was the eldest
single sister —thin, pale, and haggard-looking—
that had had the hard worry in the family till her
temper was spoilt, as you could see by the peevish,
irritable lines in her face—that had to be the
mother of them all now, and had never known,
perhaps, what it was to be a girl or a sweetheart.
She gave a hard, mechanical sort of smile when
she saw her father, and then stood looking at the
boat in a vacant, hopeless sort of way. There
was the baby, that he saw now for the first time,
crowing and jumping at the sight of the boat
coming in ; there was the eldest boy, looking
awkward and out of place in his new slop-suit of
black, shifting round uneasily and looking any-
where but at his father. But the little girl was
the worst, and a pretty little girl she was, too :
she never took her streaming eyes off her father’s
face the whole time. You could see that her little
heart was bursting, and with pity for him. They
were too far apart to speak to each other as yet.
The boat seemed a cruel long time swinging along-
side—l wished they’d hurry up. He’d brought
his traps up early and laid ’em on the deck under
the rail; he stood very quiet with his hands be-
hind him, looking at his children. He had a
strong, square, workman’s face, but I could see
his chin and mouth quivering under the stubbly
iron-grey beard, and the lump working in his
throat, and one strong hand gripped the other
very tight behind, but his eyelids never quivered
—only his eyes seemed to grow more and more sad
and lonesome. These are the sort of long, cruel
moments when a man sits or stands very tight and
quiet and calm-looking, with his whole past life
going whirling through his brain, year after year,
and over and over again. Just as the digger
seemed about to speak to them he met the brim-
ming eyes of his little girl turned up to his face.
He looked at her for a moment, and then turned
suddenly and went below as if pretending to go
down for his things. I noticed that Mitchell—
who hadn’t seemed to be noticing anything in par-
ticular —followed him down. When they came on
deck again we were right alongside.
“’Elio, Nell!’ said the digger to the eldest
daughter.
“ ’Elio, father ! ” she said, with a sort of gasp,
but trying to smile.
“ ’Elio, Jack, how are you getting on ? ”
“ All right, father,” said the boy, brightening up
and seeming greatly relieved.
He looked down at the little girl with a
smile that I can’t describe, but didn’t speak to
her. She still stood with quivering chin and
mouth and great brimming eyes upturned, full of
such pity as I never saw before in a child-face —
pity for him.
“ You can get ashore now,” said Mitchell ;
“see, they’ve got the gangway out aft.”
Presently I saw Mitchell with the portmanteau
in his hand, and the baby on his arm, steering
them away to a quiet corner of the shed at the
top of the wharf. The digger had the little girl
in his arms, and both hers were round his neck,
and her face hidden on his shoulder.
When Mitchell came back, he leant on the rail
for a while by my side, as if it was a boundary
fence out back, and there Was no hurry to break
up camp and make a start.
“ What did you follow him below that time for,
Mitchell ? ” I asked presently, for want of some-
thing better to say.
Mitchell looked at me out of the corners of his
eyes.
“I wanted to score a drink!” he said. “I
thought he wanted one and wouldn’t like to be a
Johnnie Woodser! ”

“ Tite Barnacle ” : Vic. Public Service
Board, specially created to administer the
Public Service Act, and whose three members
draw something like =£4ooo a year among them,
has not had sufficient knowledge of the depart-
ments to recommend a new appointment or an
increase in salary for. the last five years, nor
courage enough to say such are not necessary.
Meantime, a special Reclassification Board has
been appointed, and is wending its way at three-
guineas-a-day through the Service. By the
time it gets to the last department there will be
another batch of anomalies in the first one. The
employes, meanwhile, are like the small boy who
thought he would never get to school, because by
the time he got a new pair of pants his boots were
gone, and when his boots came along his “ weskit
was tore.” The members of the Public Service
Board are all over 60. It takes the two Houses of
Parliament to shift a member, however; so they
always have a life-tenure. And, when they do
die, they only make room for another sexagenarian,
who steps into the vacancy and thus escapes
official execution. Ever since Templeton’s retire-
ment, about ten years *vgo, the Board has been the
haven of fossildom.

A WOMAN’S WORD.
STRONG PRAISE FROM A NEWCASTLE LADY.
CURED AFTER THIRTEEN YEARS’ CONTINUAL SUFFERING.
Mbs. Jane Colman, of Beauford-street, Hamilton, Newcastle, says she cannot speak too highly of
Bile Beans. “ For 13 years,” she says, “I was in a delicate state of health, my earliest symptoms
being violent retching and pains in the head ; these were shortly followed by a complete loss of appetite
and restless nights. I had several of the best doctors in Newcastle in attendance on me from time to
time, and tried all kinds of patent medicines, but I could get no real relief; in fact, it became a
common thing for me to have to get out of bed and sit up for an hour or two at a stretch. It
seemed, too, that what little I could eat I could not digest. Dreadful pains would now and again
come in my back and kidneys, and especially under. the shoulder-blade. The pain in the shoulder
seemed to stab me like a knife. To walk merely about the house was hard work for me, and I nearly
always felt sick. My eyes grew dull, everything had a blurred look, and I really felt ‘ like a log of
wood.’ The least exertion prostrated me, I could not stand for ten minutes without becoming
fatigued, my feet getting numb as if pins and needles were being stuck in them.
“Bile Beans for Biliousness were brought under my notice, and just as an experiment I took
some. After a few doses I found an improvement in my condition, so I continued taking them, and
I have steadily improved ever since. I’ve been taking the Beans about eight weeks now, and must
say I never felt better in 20 years. My friends are astounded at my cure, and all say I look remark-
ably well. As for my appetite, well, I’m always ready for my meals, and my daughters are sometimes
astonished at what I can eat. Yes,” concluded Mrs. Colman, in answer to a query, “ I firmly believe
that it was Bile Beans alone that cured me. I have taken nothing in the meanwhile, and they have
done me a wonderful amount of good in every conceivable way ; in fact, I am quite a new woman.”
Mrs. Colman, it may be mentioned, is well known in Hamilton, having been a resident there for
the last six years. She is now 57 years of age, and the mother of 11 children, most of them being
grown-up sons and daughters.

BILIOUSNESS.
CAUSE AND CURE.
Biliousness is an ailment caused by a surcharge of bile becoming stagnant in the stomach. It is
generally accompanied by indigestion and constipation, vomiting, and a yellow appearance of the
complexion. Dizziness and violent headaches are usual symptoms. The bile is the natural purgative
of the body, and is secreted by the liver; if it fails to flow in its usual channels, or to perform its
usual duties, the liver is responsible.
A more annoying complaint than Biliousness it is hard to find. It prohibits the sufferer from
doing work of any kind, holding a conversation, or even resting quietly ; not only this, but the nausea
is most disagreeable, and the taste and breath are exceedingly offensive. A remedy should be applied
at once that will act in an efficient manner, and the remedy to take is Bile Beans for Biliousness,
which are compounded expressly for ailments of this sort. They are so prepared that they act directly
on the liver, helping it to help itself, and enabling it to secret enough bile to flush the bowels
thoroughly, and thus clear oft all waste and offensive matter from the system. This remedy is well
known, and is conceded the most effective purgative medicine of the age. It does not afford mere
temporary relief, but an absolute and lasting cure. It sells on its merits alone.
♦
INDIGESTION.
Indigestion is an ailment which may develop into a most severe disease if not properly attended
to. Acute chronic indigestion is by no means a slight affair. Attacks of indigestion in a more or less
mild form are too common to dwell upon for any length of time. As its name implies, this disorder
is an insufficient action of the various digestive organs, which prevents the food being properly dis-
solved. In cases of this kind the effect is twofold : First, the waste part of the food is kept in the
stomach and becomes sour instead of being carried off ; and, secondly, the blood-making or useful part
of the food is kept from performing its offices. The causes of indigestion are many : Insufficient liver
action, over-eating, eating too fast, and partaking of rich and indigestible foods, etc., etc. Constant
worry or mental strain, or lack of regularity, may also cause an attack. The first thing necessary to
cure an indisposition of this sort is a thorough opening of the bowels and a complete clearing out of the
whole digestive organism. Then the liver should be stimulated to action, and regularity observed in
everything. Bile Beans will do the first two of these, the patient must attend to the third. Chronio
indigestion of course needs longer treatment than a temporary attack. It is well to bear in mind that
headaches, face pimples, listlessness, and a host of minor ailments, arise from indigestion, or some
other form of digestive disorder.

The Silent Wheelman.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
From limpid Life’s enshrouded source
To where it joins the sea,
A Silent Wheelman dogs our course
With fell persistency.
He’s near us where we watch in Space
Illusion’s pantomime ;
Or Fortune’s golden-beetles chase
Upon the wheels of Time.
He goes where heroes slay Desire
And only live to serve ;
Where statesmen side-slip in the mire
And “stiffened ” leaders swerve ;
Where quicksand Compromise retards
Reform’s audacious throng;
Where, punctured by Rebellion’s shards,
Fall tyres of Ancient Wrong.
Those who with high-gear principles
Climb quartzy steeps to Right,
As those who coast down Pleasure’s hills
On a brakeless rush to Night,
With occult sense behind them hear
His diamond bearings grate,
His tyres of Sleep, his bell of Fear,
His pedals that are Fate.
Your ripe-lipped queen of the country way,
Or you of the seaside track,
Exploit your empire while you may
With never a side-look back !
Relays of suitors, false and leal,
Escort you as you roam,
But the Suitor on the Silent Wheel
At last will take you home.
Where’er we tour, by sun or star,
Against or with the wind,
When spring horizons beckon far
Or wintry drizzles blind ;
O’er grand savannahs of the Free,
Or with the crowds who fare
On metalled roads of Destiny :
The Silent Wheelman’s there.
He “ pa' e now, the teams of Strife,
Now M ition’s wheel bids stand ;
He runs a ding-dong race with Life
To Matter’s hinterland.
There hangs beneath his handle-bar
An eerie cynosure;
Some say ’tis Hope’s eternal star,
And some a wrecker’s lure.
Y.
Danton.
The ouher day in Brisbane police-court the
issue of certain litigation depended on the date of
a receipt. It was sworn that said receipt was
signed in ’96. A sharp solicitor examined the
document, however, and detected a want of cor-
respondence between date and stamp. An ex-
pert having testified that the stamp was not
printed till the year ’97, the smart solicitor won
in a canter.
On Our Selection.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
One night after the threshing. Dad lying on the
sofa, thinking ; the rest of us sitting at the table.
Dad spoke to Joe.
“ How much,” he said, “ is 700 bushels of wheat
at six shillings ? ”
Joe, who was looked upon as the brainy one of
our family, took down his slate with a hint of
scholarly ostentation.
“ What did y’ say, Dad —700 bags?"
“ Bushels ! Bushels l ”
“Seven 'un—dered bush—els—of wheat —wheat
was it, Dad ? ”
“Yes, lohcatl ”
“ Wheat—at . , . At what, Dad ? ”
“ Six shillings a bushel.”
“ Six shil—lings—a ... A, Dad ? We’ve not
done any at a ; she’s on’y showed us per ! ”
“ Per bushel, then ! ”
_ “ Per bush—el. That’s 700 bushels of wheat at
six shillin’s per bushel. An’ y’ wants ter know,
Dad ?”
“ How much it’ll be, of course.”
“ In money, Dad, or—er ? ”
“Dammit, yes; money!" Dad raised his
voice.
For a while, Joe thought hard, then set to work
figuring and rubbing out, figuring and rubbing
out. The rest of us eyed him, envious of his
learning.
Joe finished the sum.
“Well?” from Dad.
Joe cleared his throat. We listened.
“Nine thousan’ poun’.”
Dave laughed loud. Dad said “ Pshaw ! ” and
turned his face to the wall. Joe looked at the
slate again.
“ Oh ! I see,” he said, “ I didn’t divide by 12
t* bring t’ pounds,” and laughed himself.
More figuring and rubbing out.
Finally Joe, in loud, decisive tones, announced :
“ Four thousand no ’undered an’ twenty poun’,
fourteen shillin’s an’ ”
“ Bah ! You blockhead ! ” Dad blurted out,
and jumped off the sofa and went to bed.
We all turned in.
• • • •
We were not in bed long when the dog barked
and a horse entered the yard. There was a clink
of girth-buckles ; a saddle thrown down ; then a
thump, as though with a lump of blue-metal, set
the dog yelping lustily. We lay listening till a
voice called out at the door—“ All in bed ?” Then
we knew it was Dan, and Dad and Dave sprang
out in their shirts to let him in. All of us
jumped up to see Dan. This time he had been
away a long while, and when the slush-lamp was
lit and fairly going, how we stared and wondered
at his altered looks I He had grown a long
whisker, and must have stood inches higher than
Dad. Dad was delighted. He put a fire on,
made tea, and he and Dan talked till near day-
break— Dad of the harvest, and the Government
dam that was promised, and the splendid grass
growing in the paddock ; Dan of the great dry
plains, and the shearing-sheds out back, and the
chaps he had met there. And he related in a way
that made Dad’s eyes glisten and Joe’s mouth
open, how, with a knocked-up wrist, he shore
beside Proctor and big Andy Purcell, at Well-
town, and rung the shed by half a sheep.
Dad ardently admired Dan.
* * • *
Dan was only going to stay a short while at
home, he said, then was off West again. Dad
tried to persuade him to change his mind ; he
would have him remain and help to work the
selection. But Dan only shook his head and
laughed.
Dan accompanied Dad to the plough every
morning, and walked cheerfully up and down the
furrows all day, talking to him. Sometimes he
took a turn at the plough, and Dad did the talk-
ing. Dad just loved Dan’s company.
A few days went by. Dan still accompanied
Dad to the plough ; but didn’t walk up and down
with him. He selected a shade close by, and
talked to Dad from there as he passed on his
rounds. Sometimes Dan used to forget to talk at
all—he would be asleep—and Dad would wonder
if he was unwell. Once he advised him to go up
to the house and have a good camp. Dan went.
He stretched himself on the sofa, and smoked and
spat on the floor and played the concertina—an
old one he won in a raffle.
Dan didn’t go near the plough any more. He
stayed inside every day, and drank the yeast, and
provided music for the women. Sometimes he
would leave the sofa, and go to the back-door and
look out, and watch Dad tearing up and down the
paddock after the plough ; then he’d yawn, and
wonder aloud what the diggins it was the old
man saw in a game like that on a hot day ; and
return to the sofa, tired. But every evening when
Dad knocked off and brought the horses to the
barn Dan went out and watched him unharnessing
them.
* * • m
A month passed. Dad wasn’t so fond of Dan
now, and Dan never talked of going away. One
day Anderson’s cows wandered into our yard and
surrounded the hay-stack. Dad saw them from
the paddock and cooeed, and shouted for those at
the house to drive them away. They didn’t hear
him. Dad left the plough and ran up and pelted
Anderson’s cows with stones and glass-bottles, and
pursued them with a pitchfork till, in a mad rush
to get out, half the brutes fell over the fence and
made havoc with the wire. Dad spent an hour
mending it; then went to the verandah and
savagely asked mother if she had lost her ears.
Mother said she hadn’t. “Then why the devil
couldn’t. y’ hear me singin’ out?” Mother
thought it must have been because Dan was play-
ing the concertina. “ Oh ! damn his concer-
tina ! ” Dad squealed, and kicked Joe’s little
kitten that was rubbing itself fondly against his
leg clean through the house.
* * * *
Dan found the selection pretty slow—so he told
mother—and thought he would knock about a bit.
He went to the store and bought a"supply of
ammunition, which he booked to Dad, and started
shooting. He stood at the door and put 20 bullets
into the barn ; then he shot two bears near the
stock-yard with 20 more bullets, and dragged both
bears down to the house and left, them at the
back-door. They stayed at the back-door until
they went very bad ; then Dad hooked himself to
them and dragged them down the gully.
* * * *
Somehow, Dad began to hate Dan ! He scarcely
ever spoke to him now, and at meal-times never
spoke to any of us. Dad was a hard man to
understand. We couldn’t understand him. “And
with Dan at home, too! ” Sal used to whine.
Sal verily idolised Dan. Hero-worship was strong
in Sal.
One night Dad came in for supper rather later
than usual. He’d had a hard day, and was done
up. To make matters worse, when he was taking
the collar off Captain the brute tramped heavily
on his toe, and took the nail off. Supper wasn’t
ready. The dining-room was engaged. Dan was
showing Sal how the Prince of Wales schottische
was danced in the huts Out Back. For music, Sal
was humming, and the two were flying about the
room. Dad stood at the door and looked on, with
blood in Ins eye.
“ Look here !” he thundered suddenly, inter-
rupting Dan—“ I’ve had enough of you!” (the
couple stopped, astonished, and sal cried. “ bad!'
But Dad was hot). “ Out of this ! ” (placing his
hand on Dan, and shoving him). “You’ve leafed
long enough on me ! Off y’ go, t’ th’ devil ! ”
Dan went over to Anderson’s, and Anderson
took him in and kept him a week. Then Dan
took Anderson in :he sold him for 40s. a horse
which had been dead two days, and went away
West, again.
STEEL RUDD.
Mr. Morey, P.M. at Maryborough (Q.), be-
lieving afterwards that he had wrongly adjudged a
case by dismissing it “ with costs,” sent his own
cheque for £2 7s. to complainant, the sum named
being the amount of the costs. Such P.M.’s are
really too good to live, let alone to feed sharks.
In the Omeo district (Vic.), miners are
very grateful for a little rabbit, but are taxed, in
the interests of boss-cockies and “sich,”to keep
’em out. A revolt is brewing. Recent council-
election was fought to some extent on the rabbit

During the recent N.S. Wales election the
proprietors of the Chinese Australian Herald
received this note from a Bathurst chinkie
Dear Cousins, —I got vote. I tink no good Barton. I
tink no good Reid. Blit I tink no no good good, very
bad Sydney Smith. I tink I vote for Suttor. lie always
smile and say “ Good day." What you tell me ?
Dear Bulletin.— Re the coming Queen
Victoria Hospital in Melbourne, an antagonistic
paragraphist observes: “ The work it does is
already provided for by the Women’s Hospital,
and were the money devoted to the new venture
applied to augment the income of the latter insti-
tution far more genuine charity would result.’’
Is the author of that paragraph a male doctor?
The Q.V. Hospital is an institution for the treat-
ment of female complaints by women practitioners
only. It offers relief to women of the more femi-
nine type, who shrink from the thought of discus-
sing their sexual diseases and troubles with men,
and are, furthermore, of opinion that a pettieoated
expert is better qualified to deal with the case.
The Women’s Hospital, on the other hand, is con-
trolled by a clique of female Tories who have thus-
far refused to include women - doctors in the
medical staff. Its management is a stubborn pro-
test against the principle whereon the younger
hospital is based. “ All modesty abandon ye who
enter here ” is practically inscribed on its portals.
Yet your paragraphist has the impudence to state
that it works on the same lines as the hospital
doctored by Women for Women. — Mary’s Young
Man.

clients, in town or out of town, and give more for their

spot for everything we buy, we buy cheapest, conse-
quently sell cheapest ; and, being always on the spot,
we have first pick of the newest European goods. Now
what do you require ? An answer on a penny postcard
will do nicely. Hordern Brothers, 203, 205, 207, 209, 211
Pitt-street (only), Sydney.

“In 1890 I had a severe attack of typhoid fever, and for nearly five
years after the fever left me was a confirmed invalid. I had no appetite;
became pale and emaciated, and was unable to work. I tried various
remedies but found no relief until I began taking AYER’S SARSAPA-
RILLA. This medicine greatly improved my appetite, restored my liver
and stomach to a healthy condition, and now my food digests perfectly
and I am better than ever I was in my life. For this I am deeply grate-
ful, and take pleasure in making known to others the value of your Sar-
saparilla.”
The wonderful success of the great blood purifier
Sarsaparilla
has led to numerous imitations. The name—Ayer’s Sarsaparilla— is
prominent on the wrapper and is blown in the glass of each bottle. Re-
fuse all substitutes.

Alf EXTRACT.
“The first night I had slept a little from sheer exhaustion. The
next was terrible. I vomited incessantly; I was chilled to the bone, and
could not warm myself. I suffered horrible pain in the head, and through-
out my whole body. Next day, of course, I was a wreck. They told me I
had been delirious half the night I remember nothing of the day; but
next night I got a couple of hours’ sleep, and was rather better in conse-
quence. But vomiting went on steadily, and the fourth night I was again
delirious. The next day I was as bad as ever; but, thanks to a good
dose of Beecham’s Pills, i had an excellent
night, and I truly believe that they saved my life. I knew well where I
picked up this fever—-in the marshes of Mwenzo, and near Kituta—and the
killing thing was being unable to stop to cure it; the ten days on the lake
especially—often without food for twenty-four hours—nearly finished me.
I felt certain at the time that I was done for; and I very nearly was.” See
“ Three Years in Savage Africa” by Lionel Bede—Methuen & Go., London.
Mr. Lionel Decle covered a distance of over 7000 miles at one
stretch, between Capetown on the South and Mombassa on the East coast of
Africa. It is worth noting that this ** unique journey ” would probably
have been cut short; and, to quote the introduction by Mr. H. M. Stanley,
“ it* vivid record ” would never have been penned had not Mr.
Lionel Decle provided himself with a supply of
